10135{_}468{_}There was once a certain, or rather uncertain, bard, ycleped Robert Burns, who made a number of good songs; but this that the Laird sang was an amorous song of great antiquity, which, like all the said bard's best songs, was sung one hundred and fifty years before he was born.
10135{_}469{_}It began thus:    "I am the Laird of Windy-wa's,   I cam nae here without a cause,   An' I hae gotten forty fa's     In coming o'er the knowe, joe.
10135{_}470{_}The night it is baith wind and weet;   The morn it will be snaw and sleet;   My shoon are frozen to my feet;     O, rise an' let me in, joe!
10135{_}471{_}Let me in this ae night," etc.
10135{_}472{_}This song was the Laird singing, while, at the same time, he was smudging and laughing at the catastrophe, when, ere ever aware, he beheld, a short way before him, an uncommonly elegant and beautiful girl walking in the same direction with him.
10135{_}473{_}"Aye," said the Laird to himself, "here is something very attractive indeed!
10135{_}474{_}Where the deuce can she have sprung from?
10135{_}475{_}She must have risen out of the earth, for I never saw her till this breath.
10135{_}476{_}Well, I declare I have not seen such a female figure--I wish I had such an assignation with her as the Laird of Windy-wa's had with his sweetheart."
10135{_}477{_}As the Laird was half-thinking, half-speaking this to himself, the enchanting creature looked back at him with a motion of intelligence that she knew what he was half-saying, half-thinking, and then vanished over the summit of the rising ground before him, called the Birky Brow.
10135{_}478{_}"Aye, go your ways!"
10135{_}479{_}said the Laird; "I see by you, you'll not be very hard to overtake.
10135{_}480{_}You cannot get off the road, and I'll have a chat with you before you make the Deer's Den."
10135{_}481{_}The Laird jogged on.
10135{_}482{_}He did not sing the _Laird of Windy-wa's_ any more, for he felt a stifling about his heart; but he often repeated to himself, "She's a very fine woman!--a very fine woman indeed!--and to be walking here by herself!
10135{_}483{_}I cannot comprehend it."
10135{_}484{_}When he reached the summit of the Birky Brow he did not see her, although he had a longer view of the road than before.
10135{_}485{_}He thought this very singular, and began to suspect that she wanted to escape him, although apparently rather lingering on him before.
10135{_}486{_}"I shall have another look at her, however," thought the Laird, and off he set at a flying trot.
10135{_}487{_}No.
10135{_}488{_}He came first to one turn, then another.
10135{_}489{_}There was nothing of the young lady to be seen.
10135{_}490{_}"Unless she take wings and fly away, I shall be up with her," quoth the Laird, and off he set at the full gallop.
10135{_}491{_}In the middle of his career he met with Mr. McMurdie, of Aulton, who hailed him with, "Hilloa, Birkendelly!
10135{_}492{_}Where the deuce are you flying at that rate?"
10135{_}493{_}"I was riding after a woman," said the Laird, with great simplicity, reining in his steed.
10135{_}494{_}"Then I am sure no woman on earth can long escape you, unless she be in an air balloon."
10135{_}495{_}"I don't know that.
10135{_}496{_}Is she far gone?"
10135{_}497{_}"In which way do you mean?"
10135{_}498{_}"In this."
10135{_}499{_}"Aha-ha-ha!
10135{_}500{_}Hee-hee-hee!"
10135{_}501{_}nichered McMurdie, misconstruing the Laird's meaning.
10135{_}502{_}"What do you laugh at, my dear sir?
10135{_}503{_}Do you know her, then?"
10135{_}504{_}"Ho-ho-ho!
10135{_}505{_}Hee-hee-hee!
10135{_}506{_}How should I, or how can I, know her, Birkendelly, unless you inform me who she is?"
10135{_}507{_}"Why, that is the very thing I want to know of you.
10135{_}508{_}I mean the young lady whom you met just now."
10135{_}509{_}"You are raving, Birkendelly.
10135{_}510{_}I met no young lady, nor is there a single person on the road I have come by, while you know that for a mile and a half forward your way she could not get out of it."
10135{_}511{_}"I know that," said the Laird, biting his lip and looking greatly puzzled; "but confound me if I understand this; for I was within speech of her just now on the top of the Birky Brow there, and, when I think of it, she could not have been even thus far as yet.
10135{_}512{_}She had on a pure white gauze frock, a small green bonnet and feathers, and a green veil, which, flung back over her left shoulder, hung below her waist, and was altogether such an engaging figure that no man could have passed her on the road without taking some note of her.
10135{_}513{_}Are you not making game of me?
10135{_}514{_}Did you not really meet with her?"
10135{_}515{_}"On my word of truth and honor, I did not.
10135{_}516{_}Come, ride back with me, and we shall meet her still, depend on it.
10135{_}517{_}She has given you the go-by on the road.
10135{_}518{_}Let us go; I am only to call at the mill about some barley for the distillery, and will return with you to the big town."
10135{_}519{_}Birkendelly returned with his friend.
10135{_}520{_}The sun was not yet set, yet M'Murdie could not help observing that the Laird looked thoughtful and confused, and not a word could he speak about anything save this lovely apparition with the white frock and the green veil; and lo!
10135{_}521{_}when they reached the top of Birky Brow there was the maiden again before them, and exactly at the same spot where the Laird first saw her before, only walking in the contrary direction.
10135{_}522{_}"Well, this is the most extraordinary thing that I ever knew!"
10135{_}523{_}exclaimed the Laird.
10135{_}524{_}"What is it, sir?"
10135{_}525{_}said M'Murdie.
10135{_}526{_}"How that young lady could have eluded me," returned the Laird.
10135{_}527{_}"See, here she is still!"
10135{_}528{_}"I beg your pardon, sir, I don't see her.
10135{_}529{_}Where is she?"
10135{_}530{_}"There, on the other side of the angle; but you are shortsighted.
10135{_}531{_}See, there she is ascending the other eminence in her white frock and green veil, as I told you.
10135{_}532{_}What a lovely creature!"
10135{_}533{_}"Well, well, we have her fairly before us now, and shall see what she is like at all events," said McMurdie.
10135{_}534{_}Between the Birky Brow and this other slight eminence there is an obtuse angle of the road at the part where it is lowest, and, in passing this, the two friends necessarily lost sight of the object of their curiosity.
10135{_}535{_}They pushed on at a quick pace, cleared the low angle--the maiden was not there!
10135{_}536{_}They rode full speed to the top of the eminence from whence a long extent of road was visible before them--there was no human creature in view.
10135{_}537{_}McMurdie laughed aloud, but the Laird turned pale as death and bit his lip.
10135{_}538{_}His friend asked him good-humoredly why he was so much affected.
10135{_}539{_}He said, because he could not comprehend the meaning of this singular apparition or illusion, and it troubled him the more as he now remembered a dream of the same nature which he had, and which terminated in a dreadful manner.
10135{_}540{_}"Why, man, you are dreaming still," said McMurdie.
10135{_}541{_}"But never mind; it is quite common for men of your complexion to dream of beautiful maidens with white frocks, and green veils, bonnets, feathers, and slender waists.
10135{_}542{_}It is a lovely image, the creation of your own sanguine imagination, and you may worship it without any blame.
10135{_}543{_}Were her shoes black or green?
10135{_}544{_}And her stockings--did you note them?
10135{_}545{_}The symmetry of the limbs, I am sure you did!
10135{_}546{_}Good-bye; I see you are not disposed to leave the spot.
10135{_}547{_}Perhaps she will appear to you again."
10135{_}548{_}So saying, McMurdie rode on toward the mill, and Birkendelly, after musing for some time, turned his beast's head slowly round, and began to move toward the great muckle village.
10135{_}549{_}The Laird's feelings were now in terrible commotion.
10135{_}550{_}He was taken beyond measure with the beauty and elegance of the figure he had seen, but he remembered, with a mixture of admiration and horror, that a dream of the same enchanting object had haunted his slumbers all the days of his life; yet, how singular that he should never have recollected the circumstance till now!
10135{_}551{_}But farther, with the dream there were connected some painful circumstances which, though terrible in their issue, he could not recollect so as to form them into any degree of arrangement.
10135{_}552{_}As he was considering deeply of these things and riding slowly down the declivity, neither dancing his cane nor singing the _Laird of Windy-wa's_, he lifted up his eyes, and there was the girl on the same spot where he saw her first, walking deliberately up the Birky Brow.
10135{_}553{_}The sun was down, but it was the month of August and a fine evening, and the Laird, seized with an unconquerable desire to see and speak with that incomparable creature, could restrain himself no longer, but shouted out to her to stop till he came up.
10135{_}554{_}She beckoned acquiescence, and slackened her pace into a slow movement.
10135{_}555{_}The Laird turned the corner quickly, but when he had rounded it the maiden was still there, though on the summit of the brow.
10135{_}556{_}She turned round, and, with an ineffable smile and curtsy, saluted him, and again moved slowly on.
10135{_}557{_}She vanished gradually beyond the summit, and while the green feathers were still nodding in view, and so nigh that the Laird could have touched them with a fishing-rod, he reached the top of the brow himself.
10135{_}558{_}There was no living soul there, nor onward, as far as his view reached.
10135{_}559{_}He now trembled in every limb, and, without knowing what he did, rode straight on to the big town, not daring well to return and see what he had seen for three several times; and certain he would see it again when the shades of evening were deepening, he deemed it proper and prudent to decline the pursuit of such a phantom any farther.
10135{_}560{_}He alighted at the Queen's Head, called for some brandy and water, quite forgot what was his errand to the great muckle town that afternoon, there being nothing visible to his mental sight but lovely images, with white gauze frocks and green veils.
10135{_}561{_}His friend M'Murdie joined him; they drank deep, bantered, reasoned, got angry, reasoned themselves calm again, and still all would not do.
10135{_}562{_}The Laird was conscious that he had seen the beautiful apparition, and, moreover, that she was the very maiden, or the resemblance of her, who, in the irrevocable decrees of Providence, was destined to be his.
10135{_}563{_}It was in vain that M'Murdie reasoned of impressions on the imagination, and    "Of fancy moulding in the mind,   Light visions on the passing wind."
10135{_}564{_}Vain also was a story that he told him of a relation of his own, who was greatly harassed by the apparition of an officer in a red uniform that haunted him day and night, and had very nigh put him quite distracted several times, till at length his physician found out the nature of this illusion so well that he knew, from the state of his pulse, to an hour when the ghost of the officer would appear, and by bleeding, low diet, and emollients contrived to keep the apparition away altogether.
10135{_}565{_}The Laird admitted the singularity of this incident, but not that it was one in point; for the one, he said, was imaginary, the other real, and that no conclusions could convince him in opposition to the authority of his own senses.
10135{_}566{_}He accepted of an invitation to spend a few days with M'Murdie and his family, but they all acknowledged afterward that the Laird was very much like one bewitched.
10135{_}567{_}As soon as he reached home he went straight to the Birky Brow, certain of seeing once more the angelic phantom, but she was not there.
10135{_}568{_}He took each of his former positions again and again, but the desired vision would in no wise make its appearance.
10135{_}569{_}He tried every day and every hour of the day, all with the same effect, till he grew absolutely desperate, and had the audacity to kneel on the spot and entreat of Heaven to see her.
10135{_}570{_}Yes, he called on Heaven to see her once more, whatever she was, whether a being of earth, heaven, or hell.
10135{_}571{_}He was now in such a state of excitement that he could not exist; he grew listless, impatient, and sickly, took to his bed, and sent for M'Murdie and the doctor; and the issue of the consultation was that Birkendelly consented to leave the country for a season, on a visit to his only sister in Ireland, whither we must accompany him for a short space.
10135{_}572{_}His sister was married to Captain Bryan, younger, of Scoresby, and they two lived in a cottage on the estate, and the Captain's parents and sisters at Scoresby Hall.
10135{_}573{_}Great was the stir and preparation when the gallant young Laird of Birkendelly arrived at the cottage, it never being doubted that he came to forward a second bond of connection with the family, which still contained seven dashing sisters, all unmarried, and all alike willing to change that solitary and helpless state for the envied one of matrimony--a state highly popular among the young women of Ireland.
10135{_}574{_}Some of the Misses Bryan had now reached the years of womanhood, several of them scarcely, but these small disqualifications made no difference in the estimation of the young ladies themselves; each and all of them brushed up for the competition with high hopes and unflinching resolutions.
10135{_}575{_}True, the elder ones tried to check the younger in their good-natured, forthright Irish way; but they retorted, and persisted in their superior pretensions.
10135{_}576{_}Then there was such shopping in the county town!
10135{_}577{_}It was so boundless that the credit of the Hall was finally exhausted, and the old Squire was driven to remark that "Och, and to be sure it was a dreadful and tirrabell concussion, to be put upon the equipment of seven daughters all at the same moment, as if the young gentleman could marry them all!
10135{_}578{_}Och, then, poor dear shoul, he would be after finding that one was sufficient, if not one too many.
10135{_}579{_}And therefore there was no occasion, none at all, at all, and that there was not, for any of them to rig out more than one."
10135{_}580{_}It was hinted that the Laird had some reason for complaint at this time, but as the lady sided with her daughters, he had no chance.
10135{_}581{_}One of the items of his account was thirty-seven buckling-combs, then greatly in vogue.
10135{_}582{_}There were black combs, pale combs, yellow combs, and gilt ones, all to suit or set off various complexions; and if other articles bore any proportion at all to these, it had been better for the Laird and all his family that Birkendelly had never set foot in Ireland.
10135{_}583{_}The plan was all concocted.
10135{_}584{_}There was to be a grand dinner at the Hall, at which the damsels were to appear in all their finery.
10135{_}585{_}A ball to follow, and note be taken which of the young ladies was their guest's choice, and measures taken accordingly.
10135{_}586{_}The dinner and the ball took place; and what a pity I may not describe that entertainment, the dresses, and the dancers, for they were all exquisite in their way, and _outre_ beyond measure.
10135{_}587{_}But such details only serve to derange a winter evening's tale such as this.
10135{_}588{_}Birkendelly having at this time but one model for his choice among womankind, all that ever he did while in the presence of ladies was to look out for some resemblance to her, the angel of his fancy; and it so happened that in one of old Bryan's daughters named Luna, or, more familiarly, Loony, he perceived, or thought he perceived, some imaginary similarity in form and air to the lovely apparition.
10135{_}589{_}This was the sole reason why he was incapable of taking his eyes off from her the whole of that night; and this incident settled the point, not only with the old people, but even the young ladies were forced, after every exertion on their own parts, to "yild the p'int to their sister Loony, who certainly was not the mist genteelest nor mist handsomest of that guid-lucking fimily."
10135{_}590{_}The next day Lady Luna was dispatched off to the cottage in grand style, there to live hand in glove with her supposed lover.
10135{_}591{_}There was no standing all this.
10135{_}592{_}There were the two parrocked together, like a ewe and a lamb, early and late; and though the Laird really appeared to have, and probably had, some delight in her company, it was only in contemplating that certain indefinable air of resemblance which she bore to the sole image impressed on his heart.
10135{_}593{_}He bought her a white gauze frock, a green bonnet and feather, with a veil, which she was obliged to wear thrown over her left shoulder, and every day after, six times a day, was she obliged to walk over a certain eminence at a certain distance before her lover.
10135{_}594{_}She was delighted to oblige him; but still, when he came up, he looked disappointed, and never said, "Luna, I love you; when are we to be married?"
10135{_}595{_}No, he never said any such thing, for all her looks and expressions of fondest love; for, alas!
10135{_}596{_}in all this dalliance he was only feeding a mysterious flame that preyed upon his vitals, and proved too severe for the powers either of reason or religion to extinguish.
10135{_}597{_}Still, time flew lighter and lighter by, his health was restored, the bloom of his cheek returned, and the frank and simple confidence of Luna had a certain charm with it that reconciled him to his sister's Irish economy.
10135{_}598{_}But a strange incident now happened to him which deranged all his immediate plans.
10135{_}599{_}He was returning from angling one evening, a little before sunset, when he saw Lady Luna awaiting him on his way home.
10135{_}600{_}But instead of brushing up to meet him as usual, she turned, and walked up the rising ground before him.
10135{_}601{_}"Poor sweet girl!
10135{_}602{_}how condescending she is," said he to himself, "and how like she is in reality to the angelic being whose form and features are so deeply impressed on my heart!
10135{_}603{_}I now see it is no fond or fancied resemblance.
10135{_}604{_}It is real!
10135{_}605{_}real!
10135{_}606{_}real!
10135{_}607{_}How I long to clasp her in my arms, and tell her how I love her; for, after all, that is the girl that is to be mine, and the former a vision to impress this the more on my heart."
10135{_}608{_}He posted up the ascent to overtake her.
10135{_}609{_}When at the top she turned, smiled, and curtsied.
10135{_}610{_}Good heavens!
10135{_}611{_}it was the identical lady of his fondest adoration herself, but lovelier, far lovelier, than ever.
10135{_}612{_}He expected every moment that she would vanish, as was her wont; but she did not--she awaited him, and received his embraces with open arms.
10135{_}613{_}She was a being of real flesh and blood, courteous, elegant, and affectionate.
10135{_}614{_}He kissed her hand, he kissed her glowing cheek, and blessed all the powers of love who had thus restored her to him again, after undergoing pangs of love such as man never suffered.
10135{_}615{_}"But, dearest heart, here we are standing in the middle of the highway," said he; "suffer me to conduct you to my sister's house, where you shall have an apartment with a child of nature having some slight resemblance to yourself."
10135{_}616{_}She smiled, and said, "No, I will not sleep with Lady Luna to-night.
10135{_}617{_}Will you please to look round you, and see where you are."
10135{_}618{_}He did so, and behold they were standing on the Birky Brow, on the only spot where he had ever seen her.
10135{_}619{_}She smiled at his embarrassed look, and asked if he did not remember aught of his coming over from Ireland.
10135{_}620{_}He said he thought he did remember something of it, but love with him had long absorbed every other sense.
10135{_}621{_}He then asked her to his own house, which she declined, saying she could only meet him on that spot till after their marriage, which could not be before St. Lawrence's Eve come three years.
10135{_}622{_}"And now," said she, "we must part.
10135{_}623{_}My name is Jane Ogilvie, and you were betrothed to me before you were born.
10135{_}624{_}But I am come to release you this evening, if you have the slightest objection."
10135{_}625{_}He declared he had none; and kneeling, swore the most solemn oath to be hers forever, and to meet her there on St. Lawrence's Eve next, and every St. Lawrence's Eve until that blessed day on which she had consented to make him happy by becoming his own forever.
10135{_}626{_}She then asked him affectionately to change rings with her, in pledge of their faith and troth, in which he joyfully acquiesced; for she could not have then asked any conditions which, in the fulness of his heart's love, he would not have granted; and after one fond and affectionate kiss, and repeating all their engagements over again, they parted.
10135{_}627{_}Birkendelly's heart was now melted within him, and all his senses overpowered by one overwhelming passion.
10135{_}628{_}On leaving his fair and kind one, he got bewildered, and could not find the road to his own house, believing sometimes that he was going there, and sometimes to his sister's, till at length he came, as he thought, upon the Liffey, at its junction with Loch Allan; and there, in attempting to call for a boat, he awoke from a profound sleep, and found himself lying in his bed within his sister's house, and the day sky just breaking.
10135{_}629{_}If he was puzzled to account for some things in the course of his dream, he was much more puzzled to account for them now that he was wide awake.
10135{_}630{_}He was sensible that he had met his love, had embraced, kissed, and exchanged vows and rings with her, and, in token of the truth and reality of all these, her emerald ring was on his finger, and his own away; so there was no doubt that they had met--by what means it was beyond the power of man to calculate.
10135{_}631{_}There was then living with Mrs. Bryan an old Scotswoman, commonly styled Lucky Black.
10135{_}632{_}She had nursed Birkendelly's mother, and been dry-nurse to himself and sister; and having more than a mother's attachment for the latter, when she was married, old Lucky left her country to spend the last of her days in the house of her beloved young lady.
10135{_}633{_}When the Laird entered the breakfast-parlor that morning she was sitting in her black velvet hood, as usual, reading _The Fourfold State of Man_, and, being paralytic and somewhat deaf, she seldom regarded those who went or came.
10135{_}634{_}But chancing to hear him say something about the 9th of August, she quitted reading, turned round her head to listen, and then asked, in a hoarse, tremulous voice: "What's that he's saying?
10135{_}635{_}What's the unlucky callant saying about the 9th of August?
10135{_}636{_}Aih?
10135{_}637{_}To be sure it is St. Lawrence's Eve, although the 10th be his day.
10135{_}638{_}It's ower true, ower true, ower true for him an' a' his kin, poor man!
10135{_}639{_}Aih?
10135{_}640{_}What was he saying then?"
10135{_}641{_}The men smiled at her incoherent earnestness, but the lady, with true feminine condescension, informed her, in a loud voice, that Allan had an engagement in Scotland on St. Lawrence's Eve.
10135{_}642{_}She then started up, extended her shrivelled hands, that shook like the aspen, and panted out: "Aih, aih?
10135{_}643{_}Lord preserve us!
10135{_}644{_}Whaten an engagement has he on St. Lawrence's Eve?
10135{_}645{_}Bind him!
10135{_}646{_}bind him!
10135{_}647{_}Shackle him wi' bands of steel, and of brass, and of iron!
10135{_}648{_}O may He whose blessed will was pleased to leave him an orphan sae soon, preserve him from the fate which I tremble to think on!"
10135{_}649{_}She then tottered round the table, as with supernatural energy, and seizing the Laird's right hand, she drew it close to her unstable eyes, and then perceiving the emerald ring chased in blood, she threw up her arms with a jerk, opened her skinny jaws with a fearful gape, and uttering a shriek that made all the house yell, and every one within it to tremble, she fell back lifeless and rigid on the floor.
10135{_}650{_}The gentlemen both fled, out of sheer terror; but a woman never deserts her friends in extremity.
10135{_}651{_}The lady called her maids about her, had her old nurse conveyed to bed, where every means were used to restore animation.
10135{_}652{_}But, alas, life was extinct!
10135{_}653{_}The vital spark had fled forever, which filled all their hearts with grief, disappointment, and horror, as some dreadful tale of mystery was now sealed up from their knowledge which, in all likelihood, no other could reveal.
10135{_}654{_}But to say the truth, the Laird did not seem greatly disposed to probe it to the bottom.
10135{_}655{_}Not all the arguments of Captain Bryan and his lady, nor the simple entreaties of Lady Luna, could induce Birkendelly to put off his engagement to meet his love on the Birky Brow on the evening of the 9th of August; but he promised soon to return, pretending that some business of the utmost importance called him away.
10135{_}656{_}Before he went, however, he asked his sister if ever she had heard of such a lady in Scotland as Jane Ogilvie.
10135{_}657{_}Mrs. Bryan repeated the name many times to herself, and said that name undoubtedly was once familiar to her, although she thought not for good, but at that moment she did not recollect one single individual of the name.
10135{_}658{_}He then showed her the emerald ring that had been the death of Lucky Black; but the moment the lady looked at it, she made a grasp at it to take it off by force, which she had very nearly effected.
10135{_}659{_}"Oh, burn it!
10135{_}660{_}burn it!"
10135{_}661{_}cried she; "it is not a right ring!
10135{_}662{_}Burn it!"
10135{_}663{_}"My dear sister, what fault is in the ring?"
10135{_}664{_}said he.
10135{_}665{_}"It is a very pretty ring, and one that I set great value by."
10135{_}666{_}"Oh, for Heaven's sake, burn it, and renounce the giver!"
10135{_}667{_}cried she.
10135{_}668{_}"If you have any regard for your peace here or your soul's welfare hereafter, burn that ring!
10135{_}669{_}If you saw with your own eyes, you would easily perceive that that is not a ring befitting a Christian to wear."
10135{_}670{_}This speech confounded Birkendelly a good deal.
10135{_}671{_}He retired by himself and examined the ring, and could see nothing in it unbecoming a Christian to wear.
10135{_}672{_}It was a chased gold ring, with a bright emerald, which last had a red foil, in some lights giving it a purple gleam, and inside was engraven "_Elegit_," much defaced, but that his sister could not see; therefore he could not comprehend her vehement injunctions concerning it.
10135{_}673{_}But that it might no more give her offence, or any other, he sewed it within his vest, opposite his heart, judging that there was something in it which his eyes were withholden from discerning.
10135{_}674{_}Thus he left Ireland with his mind in great confusion, groping his way, as it were, in a hole of mystery, yet with the passion that preyed on his heart and vitals more intense than ever.
10135{_}675{_}He seems to have had an impression all his life that some mysterious fate awaited him, which the correspondence of his dreams and day visions tended to confirm.
10135{_}676{_}And though he gave himself wholly up to the sway of one overpowering passion, it was not without some yearnings of soul, manifestations of terror, and so much earthly shame, that he never more mentioned his love, or his engagements, to any human being, not even to his friend M'Murdie, whose company he forthwith shunned.
10135{_}677{_}It is on this account that I am unable to relate what passed between the lovers thenceforward.
10135{_}678{_}It is certain they met at the Birky Brow that St. Lawrence's Eve, for they were seen in company together; but of the engagements, vows, or dalliance that passed between them I can say nothing; nor of all their future meetings, until the beginning of August, 1781, when the Laird began decidedly to make preparations for his approaching marriage; yet not as if he and his betrothed had been to reside at Birkendelly, all his provisions rather bespeaking a meditated journey.
10135{_}679{_}On the morning of the 9th he wrote to his sister, and then arraying himself in his new wedding suit, and putting the emerald ring on his finger, he appeared all impatience, until toward evening, when he sallied out on horseback to his appointment.
10135{_}680{_}It seems that his mysterious inamorata had met him, for he was seen riding through the big town before sunset, with a young lady behind him, dressed in white and green, and the villagers affirmed that they were riding at the rate of fifty miles an hour!
10135{_}681{_}They were seen to pass a cottage called Mosskilt, ten miles farther on, where there was no highway, at the same tremendous speed; and I could never hear that they were any more seen, until the following morning, when Birkendelly's fine bay horse was found lying dead at his own stable door; and shortly after his master was likewise discovered lying, a blackened corpse, on the Birky Brow at the very spot where the mysterious but lovely dame had always appeared to him.
10135{_}682{_}There was neither wound, bruise, nor dislocation in his whole frame; but his skin was of a livid color, and his features terribly distorted.
10135{_}683{_}This woful catastrophe struck the neighborhood with great consternation, so that nothing else was talked of.
10135{_}684{_}Every ancient tradition and modern incident were raked together, compared, and combined; and certainly a most rare concatenation of misfortunes was elicited.
10135{_}685{_}It was authenticated that his father had died on the same spot that day twenty years, and his grandfather that day forty years, the former, as was supposed, by a fall from his horse when in liquor, and the latter, nobody knew how; and now this Allan was the last of his race, for Mrs. Bryan had no children.
10135{_}686{_}It was, moreover, now remembered by many, and among the rest by the Rev.
10135{_}687{_}Joseph Taylor, that he had frequently observed a young lady, in white and green, sauntering about the spot on a St. Lawrence's Eve.
10135{_}688{_}When Captain Bryan and his lady arrived to take possession of the premises, they instituted a strict inquiry into every circumstance; but nothing further than what was related to them by Mr. M'Murdie could be learned of this Mysterious Bride, besides what the Laird's own letter bore.
10135{_}689{_}It ran thus:  "DEAREST SISTER,--I shall before this time to-morrow be the most happy, or most miserable, of mankind, having solemnly engaged myself this night to wed a young and beautiful lady, named Jane Ogilvie, to whom it seems I was betrothed before I was born.
10135{_}690{_}Our correspondence has been of a most private and mysterious nature; but my troth is pledged, and my resolution fixed.
10135{_}691{_}We set out on a far journey to the place of her abode on the nuptial eve, so that it will be long before I see you again.
10135{_}692{_}Yours till death,  "ALLAN GEORGE SANDISON.
10135{_}693{_}"BIRKENDELLY, _August 8_, 1781."
10135{_}694{_}That very same year, an old woman, named Marion Haw, was returned upon that, her native parish, from Glasgow.
10135{_}695{_}She had led a migratory life with her son--who was what he called a bell-hanger, but in fact a tinker of the worst grade--for many years, and was at last returned to the muckle town in a state of great destitution.
10135{_}696{_}She gave the parishioners a history of the Mysterious Bride, so plausibly correct, but withal so romantic, that everybody said of it (as is often said of my narratives, with the same narrow-minded prejudice and injustice) that it was a _made story_.
10135{_}697{_}There were, however, some strong testimonies of its veracity.
10135{_}698{_}She said the first Allan Sandison, who married the great heiress of Birkendelly, was previously engaged to a beautiful young lady named Jane Ogilvie, to whom he gave anything but fair play; and, as she believed, either murdered her, or caused her to be murdered, in the midst of a thicket of birch and broom, at a spot which she mentioned; and she had good reason for believing so, as she had seen the red blood and the new grave, when she was a little girl, and ran home and mentioned it to her grandfather, who charged her as she valued her life never to mention that again, as it was only the nombles and hide of a deer which he himself had buried there.
10135{_}699{_}But when, twenty years subsequent to that, the wicked and unhappy Allan Sandison was found dead on that very spot, and lying across the green mound, then nearly level with the surface, which she had once seen a new grave, she then for the first time ever thought of a Divine Providence; and she added, "For my grandfather, Neddy Haw, he dee'd too; there's naebody kens how, nor ever shall."
10135{_}700{_}As they were quite incapable of conceiving from Marion's description anything of the spot, Mr. M'Murdie caused her to be taken out to the Birky Brow in a cart, accompanied by Mr. Taylor and some hundreds of the town's folks; but whenever she saw it, she said, "Aha, birkies!
10135{_}701{_}the haill kintra's altered now.
10135{_}702{_}There was nae road here then; it gaed straight ower the tap o' the hill.
10135{_}703{_}An' let me see--there's the thorn where the cushats biggit; an' there's the auld birk that I ance fell aff an' left my shoe sticking i' the cleft.
10135{_}704{_}I can tell ye, birkies, either the deer's grave or bonny Jane Ogilvie's is no twa yards aff the place where that horse's hind-feet are standin'; sae ye may howk, an' see if there be ony remains."
10135{_}705{_}The minister and M'Murdie and all the people stared at one another, for they had purposely caused the horse to stand still on the very spot where both the father and son had been found dead.
10135{_}706{_}They digged, and deep, deep below the road they found part of the slender bones and skull of a young female, which they deposited decently in the church-yard.
10135{_}707{_}The family of the Sandisons is extinct, the Mysterious Bride appears no more on the Eve of St. Lawrence, and the wicked people of the great muckle village have got a lesson on divine justice written to them in lines of blood.
10135{_}708{_}THE DEVIL AND TOM WALKER [1]  [Footnote 1: From _The Money-diggers_.]
10135{_}709{_}_Washington Irving_ (1783-1859)   A few miles from Boston, in Massachusetts, there is a deep inlet winding several miles into the interior of the country from Charles Bay, and terminating in a thickly wooded swamp or morass.
10135{_}710{_}On one side of this inlet is a beautiful dark grove; on the opposite side the land rises abruptly from the water's edge into a high ridge, on which grow a few scattered oaks of great age and immense size.
10135{_}711{_}Under one of these gigantic trees, according to old stories, there was a great amount of treasure buried by Kidd the pirate.
10135{_}712{_}The inlet allowed a facility to bring the money in a boat secretly, and at night, to the very foot of the hill; the elevation of the place permitted a good lookout to be kept that no one was at hand; while the remarkable trees formed good landmarks by which the place might easily be found again.
10135{_}713{_}The old stories add, moreover, that the devil presided at the hiding of the money, and took it under his guardianship; but this, it is well known, he always does with buried treasure, particularly when it has been ill-gotten.
10135{_}714{_}Be that as it may, Kidd never returned to recover his wealth; being shortly after seized at Boston, sent out to England, and there hanged for a pirate.
10135{_}715{_}About the year 1727, just at the time that earthquakes were prevalent in New England, and shook many tall sinners down upon their knees, there lived near this place a meagre, miserly fellow, of the name of Tom Walker.
10135{_}716{_}He had a wife as miserly as himself; they were so miserly that they even conspired to cheat each other.
10135{_}717{_}Whatever the woman could lay hands on she hid away; a hen could not cackle but she was on the alert to secure the new-laid egg.
10135{_}718{_}Her husband was continually prying about to detect her secret hoards, and many and fierce were the conflicts that took place about what ought to have been common property.
10135{_}719{_}They lived in a forlorn-looking house that stood alone and had an air of starvation.
10135{_}720{_}A few straggling savin-trees, emblems of sterility, grew near it; no smoke ever curled from its chimney; no traveller stopped at its door.
10135{_}721{_}A miserable horse, whose ribs were as articulate as the bars of a gridiron, stalked about a field, where a thin carpet of moss, scarcely covering the ragged beds of pudding-stone, tantalized and balked his hunger; and sometimes he would lean his head over the fence, look piteously at the passer-by, and seem to petition deliverance from this land of famine.
10135{_}722{_}The house and its inmates had altogether a bad name.
10135{_}723{_}Tom's wife was a tall termagant, fierce of temper, loud of tongue, and strong of arm.
10135{_}724{_}Her voice was often heard in wordy warfare with her husband; and his face sometimes showed signs that their conflicts were not confined to words.
10135{_}725{_}No one ventured, however, to interfere between them.
10135{_}726{_}The lonely wayfarer shrank within himself at the horrid clamor and clapper-clawing; eyed the den of discord askance; and hurried on his way, rejoicing, if a bachelor, in his celibacy.
10135{_}727{_}One day that Tom Walker had been to a distant part of the neighborhood, he took what he considered a short-cut homeward, through the swamp.
10135{_}728{_}Like most short-cuts, it was an ill-chosen route.
10135{_}729{_}The swamp was thickly grown with great, gloomy pines and hemlocks, some of them ninety feet high, which made it dark at noonday and a retreat for all the owls of the neighborhood.
10135{_}730{_}It was full of pits and quagmires, partly covered with weeds and mosses, where the green surface often betrayed the traveller into a gulf of black, smothering mud; there were also dark and stagnant pools, the abodes of the tadpole, the bull-frog, and the water-snake, where the trunks of pines and hemlocks lay half-drowned, half-rotting, looking like alligators sleeping in the mire.
10135{_}731{_}Tom had long been picking his way cautiously through this treacherous forest, stepping from tuft to tuft of rushes and roots, which afforded precarious footholds among deep sloughs, or pacing carefully, like a cat, along the prostrate trunks of trees, startled now and then by the sudden screaming of the bittern, or the quacking of a wild duck, rising on the wing from some solitary pool.
10135{_}732{_}At length he arrived at a firm piece of ground, which ran like a peninsula into the deep bosom of the swamp.
10135{_}733{_}It had been one of the strongholds of the Indians during their wars with the first colonists.
10135{_}734{_}Here they had thrown up a kind of fort, which they had looked upon as almost impregnable, and had used as a place of refuge for their squaws and children.
10135{_}735{_}Nothing remained of the old Indian fort but a few embankments, gradually sinking to the level of the surrounding earth, and already overgrown in part by oaks and other forest trees, the foliage of which formed a contrast to the dark pines and hemlocks of the swamps.
10135{_}736{_}It was late in the dusk of evening when Tom Walker reached the old fort, and he paused there awhile to rest himself.
10135{_}737{_}Any one but he would have felt unwilling to linger in this lonely, melancholy place, for the common people had a bad opinion of it, from the stories handed down from the times of the Indian wars, when it was asserted that the savages held incantations here and made sacrifices to the Evil Spirit.
10135{_}738{_}Tom Walker, however, was not a man to be troubled with any fears of the kind.
10135{_}739{_}He reposed himself for some time on the trunk of a fallen hemlock, listening to the boding cry of the tree-toad, and delving with his walking-staff into a mound of black mould at his feet.
10135{_}740{_}As he turned up the soil unconsciously, his staff struck against something hard.
10135{_}741{_}He raked it out of the vegetable mould, and lo!
10135{_}742{_}a cloven skull, with an Indian tomahawk buried deep in it, lay before him.
10135{_}743{_}The rust on the weapon showed the time that had elapsed since this death-blow had been given.
10135{_}744{_}It was a dreary memento of the fierce struggle that had taken place in this last foothold of the Indian warriors.
10135{_}745{_}"Humph!"
10135{_}746{_}said Tom Walker, as he gave it a kick to shake the dirt from it.
10135{_}747{_}"Let that skull alone!"
10135{_}748{_}said a gruff voice.
10135{_}749{_}Tom lifted up his eyes and beheld a great black man seated directly opposite him, on the stump of a tree.
10135{_}750{_}He was exceedingly surprised, having neither heard nor seen any one approach; and he was still more perplexed on observing, as well as the gathering gloom would permit, that the stranger was neither negro nor Indian.
10135{_}751{_}It is true he was dressed in a rude Indian garb, and had a red belt or sash swathed round his body; but his face was neither black nor copper-color, but swarthy and dingy, and begrimed with soot, as if he had been accustomed to toil among fires and forges.
10135{_}752{_}He had a shock of coarse black hair, that stood out from his head in all directions, and bore an axe on his shoulder.
10135{_}753{_}He scowled for a moment at Tom with a pair of great red eyes.
10135{_}754{_}"What are you doing on my grounds?"
10135{_}755{_}said the black man, with a hoarse, growling voice.
10135{_}756{_}"Your grounds!"
10135{_}757{_}said Tom, with a sneer; "no more your grounds than mine; they belong to Deacon Peabody."
10135{_}758{_}"Deacon Peabody be damned," said the stranger, "as I flatter myself he will be, if he does not look more to his own sins and less to those of his neighbors.
10135{_}759{_}Look yonder, and see how Deacon Peabody is faring."
10135{_}760{_}Tom looked in the direction that the stranger pointed, and beheld one of the great trees, fair and flourishing without, but rotten at the core, and saw that it had been nearly hewn through, so that the first high wind was likely to blow it down.
10135{_}761{_}On the bark of the tree was scored the name of Deacon Peabody, an eminent man who had waxed wealthy by driving shrewd bargains with the Indians.
10135{_}762{_}He now looked around, and found most of the tall trees marked with the name of some great man of the colony, and all more or less scored by the axe.
10135{_}763{_}The one on which he had been seated, and which had evidently just been hewn down, bore the name of Crowninshield; and he recollected a mighty rich man of that name, who made a vulgar display of wealth, which it was whispered he had acquired by buccaneering.
10135{_}764{_}"He's just ready for burning!"
10135{_}765{_}said the black man, with a growl of triumph.
10135{_}766{_}"You see I am likely to have a good stock of firewood for winter."
10135{_}767{_}"But what right have you," said Tom, "to cut down Deacon Peabody's timber?"
10135{_}768{_}"The right of a prior claim," said the other.
10135{_}769{_}"This woodland belonged to me long before one of your white-faced race put foot upon the soil."
10135{_}770{_}"And, pray, who are you, if I may be so bold?"
10135{_}771{_}said Tom.
10135{_}772{_}"Oh, I go by various names.
10135{_}773{_}I am the wild huntsman in some countries; the black miner in others.
10135{_}774{_}In this neighborhood I am known by the name of the black woodsman.
10135{_}775{_}I am he to whom the red men consecrated this spot, and in honor of whom they now and then roasted a white man, by way of sweet-smelling sacrifice.
10135{_}776{_}Since the red men have been exterminated by you white savages, I amuse myself by presiding at the persecutions of Quakers and Anabaptists; I am the great patron and prompter of slave-dealers and the grand-master of the Salem witches."
10135{_}777{_}"The upshot of all which is, that, if I mistake not," said Tom, sturdily, "you are he commonly called Old Scratch."
10135{_}778{_}"The same, at your service!"
10135{_}779{_}replied the black man, with a half-civil nod.
10135{_}780{_}Such was the opening of this interview, according to the old story; though it has almost too familiar an air to be credited.
10135{_}781{_}One would think that to meet with such a singular personage in this wild, lonely place would have shaken any man's nerves; but Tom was a hard-minded fellow, not easily daunted, and he had lived so long with a termagant wife that he did not even fear the devil.
10135{_}782{_}It is said that after this commencement they had a long and earnest conversation together, as Tom returned homeward.
10135{_}783{_}The black man told him of great sums of money buried by Kidd the pirate under the oak-trees on the high ridge, not far from the morass.
10135{_}784{_}All these were under his command, and protected by his power, so that none could find them but such as propitiated his favor.
10135{_}785{_}These he offered to place within Tom Walker's reach, having conceived an especial kindness for him; but they were to be had only on certain conditions.
10135{_}786{_}What these conditions were may be easily surmised, though Tom never disclosed them publicly.
10135{_}787{_}They must have been very hard, for he required time to think of them, and he was not a man to stick at trifles when money was in view.
10135{_}788{_}When they had reached the edge of the swamp, the stranger paused.
10135{_}789{_}"What proof have I that all you have been telling me is true?"
10135{_}790{_}said Tom.
10135{_}791{_}"There's my signature," said the black man, pressing his finger on Tom's forehead.
10135{_}792{_}So saying, he turned off among the thickets of the swamp, and seemed, as Tom said, to go down, down, down, into the earth, until nothing but his head and shoulders could be seen, and so on, until he totally disappeared.
10135{_}793{_}When Tom reached home he found the black print of a finger burned, as it were, into his forehead, which nothing could obliterate.
10135{_}794{_}The first news his wife had to tell him was the sudden death of Absalom Crowninshield, the rich buccaneer.
10135{_}795{_}It was announced in the papers, with the usual flourish, that "A great man had fallen in Israel."
10135{_}796{_}Tom recollected the tree which his black friend had just hewn down, and which was ready for burning.
10135{_}797{_}"Let the freebooter roast," said Tom; "who cares!"
10135{_}798{_}He now felt convinced that all he had heard and seen was no illusion.
10135{_}799{_}He was not prone to let his wife into his confidence; but as this was an uneasy secret, he willingly shared it with her.
10135{_}800{_}All her avarice was awakened at the mention of hidden gold, and she urged her husband to comply with the black man's terms, and secure what would make them wealthy for life.
10135{_}801{_}However Tom might have felt disposed to sell himself to the devil, he was determined not to do so to oblige his wife; so he flatly refused, out of the mere spirit of contradiction.
10135{_}802{_}Many and bitter were the quarrels they had on the subject; but the more she talked, the more resolute was Tom not to be damned to please her.
10135{_}803{_}At length she determined to drive the bargain on her own account, and, if she succeeded, to keep all the gain to herself.
10135{_}804{_}Being of the same fearless temper as her husband, she set off for the old Indian fort toward the close of a summer's day.
10135{_}805{_}She was many hours absent.
10135{_}806{_}When she came back, she was reserved and sullen in her replies.
10135{_}807{_}She spoke something of a black man, whom she had met about twilight hewing at the root of a tall tree.
10135{_}808{_}He was sulky, however, and would not come to terms; she was to go again with a propitiatory offering, but what it was she forbore to say.
10135{_}809{_}The next evening she set off again for the swamp, with her apron heavily laden.
10135{_}810{_}Tom waited and waited for her, but in vain; midnight came, but she did not make her appearance; morning, noon, night returned, but still she did not come.
10135{_}811{_}Tom now grew uneasy for her safety, especially as he found she had carried off in her apron the silver tea-pot and spoons, and every portable article of value.
10135{_}812{_}Another night elapsed, another morning came; but no wife.
10135{_}813{_}In a word, she was never heard of more.
10135{_}814{_}What was her real fate nobody knows, in consequence of so many pretending to know.
10135{_}815{_}It is one of those facts which have become confounded by a variety of historians.
10135{_}816{_}Some asserted that she lost her way among the tangled mazes of the swamp, and sank into some pit or slough; others, more uncharitable, hinted that she had eloped with the household booty, and made off to some other province; while others surmised that the tempter had decoyed her into a dismal quagmire, on the top of which her hat was found lying.
10135{_}817{_}In confirmation of this, it was said a great black man, with an axe on his shoulder, was seen late that very evening coming out of the swamp, carrying a bundle tied in a check apron, with an air of surly triumph.
10135{_}818{_}The most current and probable story, however, observes that Tom Walker grew so anxious about the fate of his wife and his property that he set out at length to seek them both at the Indian fort.
10135{_}819{_}During a long summer's afternoon he searched about the gloomy place, but no wife was to be seen.
10135{_}820{_}He called her name repeatedly, but she was nowhere to be heard.
10135{_}821{_}The bittern alone responded to his voice, as he flew screaming by; or the bull-frog croaked dolefully from a neighboring pool.
10135{_}822{_}At length, it is said, just in the brown hour of twilight, when the owls began to hoot and the bats to flit about, his attention was attracted by the clamor of carrion crows hovering about a cypress-tree.
10135{_}823{_}He looked up and beheld a bundle tied in a check apron and hanging in the branches of the tree, with a great vulture perched hard by, as if keeping watch upon it.
10135{_}824{_}He leaped with joy, for he recognized his wife's apron, and supposed it to contain the household valuables.
10135{_}825{_}"Let us get hold of the property," said he, consolingly, to himself, "and we will endeavor to do without the woman."
10135{_}826{_}As he scrambled up the tree, the vulture spread its wide wings and sailed off, screaming, into the deep shadows of the forest.
10135{_}827{_}Tom seized the checked apron, but, woful sight!
10135{_}828{_}found nothing but a heart and liver tied up in it!
10135{_}829{_}Such, according to this most authentic old story, was all that was to be found of Tom's wife.
10135{_}830{_}She had probably attempted to deal with the black man as she had been accustomed to deal with her husband; but though a female scold is generally considered a match for the devil, yet in this instance she appears to have had the worst of it.
10135{_}831{_}She must have died game, however; for it is said Tom noticed many prints of cloven feet deeply stamped about the tree, and found handfuls of hair, that looked as if they had been plucked from the coarse black shock of the woodsman.
10135{_}832{_}Tom knew his wife's prowess by experience.
10135{_}833{_}He shrugged his shoulders as he looked at the signs of fierce clapper-clawing.
10135{_}834{_}"Egad," said he to himself, "Old Scratch must have had a tough time of it!"
10135{_}835{_}Tom consoled himself for the loss of his property, with the loss of his wife, for he was a man of fortitude.
10135{_}836{_}He even felt something like gratitude toward the black woodsman, who, he considered, had done him a kindness.
10135{_}837{_}He sought, therefore, to cultivate a further acquaintance with him, but for some time without success; the old black-legs played shy, for, whatever people may think, he is not always to be had for the calling; he knows how to play his cards when pretty sure of his game.
10135{_}838{_}At length, it is said, when delay had whetted Tom's eagerness to the quick and prepared him to agree to anything rather than not gain the promised treasure, he met the black man one evening in his usual woodsman's dress, with his axe on his shoulder, sauntering along the swamp and humming a tune.
10135{_}839{_}He affected to receive Tom's advances with great indifference, made brief replies, and went on humming his tune.
10135{_}840{_}By degrees, however, Tom brought him to business, and they began to haggle about the terms on which the former was to have the pirate's treasure.
10135{_}841{_}There was one condition which need not be mentioned, being generally understood in all cases where the devil grants favors; but there were others about which, though of less importance, he was inflexibly obstinate.
10135{_}842{_}He insisted that the money found through his means should be employed in his service.
10135{_}843{_}He proposed, therefore, that Tom should employ it in the black traffic; that is to say, that he should fit out a slave-ship.
10135{_}844{_}This, however, Tom resolutely refused; he was bad enough in all conscience, but the devil himself could not tempt him to turn slave-trader.
10135{_}845{_}Finding Tom so squeamish on this point, he did not insist upon it, but proposed, instead, that he should turn usurer; the devil being extremely anxious for the increase of usurers, looking upon them as his peculiar people.
10135{_}846{_}To this no objections were made, for it was just to Tom's taste.
10135{_}847{_}"You shall open a broker's shop in Boston next month," said the black man.
10135{_}848{_}"I'll do it to-morrow, if you wish," said Tom Walker.
10135{_}849{_}"You shall lend money at two per cent.
10135{_}850{_}a month."
10135{_}851{_}"Egad, I'll charge four!"
10135{_}852{_}replied Tom Walker.
10135{_}853{_}"You shall extort bonds, foreclose mortgages, drive the merchants to bankruptcy--"  "I'll drive them to the devil," cried Tom Walker.
10135{_}854{_}"_You_ are the usurer for my money!"
10135{_}855{_}said black-legs with delight.
10135{_}856{_}"When will you want the rhino?"
10135{_}857{_}"This very night."
10135{_}858{_}"Done!"
10135{_}859{_}said the devil.
10135{_}860{_}"Done!"
10135{_}861{_}said Tom Walker.
10135{_}862{_}So they shook hands and struck a bargain.
10135{_}863{_}A few days' time saw Tom Walker seated behind his desk in a counting-house in Boston.
10135{_}864{_}His reputation for a ready-moneyed man, who would lend money out for a good consideration, soon spread abroad.
10135{_}865{_}Everybody remembers the time of Governor Belcher, when money was particularly scarce.
10135{_}866{_}It was a time of paper credit.
10135{_}867{_}The country had been deluged with government bills; the famous Land Bank had been established; there had been a rage for speculating; the people had run mad with schemes for new settlements, for building cities in the wilderness; land-jobbers went about with maps of grants and townships and Eldorados, lying nobody knew where, but which everybody was ready to purchase.
10135{_}868{_}In a word, the great speculating fever which breaks out every now and then in the country had raged to an alarming degree, and everybody was dreaming of making sudden fortunes from nothing.
10135{_}869{_}As usual, the fever had subsided, the dream had gone off, and the imaginary fortunes with it; the patients were left in doleful plight, and the whole country resounded with the consequent cry of "hard times."
10135{_}870{_}At this propitious time of public distress did Tom Walker set up as usurer in Boston.
10135{_}871{_}His door was soon thronged by customers.
10135{_}872{_}The needy and adventurous, the gambling speculator, the dreaming land-jobber, the thriftless tradesman, the merchant with cracked credit--in short, everyone driven to raise money by desperate means and desperate sacrifices hurried to Tom Walker.
10135{_}873{_}Thus Tom was the universal friend to the needy, and acted like "a friend in need"; that is to say, he always exacted good pay and security.
10135{_}874{_}In proportion to the distress of the applicant was the hardness of his terms.
10135{_}875{_}He accumulated bonds and mortgages, gradually squeezed his customers closer and closer, and sent them at length, dry as a sponge, from his door.
10135{_}876{_}In this way he made money hand over hand, became a rich and mighty man, and exalted his cocked hat upon "Change."
10135{_}877{_}He built himself, as usual, a vast house, out of ostentation, but left the greater part of it unfinished and unfurnished, out of parsimony.
10135{_}878{_}He even set up a carriage in the fulness of his vain-glory, though he nearly starved the horses which drew it; and, as the ungreased wheels groaned and screeched on the axle-trees, you would have thought you heard the souls of the poor debtors he was squeezing.
10135{_}879{_}As Tom waxed old, however, he grew thoughtful.
10135{_}880{_}Having secured the good things of this world, he began to feel anxious about those of the next.
10135{_}881{_}He thought with regret of the bargain he had made with his black friend, and set his wits to work to cheat him out of the conditions.
10135{_}882{_}He became, therefore, all of a sudden, a violent church-goer.
10135{_}883{_}He prayed loudly and strenuously, as if heaven were to be taken by force of lungs.
10135{_}884{_}Indeed, one might always tell when he had sinned most during the week by the clamor of his Sunday devotion.
10135{_}885{_}The quiet Christians who had been modestly and steadfastly travelling Zionward were struck with self-reproach at seeing themselves so suddenly outstripped in their career by this new-made convert.
10135{_}886{_}Tom was as rigid in religious as in money matters; he was a stern supervisor and censurer of his neighbors, and seemed to think every sin entered up to their account became a credit on his own side of the page.
10135{_}887{_}He even talked of the expediency of reviving the persecution of Quakers and Anabaptists.
10135{_}888{_}In a word, Tom's zeal became as notorious as his riches.
10135{_}889{_}Still, in spite of all this strenuous attention to forms, Tom had a lurking dread that the devil, after all, would have his due.
10135{_}890{_}That he might not be taken unawares, therefore, it is said he always carried a small Bible in his coat-pocket.
10135{_}891{_}He had also a great folio Bible on his counting-house desk, and would frequently be found reading it when people called on business; on such occasions he would lay his green spectacles in the book, to mark the place, while he turned round to drive some usurious bargain.
10135{_}892{_}Some say that Tom grew a little crack-brained in his old days, and that, fancying his end approaching, he had his horse new shod, saddled, and bridled, and buried with his feet uppermost; because he supposed that at the last day the world would be turned upside-down; in which case he should find his horse standing ready for mounting, and he was determined at the worst to give his old friend a run for it.
10135{_}893{_}This, however, is probably a mere old wives' fable.
10135{_}894{_}If he really did take such a precaution, it was totally superfluous; at least so says the authentic old legend, which closes his story in the following manner:  One hot summer afternoon in the dog-days, just as a terrible black thunder-gust was coming up, Tom sat in his counting-house, in his white linen cap and India silk morning-gown.
10135{_}895{_}He was on the point of foreclosing a mortgage, by which he would complete the ruin of an unlucky land-speculator for whom he had professed the greatest friendship.
10135{_}896{_}The poor land-jobber begged him to grant a few months' indulgence.
10135{_}897{_}Tom had grown testy and irritated, and refused another delay.
10135{_}898{_}"My family will be ruined, and brought upon the parish," said the land-jobber.
10135{_}899{_}"Charity begins at home," replied Tom; "I must take care of myself in these hard times."
10135{_}900{_}"You have made so much money out of me," said the speculator.
10135{_}901{_}Tom lost his patience and his piety.
10135{_}902{_}"The devil take me," said he, "if I have made a farthing!"
10135{_}903{_}Just then there were three loud knocks at the street door.
10135{_}904{_}He stepped out to see who was there.
10135{_}905{_}A black man was holding a black horse, which neighed and stamped with impatience.
10135{_}906{_}"Tom, you're come for," said the black fellow, gruffly.
10135{_}907{_}Tom shrank back, but too late.
10135{_}908{_}He had left his little Bible at the bottom of his coat-pocket and his big Bible on the desk buried under the mortgage he was about to foreclose: never was sinner taken more unawares.
10135{_}909{_}The black man whisked him like a child into the saddle, gave the horse the lash, and away he galloped, with Tom on his back, in the midst of the thunder-storm.
10135{_}910{_}The clerks stuck their pens behind their ears, and stared after him from the windows.
10135{_}911{_}Away went Tom Walker, dashing down the streets, his white cap bobbing up and down, his morning-gown fluttering in the wind, and his steed striking fire out of the pavement at every bound.
10135{_}912{_}When the clerks turned to look for the black man, he had disappeared.
10135{_}913{_}Tom Walker never returned to foreclose the mortgage.
10135{_}914{_}A countryman, who lived on the border of the swamp, reported that in the height of the thunder-gust he had heard a great clattering of hoofs and a howling along the road, and running to the window caught sight of a figure, such as I have described, on a horse that galloped like mad across the fields, over the hills, and down into the black hemlock swamp toward the old Indian fort, and that shortly after a thunder-bolt falling in that direction seemed to set the whole forest in a blaze.
10135{_}915{_}The good people of Boston shook their heads and shrugged their shoulders, but had been so much accustomed to witches and goblins, and tricks of the devil, in all kinds of shapes, from the first settlement of the colony, that they were not so much horror-struck as might have been expected.
10135{_}916{_}Trustees were appointed to take charge of Tom's effects.
10135{_}917{_}There was nothing, however, to administer upon.
10135{_}918{_}On searching his coffers, all his bonds and mortgages were reduced to cinders.
10135{_}919{_}In place of gold and silver, his iron chest was filled with chips and shavings; two skeletons lay in his stable instead of his half-starved horses, and the very next day his great house took fire and was burned to the ground.
10135{_}920{_}Such was the end of Tom Walker and his ill-gotten wealth.
10135{_}921{_}Let all gripping money-brokers lay this story to heart.
10135{_}922{_}The truth of it is not to be doubted.
10135{_}923{_}The very hole under the oak-trees, whence he dug Kidd's money, is to be seen to this day; and the neighboring swamp and old Indian fort are often haunted in stormy nights by a figure on horseback, in morning-gown and white cap, which is doubtless the troubled spirit of the usurer.
10135{_}924{_}In fact, the story has resolved itself into a proverb, and is the origin of that popular saying, so prevalent throughout New England, of "The devil and Tom Walker."
10135{_}925{_}DR. HEIDEGGER'S EXPERIMENT  _Nathaniel Hawthorne_ (1807-1864)   That very singular man, old Doctor Heidegger, once invited four venerable friends to meet him in his study.
10135{_}926{_}There were three white-bearded gentlemen, Mr. Medbourne, Colonel Killigrew, and Mr. Gascoigne, and a withered gentlewoman whose name was the Widow Wycherley.
10135{_}927{_}They were all melancholy old creatures, who had been unfortunate in life, and whose greatest misfortune it was that they were not long ago in their graves.
10135{_}928{_}Mr. Medbourne, in the vigor of his age, had been a prosperous merchant, but had lost his all by a frantic speculation, and was no little better than a mendicant.
10135{_}929{_}Colonel Killigrew had wasted his best years, and his health and substance, in the pursuit of sinful pleasures, which had given birth to a brood of pains, such as the gout and divers other torments of soul and body.
10135{_}930{_}Mr. Gascoigne was a ruined politician, a man of evil fame, or at least had been so, till time had buried him from the knowledge of the present generation, and made him obscure instead of infamous.
10135{_}931{_}As for the Widow Wycherley, tradition tells us that she was a great beauty in her day; but, for a long while past, she had lived in deep seclusion, on account of certain scandalous stories which had prejudiced the gentry of the town against her.
10135{_}932{_}It is a circumstance worth mentioning that each of these three old gentlemen, Mr. Medbourne, Colonel Killigrew, and Mr. Gascoigne, were early lovers of the Widow Wycherley, and had once been on the point of cutting each other's throats for her sake.
10135{_}933{_}And, before proceeding further, I will merely hint that Doctor Heidegger and all his four guests were sometimes thought to be a little beside themselves; as is not unfrequently the case with old people, when worried either by present troubles or woful recollections.
10135{_}934{_}"My dear friends," said Doctor Heidegger, motioning them to be seated, "I am desirous of your assistance in one of those little experiments with which I amuse myself here in my study."
10135{_}935{_}If all stories were true, Doctor Heidegger's study must have been a very curious place.
10135{_}936{_}It was a dim, old-fashioned chamber, festooned with cobwebs and besprinkled with antique dust.
10135{_}937{_}Around the walls stood several oaken bookcases, the lower shelves of which were filled with rows of gigantic folios and black-letter quartos, and the upper with little parchment-covered duodecimos.
10135{_}938{_}Over the central bookcase was a bronze bust of Hippocrates, with which, according to some authorities, Doctor Heidegger was accustomed to hold consultations in all difficult cases of his practice.
10135{_}939{_}In the obscurest corner of the room stood a tall and narrow oaken closet, with its door ajar, within which doubtfully appeared a skeleton.
10135{_}940{_}Between two of the bookcases hung a looking-glass, presenting its high and dusty plate within a tarnished gilt frame.
10135{_}941{_}Among many wonderful stories related of this mirror, it was fabled that the spirits of all the doctor's deceased patients dwelt within its verge, and would stare him in the face whenever he looked thitherward.
10135{_}942{_}The opposite side of the chamber was ornamented with the full-length portrait of a young lady, arrayed in the faded magnificence of silk, satin, and brocade, and with a visage as faded as her dress.
10135{_}943{_}Above half a century ago Doctor Heidegger had been on the point of marriage with this young lady; but, being affected with some slight disorder, she had swallowed one of her lover's prescriptions, and died on the bridal evening.
10135{_}944{_}The greatest curiosity of the study remains to be mentioned; it was a ponderous folio volume, bound in black leather, with massive silver clasps.
10135{_}945{_}There were no letters on the back, and nobody could tell the title of the book.
10135{_}946{_}But it was well known to be a book of magic; and once, when a chambermaid had lifted it, merely to brush away the dust, the skeleton had rattled in its closet, the picture of the young lady had stepped one foot upon the floor, and several ghastly faces had peeped forth from the mirror; while the brazen head of Hippocrates frowned, and said: "Forbear!"
10135{_}947{_}Such was Doctor Heidegger's study.
10135{_}948{_}On the summer afternoon of our tale a small round table, as black as ebony, stood in the centre of the room, sustaining a cut-glass vase of beautiful form and workmanship.
10135{_}949{_}The sunshine came through the window, between the heavy festoons of two faded damask curtains, and fell directly across this vase; so that a mild splendor was reflected from it on the ashen visages of the five old people who sat around.
10135{_}950{_}Four champagne glasses were also on the table.
10135{_}951{_}"My dear old friends," repeated Doctor Heidegger, "may I reckon on your aid in performing an exceedingly curious experiment?"
10135{_}952{_}Now Doctor Heidegger was a very strange old gentleman, whose eccentricity had become the nucleus for a thousand fantastic stories.
10135{_}953{_}Some of these fables, to my shame be it spoken, might possibly be traced back to mine own veracious self; and if any passages of the present tale should startle the reader's faith, I must be content to bear the stigma of a fiction-monger.
10135{_}954{_}When the doctor's four guests heard him talk of his proposed experiment, they anticipated nothing more wonderful than the murder of a mouse in an air-pump or the examination of a cobweb by the microscope, or some similiar nonsense, with which he was constantly in the habit of pestering his intimates.
10135{_}955{_}But without waiting for a reply, Doctor Heidegger hobbled across the chamber, and returned with the same ponderous folio, bound in black leather, which common report affirmed to be a book of magic.
10135{_}956{_}Undoing the silver clasps, he opened the volume, and took from among its black-letter pages a rose, or what was once a rose, though now the green leaves and crimson petals had assumed one brownish hue, and the ancient flower seemed ready to crumble to dust in the doctor's hands.
10135{_}957{_}"This rose," said Doctor Heidegger, with a sigh, "this same withered and crumbling flower, blossomed five and fifty years ago.
10135{_}958{_}It was given me by Sylvia Ward, whose portrait hangs yonder, and I meant to wear it in my bosom at our wedding.
10135{_}959{_}Five and fifty years it has been treasured between the leaves of this old volume.
10135{_}960{_}Now, would you deem it possible that this rose of half a century could ever bloom again?"
10135{_}961{_}"Nonsense!"
10135{_}962{_}said the Widow Wycherley, with a peevish toss of her head.
10135{_}963{_}"You might as well ask whether an old woman's wrinkled face could ever bloom again."
10135{_}964{_}"See!"
10135{_}965{_}answered Doctor Heidegger.
10135{_}966{_}He uncovered the vase, and threw the faded rose into the water which it contained.
10135{_}967{_}At first, it lay lightly on the surface of the fluid, appearing to imbibe none of its moisture.
10135{_}968{_}Soon, however, a singular change began to be visible.
10135{_}969{_}The crushed and dried petals stirred, and assumed a deepening tinge of crimson, as if the flower were reviving from a death-like slumber; the slender stalk and twigs of foliage became green; and there was the rose of half a century, looking as fresh as when Sylvia Ward had first given it to her lover.
10135{_}970{_}It was scarcely full blown; for some of its delicate red leaves curled modestly around its moist bosom, within which two or three dewdrops were sparkling.
10135{_}971{_}"That is certainly a very pretty deception," said the doctor's friends; careless, however, for they had witnessed greater miracles at a conjurer's show; "pray how was it effected?"
10135{_}972{_}"Did you ever hear of the 'Fountain of Youth,'" asked Doctor Heidegger, "which Ponce de Leon, the Spanish adventurer, went in search of, two or three centuries ago?"
10135{_}973{_}"But did Ponce de Leon ever find it?"
10135{_}974{_}said the Widow Wycherley.
10135{_}975{_}"No," answered Doctor Heidegger, "for he never sought it in the right place.
10135{_}976{_}The famous Fountain of Youth, if I am rightly informed, is situated in the southern part of the Floridian peninsula, not far from Lake Macaco.
10135{_}977{_}Its source is overshadowed by several magnolias, which, though numberless centuries old, have been kept as fresh as violets, by the virtues of this wonderful water.
10135{_}978{_}An acquaintance of mine, knowing my curiosity in such matters, has sent me what you see in the vase."
10135{_}979{_}"Ahem!"
10135{_}980{_}said Colonel Killigrew, who believed not a word of the doctor's story; "and what may be the effect of this fluid on the human frame?"
10135{_}981{_}"You shall judge for yourself, my dear Colonel," replied Doctor Heidegger; "and all of you, my respected friends, are welcome to so much of this admirable fluid as may restore to you the bloom of youth.
10135{_}982{_}For my own part, having had much trouble in growing old, I am in no hurry to grow young again.
10135{_}983{_}With your permission, therefore, I will merely watch the progress of the experiment."
10135{_}984{_}While he spoke, Doctor Heidegger had been filling the four champagne glasses with the water of the Fountain of Youth.
10135{_}985{_}It was apparently impregnated with an effervescent gas; for little bubbles were continually ascending from the depths of the glasses, and bursting in silvery spray at the surface.
10135{_}986{_}As the liquor diffused a pleasant perfume, the old people doubted now that it possessed cordial and comfortable properties; and though utter sceptics as to its rejuvenescent power, they were inclined to swallow it at once.
10135{_}987{_}But Doctor Heidegger besought them to stay a moment.
10135{_}988{_}"Before you drink, my respectable old friends," said he, "it would be well that, with the experience of a lifetime to direct you, you should draw up a few general rules for your guidance, in passing a second time through the perils of youth.
10135{_}989{_}Think what a sin and shame it would be if, with your peculiar advantages, you should not become patterns of virtue and wisdom to all the young people of the age!"
10135{_}990{_}The doctor's four venerable friends made him no answer, except by a feeble and tremulous laugh; so very ridiculous was the idea that, knowing how closely repentance treads behind the steps of error, they should ever go astray again.
10135{_}991{_}"Drink, then," said the doctor, bowing: "I rejoice that I have so well selected the subjects of my experiment."
10135{_}992{_}With palsied hands they raised the glasses to their lips.
10135{_}993{_}The liquor, if it really possessed such virtues as Doctor Heidegger imputed to it, could not have been bestowed on four human beings who needed it more wofully.
10135{_}994{_}They looked as if they had never known what youth or pleasure was, but had been the offspring of nature's dotage, and always the gray, decrepit, sapless, miserable creatures, who now sat stooping round the doctor's table, without life enough in their souls or bodies to be animated even by the prospect of growing young again.
10135{_}995{_}They drank off the water, and replaced their glasses on the table.
10135{_}996{_}Assuredly there was an almost immediate improvement in the aspect of the party, not unlike what might have been produced by a glass of generous wine, together with a sudden glow of cheerful sunshine, brightening over all their visages at once.
10135{_}997{_}There was a healthful suffusion on their cheeks, instead of the ashen hue that had made them look so corpselike.
10135{_}998{_}They gazed at one another, and fancied that some magic power had really begun to smooth away the deep and sad inscriptions which Father Time had been so long engraving on their brows.
10135{_}999{_}The Widow Wycherley adjusted her cap, for she felt almost like a woman again.
10135{_}1000{_}"Give us more of this wondrous water!"
10135{_}1001{_}cried they, eagerly.
10135{_}1002{_}"We are younger--but we are still too old!
10135{_}1003{_}Quick--give us more!"
10135{_}1004{_}"Patience!
10135{_}1005{_}patience!"
10135{_}1006{_}quoth Doctor Heidegger, who sat watching the experiment with philosophic coolness.
10135{_}1007{_}"You have been a long time growing old.
10135{_}1008{_}Surely you might be content to grow young in half an hour!
10135{_}1009{_}But the water is at your service."
10135{_}1010{_}Again he filled their glasses with the liquor of youth, enough of which still remained in the vase to turn half the old people in the city to the age of their own grandchildren.
10135{_}1011{_}While the bubbles were yet sparkling on the brim, the doctor's four guests snatched their glasses from the table, and swallowed the contents at a single gulp.
10135{_}1012{_}Was it delusion?
10135{_}1013{_}Even while the draught was passing down their throats it seemed to have wrought a change on their whole systems.
10135{_}1014{_}Their eyes grew clear and bright; a dark shade deepened among their silvery locks; they sat round the table, three gentlemen of middle age, and a woman hardly beyond her buxom prime.
10135{_}1015{_}"My dear widow, you are charming!"
10135{_}1016{_}cried Colonel Killigrew, whose eyes had been fixed upon her face, while the shadows of age were flitting from it like darkness from the crimson daybreak.
10135{_}1017{_}The fair widow knew of old that Colonel Killigrew's compliments were not always measured by sober truth; so she started up and ran to the mirror, still dreading that the ugly visage of an old woman would meet her gaze.
10135{_}1018{_}Meanwhile the three gentlemen behaved in such a manner as proved that the water of the Fountain of Youth possessed some intoxicating qualities, unless, indeed, their exhilaration of spirits were merely a lightsome dizziness, caused by the sudden removal of the weight of years.
10135{_}1019{_}Mr. Gascoigne's mind seemed to run on political topics, but whether relating to the past, present, or future could not easily be determined, since the same ideas and phrases have been in vogue these fifty years.
10135{_}1020{_}Now he rattled forth full-throated sentences about patriotism, national glory, and the people's rights; now he muttered some perilous stuff or other, in a sly and doubtful whisper, so cautiously that even his own conscience could scarcely catch the secret; and now, again, he spoke in measured accents and a deeply deferential tone, as if a royal ear were listening to his well-turned periods.
10135{_}1021{_}Colonel Killigrew all this time had been trolling forth a jolly battle-song, and ringing his glass toward the buxom figure of the Widow Wycherley.
10135{_}1022{_}On the other side of the table Mr. Medbourne was involved in a calculation of dollars and cents, with which was strangely intermingled a project for supplying the East Indies with ice, by harnessing a team of whales to the polar icebergs.
10135{_}1023{_}As for the Widow Wycherley, she stood before the mirror, courtesying and simpering to her own image, and greeting it as the friend whom she loved better than all the world beside.
10135{_}1024{_}She thrust her face close to the glass to see whether some long-remembered wrinkle or crow's-foot had indeed vanished.
