10135{_}1025{_}She examined whether the snow had so entirely melted from her hair that the venerable cap could be safely thrown aside.
10135{_}1026{_}At last, turning briskly away, she came with a sort of dancing step to the table.
10135{_}1027{_}"My dear old doctor," cried she, "pray favor me with another glass!"
10135{_}1028{_}"Certainly, my dear madam, certainly!"
10135{_}1029{_}replied the complaisant doctor.
10135{_}1030{_}"See!
10135{_}1031{_}I have already filled the glasses."
10135{_}1032{_}There, in fact, stood the four glasses, brimful of this wonderful water, the delicate spray of which, as it effervesced from the surface, resembled the tremulous glitter of diamonds.
10135{_}1033{_}It was now so nearly sunset that the chamber had grown duskier than ever; but a mild and moon-like splendor gleamed from within the vase, and rested alike on the four guests, and on the doctor's venerable figure.
10135{_}1034{_}He sat in a high-backed, elaborately carved oaken chair, with a gray dignity of aspect that might have well befitted that very Father Time, whose power had never been disputed, save by this fortunate company.
10135{_}1035{_}Even while quaffing the third draught of the Fountain of Youth, they were almost awed by the expression of his mysterious visage.
10135{_}1036{_}But the next moment the exhilarating gush of young life shot through their veins.
10135{_}1037{_}They were now in the happy prime of youth.
10135{_}1038{_}Age, with its miserable train of cares, and sorrows, and diseases, was remembered only as the trouble of a dream, from which they had joyously awoke.
10135{_}1039{_}The fresh gloss of the soul, so early lost, and without which the world's successive scenes had been but a gallery of faded pictures, again threw its enchantment over all their prospects.
10135{_}1040{_}They felt like new-created beings in a new-created universe.
10135{_}1041{_}"We are young!
10135{_}1042{_}We are young!"
10135{_}1043{_}they cried, exultingly.
10135{_}1044{_}Youth, like the extremity of age, had effaced the strongly marked characteristics of middle life, and mutually assimilated them all.
10135{_}1045{_}They were a group of merry youngsters, almost maddened with the exuberant frolicsomeness of their years.
10135{_}1046{_}The most singular effect of their gayety was an impulse to mock the infirmity and decrepitude of which they had so lately been the victims.
10135{_}1047{_}They laughed loudly at their old-fashioned attire--the wide-skirted coats and flapped waistcoats of the young men, and the ancient cap and gown of the blooming girl.
10135{_}1048{_}One limped across the floor like a gouty grandfather; one set a pair of spectacles astride of his nose, and pretended to pore over the black-letter pages of the book of magic; a third seated himself in an arm-chair, and strove to imitate the venerable dignity of Doctor Heidegger.
10135{_}1049{_}Then all shouted mirthfully, and leaped about the room.
10135{_}1050{_}The Widow Wycherley--if so fresh a damsel could be called a widow--tripped up to the doctor's chair with a mischievous merriment in her rosy face.
10135{_}1051{_}"Doctor, you dear old soul," cried she, "get up and dance with me!"
10135{_}1052{_}And then the four young people laughed louder than ever, to think what a queer figure the poor old doctor would cut.
10135{_}1053{_}"Pray excuse me," answered the doctor, quietly.
10135{_}1054{_}"I am old and rheumatic, and my dancing days were over long ago.
10135{_}1055{_}But either of these gay young gentlemen will be glad of so pretty a partner."
10135{_}1056{_}"Dance with me, Clara!"
10135{_}1057{_}cried Colonel Killigrew.
10135{_}1058{_}"She promised me her hand fifty years ago!"
10135{_}1059{_}exclaimed Mr. Medbourne.
10135{_}1060{_}They all gathered round her.
10135{_}1061{_}One caught both her hands in his passionate grasp--another threw his arm about her waist--the third buried his hand among the curls that clustered beneath the widow's cap.
10135{_}1062{_}Blushing, panting, struggling, chiding, laughing, her warm breath fanning each of their faces by turns, she strove to disengage herself, yet still remained in their triple embrace.
10135{_}1063{_}Never was there a livelier picture of youthful rivalship, with bewitching beauty for the prize.
10135{_}1064{_}Yet, by a strange deception, owing to the duskiness of the chamber and the antique dresses which they still wore, the tall mirror is said to have reflected the figures of the three old, gray, withered grand-sires, ridiculously contending for the skinny ugliness of a shrivelled grandam.
10135{_}1065{_}But they were young: their burning passions proved them so.
10135{_}1066{_}Inflamed to madness by the coquetry of the girl-widow, who neither granted nor quite withheld her favors, the three rivals began to interchange threatening glances.
10135{_}1067{_}Still keeping hold of the fair prize, they grappled fiercely at one another's throats.
10135{_}1068{_}As they struggled to and fro, the table was overturned, and the vase dashed into a thousand fragments.
10135{_}1069{_}The precious Water of Youth flowed in a bright stream across the floor, moistening the wings of a butterfly, which, grown old in the decline of summer, had alighted there to die.
10135{_}1070{_}The insect fluttered lightly through the chamber, and settled on the snowy head of Doctor Heidegger.
10135{_}1071{_}"Come, come, gentlemen!--come, Madame Wycherley!"
10135{_}1072{_}exclaimed the doctor, "I really must protest against this riot."
10135{_}1073{_}They stood still and shivered; for it seemed as if gray Time were calling them back from their sunny youth, far down into the chill and darksome vale of years.
10135{_}1074{_}They looked at old Doctor Heidegger, who sat in his carved arm-chair, holding the rose of half a century which he had rescued from among the fragments of the shattered vase.
10135{_}1075{_}At the motion of his hand the rioters resumed their seats, the more readily because their violent exertions had wearied them, youthful though they were.
10135{_}1076{_}"My poor Sylvia's rose!"
10135{_}1077{_}ejaculated Doctor Heidegger, holding it in the light of the sunset clouds; "it appears to be fading again."
10135{_}1078{_}And so it was.
10135{_}1079{_}Even while the party were looking at it the flower continued to shrivel up, till it became as dry and fragile as when the doctor had first thrown it into the vase.
10135{_}1080{_}He shook off the few drops of moisture which clung to its petals.
10135{_}1081{_}"I love it as well thus as in its dewy freshness," observed he, pressing the withered rose to his withered lips.
10135{_}1082{_}While he spoke, the butterfly fluttered down from the doctor's snowy head, and fell upon the floor.
10135{_}1083{_}His guests shivered again.
10135{_}1084{_}A strange dullness, whether of the body or spirit they could not tell, was creeping gradually over them all.
10135{_}1085{_}They gazed at one another, and fancied that each fleeting moment snatched away a charm, and left a deepening furrow where none had been before.
10135{_}1086{_}Was it an illusion?
10135{_}1087{_}Had the changes of a lifetime been crowded into so brief a space, and were they now four aged people, sitting with their old friend, Doctor Heidegger?
10135{_}1088{_}"Are we grown old again so soon?"
10135{_}1089{_}cried they, dolefully.
10135{_}1090{_}In truth, they had.
10135{_}1091{_}The Water of Youth possessed merely a virtue more transient than that of wine.
10135{_}1092{_}The delirium which it created had effervesced away.
10135{_}1093{_}Yes, they were old again!
10135{_}1094{_}With a shuddering impulse, that showed her a woman still, the widow clasped her skinny hands over her face, and wished that the coffin lid were over it, since it could be no longer beautiful.
10135{_}1095{_}"Yes, friends, ye are old again," said Doctor Heidegger; "and lo!
10135{_}1096{_}the Water of Youth is all lavished on the ground.
10135{_}1097{_}Well, I bemoan it not; for if the fountain gushed at my doorstep, I would not stoop to bathe my lips in it--no, though its delirium were for years instead of moments.
10135{_}1098{_}Such is the lesson ye have taught me!"
10135{_}1099{_}But the doctor's four friends had taught no such lesson to themselves.
10135{_}1100{_}They resolved forthwith to make a pilgrimage to Florida, and quaff at morning, noon, and night from the Fountain of Youth.
10135{_}1101{_}THE PURLOINED LETTER[1]  [Footnote 1: The pattern in method for all detective stories.]
10135{_}1102{_}_Edgar Allan Poe_ (1809-1849)   At Paris, just after dark one gusty evening in the autumn of 18--, I was enjoying the twofold luxury of meditation and a meerschaum, in company with my friend, C. Auguste Dupin, in his little back library, or book-closet, _au troisieme_, No.
10135{_}1103{_}33 Rue Dunot, Faubourg St. Germain.
10135{_}1104{_}For one hour at least we had maintained a profound silence, while each, to any casual observer, might have seemed intently and exclusively occupied with the curling eddies of smoke that oppressed the atmosphere of the chamber.
10135{_}1105{_}For myself, however, I was mentally discussing certain topics which had formed matter for conversation between us at an earlier period of the evening; I mean the affair of the Rue Morgue, and the mystery attending the murder of Marie Roget.
10135{_}1106{_}I looked upon it, therefore, as something of a coincidence, when the door of our apartment was thrown open and admitted our old acquaintance, Monsieur G----, the Prefect of the Parisian police.
10135{_}1107{_}We gave him a hearty welcome; for there was nearly half as much of the entertaining as of the contemptible about the man, and we had not seen him for several years.
10135{_}1108{_}We had been sitting in the dark, and Dupin now arose for the purpose of lighting a lamp, but sat down again, without doing so, upon G----'s saying that he had called to consult us, or rather to ask the opinion of my friend, about some official business which had occasioned a great deal of trouble.
10135{_}1109{_}"If it is any point requiring reflection," observed Dupin, as he forebore to enkindle the wick, "we shall examine it to better purpose in the dark."
10135{_}1110{_}"That is another of your odd notions," said the Prefect, who had the fashion of calling everything "odd" that was beyond his comprehension, and thus lived amid an absolute legion of "oddities."
10135{_}1111{_}"Very true," said Dupin, as he supplied his visitor with a pipe, and rolled toward him a comfortable chair.
10135{_}1112{_}"And what is the difficulty now?"
10135{_}1113{_}I asked.
10135{_}1114{_}"Nothing more in the assassination way, I hope?"
10135{_}1115{_}"Oh no, nothing of that nature.
10135{_}1116{_}The fact is, the business is _very_ simple indeed, and I make no doubt that we can manage it sufficiently well ourselves; but then I thought Dupin would like to hear the details of it, because it is so excessively _odd_."
10135{_}1117{_}"Simple and odd," said Dupin.
10135{_}1118{_}"Why, yes; and not exactly that, either.
10135{_}1119{_}The fact is, we have all been a good deal puzzled because the affair is so simple, and yet baffles us altogether."
10135{_}1120{_}"Perhaps it is the very simplicity of the thing which puts you at fault," said my friend.
10135{_}1121{_}"What nonsense you _do_ talk!"
10135{_}1122{_}replied the Prefect, laughing heartily.
10135{_}1123{_}"Perhaps the mystery is a little _too_ plain," said Dupin.
10135{_}1124{_}"Oh, good heavens!
10135{_}1125{_}who ever heard of such an idea?"
10135{_}1126{_}"A little _too_ self-evident."
10135{_}1127{_}"Ha!
10135{_}1128{_}ha!
10135{_}1129{_}ha!--ha!
10135{_}1130{_}ha!
10135{_}1131{_}ha!--ho!
10135{_}1132{_}ho!
10135{_}1133{_}ho!"
10135{_}1134{_}roared our visitor, profoundly amused, "Oh, Dupin, you will be the death of me yet!"
10135{_}1135{_}"And what, after all, _is_ the matter on hand?"
10135{_}1136{_}I asked.
10135{_}1137{_}"Why, I will tell you," replied the Prefect, as he gave a long, steady, and contemplative puff, and settled himself in his chair.
10135{_}1138{_}"I will tell you in a few words; but, before I begin, let me caution you that this is an affair demanding the greatest secrecy, and that I should most probably lose the position I now hold were it known that I confided it to any one."
10135{_}1139{_}"Proceed," said I.
10135{_}1140{_}"Or not," said Dupin.
10135{_}1141{_}"Well, then; I have received personal information, from a very high quarter, that a certain document of the last importance has been purloined from the royal apartments.
10135{_}1142{_}The individual who purloined it is known; this beyond a doubt; he was seen to take it.
10135{_}1143{_}It is known, also, that it still remains in his possession."
10135{_}1144{_}"How is this known?"
10135{_}1145{_}asked Dupin.
10135{_}1146{_}"It is clearly inferred," replied the Prefect, "from the nature of the document, and from the non-appearance of certain results which would at once arise from its passing _out_ of the robber's possession--that is to say, from his employing it as he must design in the end to employ it."
10135{_}1147{_}"Be a little more explicit," I said.
10135{_}1148{_}"Well, I may venture so far as to say that the paper gives its holder a certain power in a certain quarter where such power is immensely valuable."
10135{_}1149{_}The Prefect was fond of the cant of diplomacy.
10135{_}1150{_}"Still I do not quite understand," said Dupin.
10135{_}1151{_}"No?
10135{_}1152{_}Well, the disclosure of the document to a third person, who shall be nameless, would bring in question the honor of a personage of the most exalted station, and this fact gives the holder of the document an ascendancy over the illustrious personage whose honor and peace are so jeopardized."
10135{_}1153{_}"But this ascendancy," I interposed, "would depend upon the robber's knowledge of the loser's knowledge of the robber.
10135{_}1154{_}Who would dare--"  "The thief," said G----, "is the Minister D----, who dares all things, those unbecoming as well as those becoming a man.
10135{_}1155{_}The method of the theft was not less ingenious than bold.
10135{_}1156{_}The document in question--a letter, to be frank--had been received by the personage robbed while alone in the royal _boudoir_.
10135{_}1157{_}During its perusal she was suddenly interrupted by the entrance of the other exalted personage from whom especially it was her wish to conceal it.
10135{_}1158{_}After a hurried and vain endeavor to thrust it in a drawer, she was forced to place it, open as it was, upon a table.
10135{_}1159{_}The address, however, was uppermost, and, the contents thus unexposed, the letter escaped notice.
10135{_}1160{_}At this juncture enters the Minister D----.
10135{_}1161{_}His lynx eye immediately perceives the paper, recognizes the handwriting of the address, observes the confusion of the personage addressed, and fathoms her secret.
10135{_}1162{_}After some business transactions, hurried through in his ordinary manner, he produces a letter somewhat similar to the one in question, opens it, pretends to read it, and then places it in close juxtaposition to the other.
10135{_}1163{_}Again he converses, for some fifteen minutes, upon the public affairs.
10135{_}1164{_}At length, in taking leave, he takes also from the table the letter to which he had no claim.
10135{_}1165{_}Its rightful owner saw, but of course, dared not call attention to the act, in the presence of the third personage who stood at her elbow.
10135{_}1166{_}The Minister decamped, leaving his own letter--one of no importance--upon the table."
10135{_}1167{_}"Here, then," said Dupin to me, "you have precisely what you demand to make the ascendancy complete--the robber's knowledge of the loser's knowledge of the robber."
10135{_}1168{_}"Yes," replied the Prefect, "and the power thus attained has, for some months past, been wielded, for political purposes, to a very dangerous extent.
10135{_}1169{_}The personage robbed is more thoroughly convinced, every day, of the necessity of reclaiming her letter.
10135{_}1170{_}But this, of course, cannot be done openly.
10135{_}1171{_}In fine, driven to despair, she has committed the matter to me."
10135{_}1172{_}"Than whom," said Dupin, amid a perfect whirlwind of smoke, "no more sagacious agent could, I suppose, be desired, or even imagined."
10135{_}1173{_}"You flatter me," replied the Prefect; "but it is possible that some such opinion may have been entertained."
10135{_}1174{_}"It is clear," said I, "as you observe, that the letter is still in the possession of the Minister; since it is this possession, and not any employment of the letter, which bestows the power.
10135{_}1175{_}With the employment the power departs."
10135{_}1176{_}"True," said G----; "and upon this conviction I proceeded.
10135{_}1177{_}My first care was to make thorough search of the Minister's hotel; and here my chief embarrassment lay in the necessity of searching without his knowledge.
10135{_}1178{_}Beyond all things, I have been warned of the danger which would result from giving him reason to suspect our design."
10135{_}1179{_}"But," said I, "you are quite _au fait_ in these investigations.
10135{_}1180{_}The Parisian police have done this thing often before."
10135{_}1181{_}"Oh yes, and for this reason I did not despair.
10135{_}1182{_}The habits of the Minister gave me, too, a great advantage.
10135{_}1183{_}He is frequently absent from home all night.
10135{_}1184{_}His servants are by no means numerous.
10135{_}1185{_}They sleep at a distance from their master's apartment, and, being chiefly Neapolitans, are readily made drunk.
10135{_}1186{_}I have keys, as you know, with which I can open any chamber or cabinet in Paris.
10135{_}1187{_}For three months a night has not passed during the greater part of which I have not been engaged, personally, in ransacking the D---- Hotel.
10135{_}1188{_}My honor is interested, and, to mention a great secret, the reward is enormous.
10135{_}1189{_}So I did not abandon the search until I had become fully satisfied that the thief is a more astute man than myself.
10135{_}1190{_}I fancy that I have investigated every nook and corner of the premises in which it is possible that the paper can be concealed."
10135{_}1191{_}"But is it not possible," I suggested, "that although the letter may be in the possession of the Minister, as it unquestionably is, he may have concealed it elsewhere than upon his own premises?"
10135{_}1192{_}"This is barely possible," said Dupin.
10135{_}1193{_}"The present peculiar condition of affairs at court, and especially of those intrigues in which D---- is known to be involved, would render the instant availability of the document--its susceptibility of being produced at a moment's notice--a point of nearly equal importance with its possession."
10135{_}1194{_}"Its susceptibility of being produced?"
10135{_}1195{_}said I.
10135{_}1196{_}"That is to say, of being _destroyed_," said Dupin.
10135{_}1197{_}"True," I observed; "the paper is clearly, then, upon the premises.
10135{_}1198{_}As for its being upon the person of the Minister, we may consider that as out of the question."
10135{_}1199{_}"Entirely," said the Prefect.
10135{_}1200{_}"He has been twice waylaid, as if by footpads, and his person rigidly searched under my own inspection."
10135{_}1201{_}"You might have spared yourself this trouble," said Dupin.
10135{_}1202{_}"D----, I presume, is not altogether a fool; and, if not, must have anticipated these waylayings, as a matter of course."
10135{_}1203{_}"Not _altogether_ a fool," said G----; "but, then, he is a poet, which I take to be only one remove from a fool."
10135{_}1204{_}"True," said Dupin, after a long and thoughtful whiff from his meerschaum, "although I have been guilty of certain doggrel myself."
10135{_}1205{_}"Suppose you detail," said I, "the particulars of your search."
10135{_}1206{_}"Why, the fact is, we took our time, and we searched _everywhere_.
10135{_}1207{_}I have had long experience in these affairs.
10135{_}1208{_}I took the entire building, room by room, devoting the nights of a whole week to each.
10135{_}1209{_}We examined, first, the furniture of each apartment.
10135{_}1210{_}We opened every possible drawer; and I presume you know that, to a properly trained police-agent, such a thing as a '_secret_' drawer is impossible.
10135{_}1211{_}Any man is a dolt who permits a 'secret' drawer to escape him in a search of this kind.
10135{_}1212{_}The thing is so plain.
10135{_}1213{_}There is a certain amount of bulk--of space--to be accounted for in every cabinet.
10135{_}1214{_}Then we have accurate rules.
10135{_}1215{_}The fiftieth part of a line could not escape us.
10135{_}1216{_}After the cabinets we took the chairs.
10135{_}1217{_}The cushions we probed with the fine long needles you have seen me employ.
10135{_}1218{_}From the tables we removed the tops."
10135{_}1219{_}"Why so?"
10135{_}1220{_}"Sometimes the top of a table, or other similarly arranged piece of furniture, is removed by the person wishing to conceal an article; then the leg is excavated, the article deposited within the cavity, and the top replaced.
10135{_}1221{_}The bottoms and tops of bedposts are employed in the same way."
10135{_}1222{_}"But could not the cavity be detected by sounding?"
10135{_}1223{_}I asked.
10135{_}1224{_}"By no means, if, when the article is deposited, a sufficient wadding of cotton be placed around it.
10135{_}1225{_}Besides, in our case, we were obliged to proceed without noise."
10135{_}1226{_}"But you could not have removed--you could not have taken to pieces _all_ articles of furniture in which it would have been possible to make a deposit in the manner you mention.
10135{_}1227{_}A letter may be compressed into a thin spiral roll, not differing much in shape or bulk from a large knitting-needle, and in this form it might be inserted into the rung of a chair, for example.
10135{_}1228{_}You did not take to pieces all the chairs?"
10135{_}1229{_}"Certainly not; but we did better--we examined the rungs of every chair in the hotel, and, indeed, the jointings of every description of furniture, by the aid of a most powerful microscope.
10135{_}1230{_}Had there been any traces of recent disturbance we should not have failed to detect it instantly.
10135{_}1231{_}A single grain of gimlet-dust, for example, would have been as obvious as an apple.
10135{_}1232{_}Any disorder in the gluing--any unusual gaping in the joints--would have sufficed to insure detection."
10135{_}1233{_}"I presume you looked to the mirrors, between the boards and the plates, and you probed the beds and the bedclothes, as well as the curtains and carpets."
10135{_}1234{_}"That, of course; and when we had absolutely completed every particle of the furniture in this way, then we examined the house itself.
10135{_}1235{_}We divided its entire surface into compartments, which we numbered, so that none might be missed; then we scrutinized each individual square inch throughout the premises, including the two houses immediately adjoining, with the microscope, as before."
10135{_}1236{_}"The two houses adjoining!"
10135{_}1237{_}I exclaimed.
10135{_}1238{_}"You must have had a great deal of trouble."
10135{_}1239{_}"We had; but the reward offered is prodigious."
10135{_}1240{_}"You include the _grounds_ about the houses?"
10135{_}1241{_}"All the grounds are paved with brick.
10135{_}1242{_}They gave us comparatively little trouble.
10135{_}1243{_}We examined the moss between the bricks, and found it undisturbed."
10135{_}1244{_}"You looked among D----'s papers, of course, and into the books of the library?"
10135{_}1245{_}"Certainly; we opened every package and parcel; we not only opened every book, but we turned over every leaf in each volume, not contenting ourselves with a mere shake, according to the fashion of some of our police officers.
10135{_}1246{_}We also measured the thickness of every book-_cover_, with the most accurate admeasurement, and applied to each the most jealous scrutiny of the microscope.
10135{_}1247{_}Had any of the bindings been recently meddled with, it would have been utterly impossible that the fact should have escaped observation.
10135{_}1248{_}Some five or six volumes, just from the hands of the binder, we carefully probed, longitudinally, with the needles."
10135{_}1249{_}"You explored the floors beneath the carpets?"
10135{_}1250{_}"Beyond doubt.
10135{_}1251{_}We removed every carpet, and examined the boards with the microscope."
10135{_}1252{_}"And the paper on the walls?"
10135{_}1253{_}"Yes."
10135{_}1254{_}"You looked into the cellars?"
10135{_}1255{_}"We did."
10135{_}1256{_}"Then," I said, "you have been making a miscalculation, and the letter is _not_ upon the premises, as you suppose."
10135{_}1257{_}"I fear you are right there," said the Prefect.
10135{_}1258{_}"And now, Dupin, what would you advise me to do?"
10135{_}1259{_}"To make a thorough research of the premises."
10135{_}1260{_}"That is absolutely needless," replied G----.
10135{_}1261{_}"I am not more sure that I breathe than I am that the letter is not at the hotel."
10135{_}1262{_}"I have no better advice to give you," said Dupin.
10135{_}1263{_}"You have, of course, an accurate description of the letter?"
10135{_}1264{_}"Oh yes!"
10135{_}1265{_}And here the Prefect, producing a memorandum book, proceeded to read aloud a minute account of the internal, and especially of the external, appearance of the missing document.
10135{_}1266{_}Soon after finishing the perusal of this description, he took his departure, more entirely depressed in spirits than I had ever known the good gentleman before.
10135{_}1267{_}In about a month afterward he paid us another visit, and found us occupied very nearly as before.
10135{_}1268{_}He took a pipe and a chair, and entered into some ordinary conversation.
10135{_}1269{_}At length I said:  "Well, but, G----, what of the purloined letter?
10135{_}1270{_}I presume you have at last made up your mind that there is no such thing as overreaching the Minister?"
10135{_}1271{_}"Confound him, say I--yes; I made the re-examination, however, as Dupin suggested--but it was all labor lost, as I knew it would be."
10135{_}1272{_}"How much was the reward offered, did you say?"
10135{_}1273{_}asked Dupin.
10135{_}1274{_}"Why, a very great deal--a _very_ liberal reward--I don't like to say how much, precisely; but one thing I _will_ say, that I wouldn't mind giving my individual check for fifty thousand francs to any one who could obtain me that letter.
10135{_}1275{_}The fact is, it is becoming of more and more importance every day; and the reward has been lately doubled.
10135{_}1276{_}If it were trebled, however, I could do no more than I have done."
10135{_}1277{_}"Why, yes," said Dupin, drawling, between the whiffs of his meerschaum, "I really--think, G----, you have not exerted yourself--to the utmost in this matter.
10135{_}1278{_}You might--do a little more, I think; eh?"
10135{_}1279{_}"How?--in what way?"
10135{_}1280{_}"Why"--puff, puff--"you might"--puff, puff--"employ counsel in the matter, eh"--puff, puff, puff.
10135{_}1281{_}"Do you remember the story they tell of Abernethy?"
10135{_}1282{_}"No; hang Abernethy!"
10135{_}1283{_}"To be sure!
10135{_}1284{_}Hang him and welcome.
10135{_}1285{_}But, once upon a time, a certain miser conceived the design of spunging upon this Abernethy for a medical opinion.
10135{_}1286{_}Getting up, for this purpose, an ordinary conversation in a private company, he insinuated his case to the physician as that of an imaginary individual."
10135{_}1287{_}"'We will suppose,' said the miser, 'that his symptoms are such and such; now, doctor, what would _you_ have directed him to take?'
10135{_}1288{_}"'Take!'
10135{_}1289{_}said Abernethy.
10135{_}1290{_}'Why, take _advice_, to be sure.'"
10135{_}1291{_}"But," said the Prefect, a little discomposed, "_I_ am _perfectly_ willing to take advice, and to pay for it.
10135{_}1292{_}I would _really_ give fifty thousand francs to any one who would aid me in the matter."
10135{_}1293{_}"In that case," replied Dupin, opening a drawer, and producing a check-book, "you may as well fill me up a check for the amount mentioned.
10135{_}1294{_}When you have signed it, I will hand you the letter."
10135{_}1295{_}I was astounded.
10135{_}1296{_}The Prefect appeared absolutely thunder-stricken.
10135{_}1297{_}For some minutes he remained speechless and motionless, looking incredulously at my friend with open mouth, and eyes that seemed starting from their sockets; then apparently recovering himself in some measure, he seized a pen, and after several pauses and vacant stares, finally filled up and signed a check for fifty thousand francs, and handed it across the table to Dupin.
10135{_}1298{_}The latter examined it carefully and deposited it in his pocket-book; then, unlocking an _escritoire_, took thence a letter and gave it to the Prefect.
10135{_}1299{_}This functionary grasped it in a perfect agony of joy, opened it with a trembling hand, cast a rapid glance at its contents, and then, scrambling and struggling to the door, rushed at length unceremoniously from the room and from the house, without having uttered a syllable since Dupin had requested him to fill up the check.
10135{_}1300{_}When he had gone, my friend entered into some explanation.
10135{_}1301{_}"The Parisian police," he said, "are exceedingly able in their way.
10135{_}1302{_}They are persevering, ingenious, cunning, and thoroughly versed in the knowledge which their duties seem chiefly to demand.
10135{_}1303{_}Thus, when G---- detailed to us his mode of searching the premises at the Hotel D----, I felt entire confidence in his having made a satisfactory investigation--so far as his labors extended."
10135{_}1304{_}"So far as his labors extended?"
10135{_}1305{_}said I.
10135{_}1306{_}"Yes," said Dupin.
10135{_}1307{_}"The measures adopted were not only the best of their kind, but carried out to absolute perfection.
10135{_}1308{_}Had the letter been deposited within the range of their search, these fellows would, beyond a question, have found it."
10135{_}1309{_}I merely laughed--but he seemed quite serious in all that he said.
10135{_}1310{_}"The measures, then," he continued, "were good in their kind, and well executed; their defect lay in their being inapplicable to the case and to the man.
10135{_}1311{_}A certain set of highly ingenious resources are, with the Prefect, a sort of Procrustean bed, to which he forcibly adapts his designs.
10135{_}1312{_}But he perpetually errs by being too deep or too shallow for the matter in hand, and many a school-boy is a better reasoner than he.
10135{_}1313{_}I knew one about eight years of age, whose success at guessing in the game of 'even and odd' attracted universal admiration.
10135{_}1314{_}This game is simple, and is played with marbles.
10135{_}1315{_}One player holds in his hand a number of these toys, and demands of another whether that number is even or odd.
10135{_}1316{_}If the guess is right, the guesser wins one; if wrong, he loses one.
10135{_}1317{_}The boy to whom I allude won all the marbles of the school.
10135{_}1318{_}Of course, he had some principle of guessing; and this lay in mere observation and admeasurement of the astuteness of his opponents.
10135{_}1319{_}For example, an arrant simpleton is his opponent, and, holding up his closed hand, asks, 'Are they even or odd?'
10135{_}1320{_}Our school-boy replies, 'Odd,' and loses; but upon the second trial he wins, for he then says to himself: 'The simpleton had them even upon the first trial, and his amount of cunning is just sufficient to make him have them odd upon the second; I will therefore guess odd'; he guesses odd, and wins.
10135{_}1321{_}Now, with a simpleton a degree above the first, he would have reasoned thus;  'This fellow finds that in the first instance I guessed odd, and, in the second, he will propose to himself, upon the first impulse, a simple variation from even to odd, as did the first simpleton; but then a second thought will suggest that this is too simple a variation, and finally he will decide upon putting it even as before.
10135{_}1322{_}I will therefore guess even'; he guesses even, and wins.
10135{_}1323{_}Now this mode of reasoning in the school-boy, whom his fellows termed 'Lucky,' what, in its last analysis, is it?"
10135{_}1324{_}"It is merely," I said, "an identification of the reasoner's intellect with that of his opponent."
10135{_}1325{_}"It is," said Dupin; "and, upon inquiring of the boy by what means he effected the _thorough_ identification in which his success consisted, I received answer as follows: 'When I wish to find out how wise, or how stupid, or how good, or how wicked, is any one, or what are his thoughts at the moment, I fashion the expression of my face, as accurately as possible, in accordance with the expression of his, and then wait to see what thoughts or sentiments arise in my mind or heart, as if to match or correspond with the expression.'
10135{_}1326{_}This response of the school-boy lies at the bottom of all the spurious profundity which has been attributed to Rochefoucault, to La Bougive, to Machiavelli, and to Campanella."
10135{_}1327{_}"And the identification," I said, "of the reasoner's intellect with that of his opponent, depends, if I understand you aright, upon the accuracy with which the opponent's intellect is admeasured."
10135{_}1328{_}"For its practical value it depends upon this," replied Dupin; "and the Prefect and his cohort fail so frequently, first, by default of this identification, and, secondly, by ill-admeasurement, or rather through non-admeasurement, of the intellect with which they are engaged.
10135{_}1329{_}They consider only their _own_ ideas of ingenuity; and, in searching for anything hidden, advert only to the modes in which _they_ would have hidden it.
10135{_}1330{_}They are right in this much--that their own ingenuity is a faithful representative of that of _the mass_; but when the cunning of the individual felon is diverse in character from their own, the felon foils them of course.
10135{_}1331{_}This always happens when it is above their own, and very usually when it is below.
10135{_}1332{_}They have no variation of principle in their investigations; at best, when urged by some unusual emergency--by some extraordinary reward--they extend or exaggerate their old modes of _practice_, without touching their principles.
10135{_}1333{_}What, for example, in this case of D----, has been done to vary the principle of action?
10135{_}1334{_}What is all this boring, and probing, and sounding, and scrutinizing with the microscope, and dividing the surface of the building into registered square inches--what is it all but an exaggeration of the application of the one principle or set of principles of search, which are based upon the one set of notions retarding human ingenuity, to which the Prefect, in the long routine of his duty, has been accustomed?
10135{_}1335{_}Do you not see he had taken it for granted that _all_ men proceed to conceal a letter, not exactly in a gimlet-hole bored in a chair-leg, but, at least, in _some_ out-of-the-way hole or corner suggested by the same tenor of thought which would urge a man to secrete a letter in a gimlet-hole bored in a chair-leg?
10135{_}1336{_}And do you not see, also, that such _recherche_ nooks for concealment are adapted only for ordinary occasions, and would be adopted only by ordinary intellects; for, in all cases of concealment, a disposal of the article concealed--a disposal of it in this _recherche_ manner--is, in the very first instance, presumable and presumed; and thus its discovery depends, not at all upon the acumen, but altogether upon the mere care, patience, and determination of the seekers; and where the case is of importance--or, when the reward is of magnitude--the qualities in question have _never_ been known to fail.
10135{_}1337{_}You will now understand what I meant in suggesting that, had the purloined letter been hidden anywhere within the limits of the Prefect's examination--in other words, had the principle of its concealment been comprehended within the principles of the Prefect--its discovery would have been a matter altogether beyond question.
10135{_}1338{_}This functionary, however, has been thoroughly mystified; and the remote source of his defeat lies in the supposition that the Minister is a fool, because he has acquired renown as a poet.
10135{_}1339{_}All fools are poets; this the Prefect _feels_; and he is merely guilty of a _non distributio medii_ in thence inferring that all poets are fools.
10135{_}1340{_}I mean to say, that if the Minister had been no more than a mathematician, the Prefect would have been under no necessity of giving me this check.
10135{_}1341{_}I knew him, however, as both mathematician and poet, and my measures were adapted to his capacity, with reference to the circumstances by which he was surrounded.
10135{_}1342{_}I knew him as a courtier, too, and as a bold _intriguant_.
10135{_}1343{_}Such a man, I considered, could not fail to be aware of the ordinary political modes of action.
10135{_}1344{_}He could not have failed to anticipate--and events have proved that he did not fail to anticipate--the waylayings to which he was subjected.
10135{_}1345{_}He must have foreseen, I reflected, the secret investigations of his premises.
10135{_}1346{_}His frequent absences from home at night, which were hailed by the Prefect as certain aids to his success, I regarded only as _ruses_, to afford opportunity for thorough search to the police, and thus the sooner to impress them with the conviction to which G----, in fact, did finally arrive--the conviction that the letter was not upon the premises.
10135{_}1347{_}I felt, also, that the whole train of thought, which I was at some pains in detailing to you just now, concerning the invariable principle of political action in searches for articles concealed--I felt that this whole train of thought would necessarily pass through the mind of the Minister.
10135{_}1348{_}It would imperatively lead him to despise all the ordinary _nooks_ of concealment.
10135{_}1349{_}_He_ could not, I reflected, be so weak as not to see that the most intricate and remote recess of his hotel would be as open as his commonest closets to the eyes, to the probes, to the gimlets, and to the microscopes of the Prefect.
10135{_}1350{_}I saw, in fine, that he would be driven, as a matter of course, to _simplicity_, if not deliberately induced to it as a matter of choice.
10135{_}1351{_}You will remember, perhaps, how desperately the Prefect laughed when I suggested, upon our first interview, that it was just possible this mystery troubled him so much on account of its being so _very_ self-evident."
10135{_}1352{_}"Yes," said I, "I remember his merriment well.
10135{_}1353{_}I really thought he would have fallen into convulsions."
10135{_}1354{_}"The material world," continued Dupin, "abounds with very strict analogies to the immaterial; and thus some color of truth has been given to the rhetorical dogma that metaphor, or simile, may be made to strengthen an argument as well as to embellish a description.
10135{_}1355{_}The principle of the _vis inertiae_, for example, seems to be identical in physics and metaphysics.
10135{_}1356{_}It is not more true, in the former, that a large body is with more difficulty set in motion than a smaller one, and that its subsequent _momentum_ is commensurate with this difficulty, than it is, in the latter, that intellects of the vaster capacity, while more forcible, more constant, and more eventful in their movements than those of inferior grade, are yet the less readily moved, and more embarrassed, and full of hesitation in the first few steps of their progress.
10135{_}1357{_}Again: have you ever noticed which of the street signs, over the shop doors, are the most attractive of attention?"
10135{_}1358{_}"I have never given the matter a thought," I said.
10135{_}1359{_}"There is a game of puzzles," he resumed, "which is played upon a map.
10135{_}1360{_}One party playing requires another to find a given word--the name of town, river, state, or empire--any word, in short, upon the motley and perplexed surface of the chart.
10135{_}1361{_}A novice in the game generally seeks to embarrass his opponents by giving them the most minutely lettered names; but the adept selects such words as stretch, in large characters, from one end of the chart to the other.
10135{_}1362{_}These, like the over-largely lettered signs and placards of the street, escape observation by dint of being excessively obvious; and here the physical oversight is precisely analogous with the moral inapprehension by which the intellect suffers to pass unnoticed those considerations which are too obtrusively and too palpably self-evident.
10135{_}1363{_}But this is a point, it appears, somewhat above or beneath the understanding of the Prefect.
10135{_}1364{_}He never once thought it probable, or possible, that the Minister had deposited the letter immediately beneath the nose of the whole world, by way of best preventing any portion of that world from perceiving it.
10135{_}1365{_}"But the more I reflected upon the daring, dashing, and discriminating ingenuity of D----; upon the fact that the document must always have been _at hand_, if he intended to use it to good purpose; and upon the decisive evidence, obtained by the Prefect, that it was not hidden within the limits of that dignitary's ordinary search, the more satisfied I became that, to conceal this letter, the Minister had resorted to the comprehensive and sagacious expedient of not attempting to conceal it.
10135{_}1366{_}"Full of these ideas, I prepared myself with a pair of green spectacles, and called one fine morning, quite by accident, at the Ministerial hotel.
10135{_}1367{_}I found D---- at home, yawning, lounging, and dawdling, as usual, and pretending to be in the last extremity of _ennui_.
10135{_}1368{_}He is, perhaps, the most really energetic human being now alive--but that is only when nobody sees him.
10135{_}1369{_}"To be even with him, I complained of my weak eyes, and lamented the necessity of the spectacles, under cover of which I cautiously and thoroughly surveyed the whole apartment, while seemingly intent only upon the conversation of my host.
10135{_}1370{_}"I paid especial attention to a large writing-table near which he sat, and upon which lay confusedly some miscellaneous letters and other papers, with one or two musical instruments and a few books.
10135{_}1371{_}Here, however, after a long and very deliberate scrutiny, I saw nothing to excite particular suspicion.
10135{_}1372{_}"At length my eyes, in going the circuit of the room, fell upon a trumpery filigree card-rack of pasteboard that hung dangling by a dirty blue ribbon from a little brass knob just beneath the middle of the mantelpiece.
10135{_}1373{_}In this rack, which had three or four compartments, were five or six soiled cards and a solitary letter.
10135{_}1374{_}This last was much soiled and crumpled.
10135{_}1375{_}It was torn nearly in two, across the middle--as if a design, in the first instance, to tear it entirely up as worthless had been altered or stayed in the second.
10135{_}1376{_}It had a large black seal, bearing the D---- cipher _very_ conspicuously, and was addressed, in a diminutive female hand, to D----, the Minister, himself.
10135{_}1377{_}It was thrust carelessly, and even, as it seemed, contemptuously, into one of the uppermost divisions of the rack.
10135{_}1378{_}"No sooner had I glanced at this letter than I concluded it to be that of which I was in search.
10135{_}1379{_}To be sure, it was, to all appearance, radically different from the one of which the Prefect had read us so minute a description.
10135{_}1380{_}Here the seal was large and black, with the D---- cipher; there it was small and red, with the ducal arms of the S---- family.
10135{_}1381{_}Here the address, to the Minister, was diminutive and feminine; there the superscription, to a certain royal personage, was markedly bold and decided; the size alone formed a point of correspondence.
10135{_}1382{_}But, then, the _radicalness_ of these differences, which was excessive; the dirt; the soiled and torn condition of the paper, so inconsistent with the _true_ methodical habits of D----, and so consistent of a design to delude the beholder into an idea of the worthlessness of the document--these things, together with the hyperobtrusive situation of this document, full in the view of every visitor, and thus exactly in accordance with the conclusions to which I had previously arrived--these things, I say, were strongly corroborative of suspicion in one who came with the intention to suspect.
10135{_}1383{_}"I protracted my visit as long as possible, and, while I maintained a most animated discussion with the Minister upon a topic which I knew well had never failed to interest and excite him, I kept my attention riveted upon the letter.
10135{_}1384{_}In this examination I committed to memory its external appearance and arrangement in the rack, and also fell, at length, upon a discovery which set at rest whatever trivial doubt I might have entertained.
10135{_}1385{_}In scrutinizing the edges of the paper, I observed them to be more _chafed_ than seemed necessary.
10135{_}1386{_}They presented the _broken_ appearance which is manifested when a stiff paper, having been once folded and pressed with a folder, is refolded in a reversed direction, in the same creases or edges which formed the original fold.
10135{_}1387{_}This discovery was sufficient.
10135{_}1388{_}It was clear to me that the letter had been turned, as a glove, inside out, re-directed and re-sealed.
10135{_}1389{_}I bade the Minister good-morning, and took my departure at once, leaving a gold snuff-box upon the table.
10135{_}1390{_}"The next morning I called for the snuff-box, when we resumed, quite eagerly, the conversation of the preceding day.
10135{_}1391{_}While thus engaged, however, a loud report, as if of a pistol, was heard immediately beneath the windows of the hotel, and was succeeded by a series of fearful screams and the shoutings of a terrified mob.
10135{_}1392{_}D---- rushed to a casement, threw it open, and looked out.
10135{_}1393{_}In the mean time I stepped to the card-rack, took the letter, put it in my pocket, and replaced it by a _facsimile_ (so far as regards externals), which I had carefully prepared at my lodgings--imitating the D---- cipher, very readily, by means of a seal formed of bread.
10135{_}1394{_}"The disturbance in the street had been occasioned by the frantic behavior of a man with a musket.
10135{_}1395{_}He had fired it among a crowd of women and children.
10135{_}1396{_}It proved, however, to have been without ball, and the fellow was suffered to go his way as a lunatic or a drunkard.
10135{_}1397{_}When he had gone, D---- came from the window, whither I had followed him immediately upon securing the object in view.
10135{_}1398{_}Soon afterward I bade him farewell.
10135{_}1399{_}The pretended lunatic was a man in my own pay."
10135{_}1400{_}"But what purpose had you," I asked, "in replacing the letter by a _facsimile_?
10135{_}1401{_}Would it not have been better, at the first visit, to have seized it openly and departed?"
10135{_}1402{_}"D----," replied Dupin, "is a desperate man and a man of nerve.
10135{_}1403{_}His hotel, too, is not without attendants devoted to his interests.
10135{_}1404{_}Had I made the wild attempt you suggest, I might never have left the Ministerial presence alive.
10135{_}1405{_}The good people of Paris might have heard of me no more.
10135{_}1406{_}But I had an object apart from these considerations.
10135{_}1407{_}You know my political prepossessions.
10135{_}1408{_}In this matter I act as a partisan of the lady concerned.
10135{_}1409{_}For eighteen months the Minister has had her in his power.
10135{_}1410{_}She has now him in hers--since, being unaware that the letter is not in his possession, he will proceed with his exactions as if it was.
10135{_}1411{_}Thus will he inevitably commit himself, at once, to his political destruction.
10135{_}1412{_}His downfall, too, will not be more precipitate than awkward.
10135{_}1413{_}It is all very well to talk about the _facilis descensus Averni_; but in all kinds of climbing, as Catalani said of singing, it is far more easy to get up than to come down.
10135{_}1414{_}In the present instance I have no sympathy--at least no pity--for him who descends.
10135{_}1415{_}He is that _monstrum horrendum_, an unprincipled man of genius.
10135{_}1416{_}I confess, however, that I should like very well to know the precise character of his thoughts, when, being defied by her whom the Prefect terms 'a certain personage,' he is reduced to opening the letter I left for him in the card-rack."
10135{_}1417{_}"How?
10135{_}1418{_}Did you put anything particular in it?"
10135{_}1419{_}"Why--it did not seem altogether right to leave the interior blank--that would have been insulting.
10135{_}1420{_}D----, at Vienna once, did me an evil turn, which I told him, quite good-humoredly, that I should remember.
10135{_}1421{_}So, as I knew he would feel some curiosity in regard to the identity of the person who had outwitted him, I thought it a pity not to give him a clew.
10135{_}1422{_}He is well acquainted with my MS., and I just copied into the middle of the blank sheet the words:    "'----   ... Un dessein si funeste,   S'il n'est digne d'Atree, este digne de Thyeste.'
10135{_}1423{_}They are to be found in Crebillon's _Atree_."
10135{_}1424{_}RAB AND HIS FRIENDS  _Dr.
10135{_}1425{_}John Brown_ (1810-1882)   Four-and-thirty years ago, Bob Ainslie and I were coming up Infirmary Street from the High School, our heads together, and our arms intertwisted as only lovers and boys know how or why.
10135{_}1426{_}When we got to the top of the street and turned north we espied a crowd at the Tron Church.
10135{_}1427{_}"A dog-fight!"
10135{_}1428{_}shouted Bob, and was off; and so was I, both of us all but praying that it might not be over before we got up!
10135{_}1429{_}And is not this boy-nature, and human nature, too?
10135{_}1430{_}And don't we all wish a house on fire not to be out before we see it?
10135{_}1431{_}Dogs like fighting; old Isaac says they "delight" in it, and for the best of all reasons; and boys are not cruel because they like to see the fight.
10135{_}1432{_}They see three of the great cardinal virtues of dog or man--courage, endurance, and skill--in intense action.
10135{_}1433{_}This is very different from a love of making dogs fight, and aggravating and making gain by their pluck.
10135{_}1434{_}A boy--be he ever so fond himself of fighting, if he be a good boy, hates and despises all this, but he would have run off with Bob and me fast enough; it is a natural, and a not wicked, interest that all boys and men have in witnessing intense energy in action.
10135{_}1435{_}Does any curious and finely ignorant woman wish to know how Bob's eye at a glance announced a dog-fight to his brain?
10135{_}1436{_}He did not--he could not--see the dogs fighting; it was a flash of an inference, a rapid induction.
10135{_}1437{_}The crowd round a couple of dogs fighting is a crowd masculine mainly, with an occasional active, compassionate woman fluttering wildly round the outside and using her tongue and her hands freely upon the men, as so many "brutes"; it is a crowd annular, compact, and mobile; a crowd centripetal, having its eyes and its heads all bent downward and inward to one common focus.
10135{_}1438{_}Well, Bob and I are up, and find it is not over; a small thoroughbred, white bull-terrier is busy throttling a large shepherd's dog, unaccustomed to war but not to be trifled with.
10135{_}1439{_}They are hard at it; the scientific little fellow doing his work in great style, his pastoral enemy fighting wildly, but with the sharpest of teeth and a great courage.
10135{_}1440{_}Science and breeding, however, soon had their own; the Game Chicken, as the premature Bob called him, working his way up, took his final grip of poor Yarrow's throat--and he lay gasping and done for.
10135{_}1441{_}His master, a brown, handsome, big, young shepherd from Tweedsmuir, would have liked to have knocked down any man, would "drink up Esil, or eat a crocodile," for that part, if he had a chance; it was no use kicking the little dog; that would only make him hold the closer.
10135{_}1442{_}Many were the means shouted out in mouthfuls of the best possible ways of ending it.
10135{_}1443{_}"Water!"
10135{_}1444{_}but there was none near, and many cried for it who might have got it from the well at Blackfriar's Wynd.
10135{_}1445{_}"Bite the tail!"
10135{_}1446{_}and a large, vague, benevolent, middle-aged man, more desirous than wise, with some struggle got the bushy end of Yarrow's tail into his ample mouth and bit it with all his might.
10135{_}1447{_}This was more than enough for the much-enduring, much-perspiring shepherd, who, with a gleam of joy over his broad visage, delivered a terrific facer upon our large, vague, benevolent, middle-aged friend, who went down like a shot.
10135{_}1448{_}Still the Chicken holds; death not far off.
10135{_}1449{_}"Snuff!
10135{_}1450{_}a pinch of snuff!"
10135{_}1451{_}observed a calm, highly dressed young buck with an eye-glass in his eye.
10135{_}1452{_}"Snuff, indeed!"
10135{_}1453{_}growled the angry crowd, affronted and glaring.
10135{_}1454{_}"Snuff!
10135{_}1455{_}a pinch of snuff!"
10135{_}1456{_}again observes the buck, but with more urgency; whereon were produced several open boxes, and from a mull which may have been at Culloden he took a pinch, knelt down, and presented it to the nose of the Chicken, The laws of physiology and of snuff take their course; the Chicken sneezes, and Yarrow is free!
10135{_}1457{_}The young pastoral giant stalks off with Yarrow in his arms--comforting him.
10135{_}1458{_}But the bull-terrier's blood is up, and his soul unsatisfied; he grips the first dog he meets, and, discovering she is not a dog, in Homeric phrase, he makes a brief sort of _amende_ and is off.
10135{_}1459{_}The boys, with Bob and me at their head, are after him: down Niddry Street he goes, bent on mischief; up the Cowgate like an arrow--Bob and I, and our small men; panting behind.
10135{_}1460{_}There, under the single arch of the South Bridge, is a huge mastiff, sauntering down the middle of the causeway, as if with his hands in his pockets; he is old, brindled, as big as a little Highland bull, and has the Shakespearean dewlaps shaking as he goes.
10135{_}1461{_}The Chicken makes straight at him, and fastens on his throat.
10135{_}1462{_}To our astonishment, the great creature does nothing but stand still, hold himself up, and roar--yes, roar, a long, serious, remonstrative roar.
10135{_}1463{_}How is this?
10135{_}1464{_}Bob and I are up to them.
10135{_}1465{_}_He is muzzled_!
10135{_}1466{_}The bailies had proclaimed a general muzzling, and his master, studying strength and economy mainly, had encompassed his huge jaws in a home-made apparatus constructed out of the leather of some ancient _breechin_.
10135{_}1467{_}His mouth was open as far as it could; his lips curled up in rage--a sort of terrible grin; his teeth gleaming, ready, from out the darkness; the strap across his mouth tense as a bowstring; his whole frame stiff with indignation and surprise; his roar asking us all round, "Did you ever see the like of this?"
10135{_}1468{_}He looked a statue of anger and astonishment done in Aberdeen granite.
10135{_}1469{_}We soon had a crowd; the Chicken held on.
10135{_}1470{_}"A knife!"
10135{_}1471{_}cried Bob; and a cobbler gave him his knife; you know the kind of knife, worn obliquely to a point and always keen.
10135{_}1472{_}I put its edge to the tense leather; it ran before it; and then!--one sudden jerk of that enormous head, a sort of dirty mist about his mouth, no noise, and the bright and fierce little fellow is dropped, limp and dead.
10135{_}1473{_}A solemn pause; this was more than any of us had bargained for.
10135{_}1474{_}I turned the little fellow over, and saw he was quite dead: the mastiff had taken him by the small of the back like a rat and broken it.
10135{_}1475{_}He looked down at his victim appeased, ashamed, and amazed; sniffed him all over, stared at him, and, taking a sudden thought, turned round and trotted off.
10135{_}1476{_}Bob took the dead dog up, and said, "John, we'll bury him after tea."
10135{_}1477{_}"Yes," said I, and was off after the mastiff.
10135{_}1478{_}He made up the Cowgate at a rapid swing; he had forgotten some engagement.
10135{_}1479{_}He turned up the Candlemaker Row, and stopped at the Harrow Inn.
10135{_}1480{_}There was a carrier's cart ready to start, and a keen, thin, impatient, black-a-vised little man, his hand at his gray horse's head, looking about angrily for something.
10135{_}1481{_}"Rab, ye thief!"
10135{_}1482{_}said he, aiming a kick at my great friend, who drew cringing up, and, avoiding the heavy shoe with more agility than dignity and watching his master's eye?
10135{_}1483{_}slunk dismayed under the cart--his ears down, and as much as he had of tail down, too.
10135{_}1484{_}What a man this must be--thought I--to whom my tremendous hero turns tail!
10135{_}1485{_}The carrier saw the muzzle hanging, cut and useless, from his neck, and I eagerly told him the story, which Bob and I always thought, and still think, Homer, or King David, or Sir Walter alone were worthy to rehearse.
10135{_}1486{_}The severe little man was mitigated, and condescended to say, "Rab, ma man--puir Rabbie," whereupon the stump of a tail rose up, the ears were cocked, the eyes filled and were comforted; the two friends were reconciled.
10135{_}1487{_}"Hupp!"
10135{_}1488{_}and a stroke of the whip were given to Jess, and off went the three.
10135{_}1489{_}Bob and I buried the Game Chicken that night (we had not much of a tea) in the back-green of his house, in Melville Street, No.
10135{_}1490{_}17, with considerable gravity and silence; and being at the time in the Iliad, and, like all boys, Trojans, we of course called him Hector.
10135{_}1491{_}Six years have passed--a long time for a boy and a dog; Bob Ainslie is off to the wars; I am a medical student, and clerk at Minto House Hospital.
10135{_}1492{_}Rab I saw almost every week, on the Wednesday, and we had much pleasant intimacy.
10135{_}1493{_}I found the way to his heart by frequent scratching of his huge head and an occasional bone.
10135{_}1494{_}When I did not notice him he would plant himself straight before me and stand wagging that bud of a tail, and looking up, with his head a little to the one side.
10135{_}1495{_}His master I occasionally saw; he used to call me "Maister John," but was laconic as any Spartan.
10135{_}1496{_}One fine October afternoon I was leaving the hospital, when I saw the large gate open, and in walked Rab, with that great and easy saunter of his.
10135{_}1497{_}He looked as if taking possession of the place, like the Duke of Wellington entering a subdued city, satiated with victory and peace.
10135{_}1498{_}After him came Jess, now white from age, with her cart; and in it a woman carefully wrapped up--the carrier leading the horse anxiously and looking back.
10135{_}1499{_}When he saw me, James (for his name was James Noble) made a curt and grotesque "boo," and said, "Maister John, this is the mistress; she's got a trouble in her breest--some kind o' an income, we're thinkin'."
10135{_}1500{_}By this time I saw the woman's face; she was sitting on a sack filled with straw, with her husband's plaid round her, and his big-coat, with its large, white metal buttons, over her feet.
10135{_}1501{_}I never saw a more unforgettable face--pale, serious, _lonely_, delicate, sweet, without being at all what we call fine.
10135{_}1502{_}She looked sixty, and had on a mutch, white as snow, with its black ribbon; her silvery, smooth hair setting off her dark-gray eyes--eyes such as one sees only twice or thrice in a lifetime, full of suffering, full also of the overcoming of it; her eyebrows black and delicate, and her mouth firm, patient, and contented, which few mouths ever are.
10135{_}1503{_}As I have said, I never saw a more beautiful countenance, or one more subdued to settled quiet.
10135{_}1504{_}"Ailie," said James, "this is Maister John, the young doctor; Rab's friend, ye ken.
10135{_}1505{_}We often speak aboot you, doctor."
10135{_}1506{_}She smiled and made a movement, but said nothing, and prepared to come down, putting her plaid aside and rising.
10135{_}1507{_}Had Solomon, in all his glory, been handing down the Queen of Sheba at his palace gate, he could not have done it more daintily, more tenderly, more like a gentleman than James, the Howland carrier, when he lifted down Ailie, his wife.
10135{_}1508{_}The contrast of his small, swarthy, weather-beaten, keen, worldly face to hers--pale, subdued, and beautiful--was something wonderful.
10135{_}1509{_}Rab looked on concerned and puzzled, but ready for anything that might turn up, were it to strangle the nurse, the porter, or even me.
10135{_}1510{_}Ailie and he seemed great friends.
10135{_}1511{_}"As I was sayin', she's got a kind o' trouble in her breest, doctor; wull ye tak' a look at it?"
10135{_}1512{_}We walked into the consulting-room, all four; Rab, grim and comic, willing to be happy and confidential if cause should be shown, willing also to be the reverse on the same terms.
10135{_}1513{_}Ailie sat down, undid her open gown and her lawn handkerchief round her neck, and, without a word, showed me her right breast.
10135{_}1514{_}I looked at it and examined it carefully, she and James watching me, and Rab eying all three.
10135{_}1515{_}What could I say?
10135{_}1516{_}There it was, that had once been so soft, so shapely, so white, so gracious and bountiful, so "full of all blessed condition," hard as a stone, a centre of horrid pain, making that pale face, with its gray, lucid, reasonable eyes, and its sweet, resolved mouth, express the full measure of suffering overcome.
10135{_}1517{_}Why was that gentle, modest, sweet woman, clean and lovable, condemned by God to bear such a burden?
10135{_}1518{_}I got her away to bed.
10135{_}1519{_}"May Rab and me bide?"
10135{_}1520{_}said James.
10135{_}1521{_}"_You_ may; and Rab, if he will behave himself."
10135{_}1522{_}"I'se warrant he's do that, doctor."
10135{_}1523{_}And in slunk the faithful beast.
10135{_}1524{_}There are no such dogs now.
10135{_}1525{_}He belonged to a lost tribe.
10135{_}1526{_}As I have said, he was brindled, and gray like Rubislaw granite; his hair short, hard, and close, like a lion's; his body thick-set, like a little bull--a sort of compressed Hercules of a dog.
10135{_}1527{_}He must have been ninety pounds' weight, at the least; he had a large, blunt head; his muzzle black as night; his mouth blacker than any night; a tooth or two--being all he had--gleaming out of his jaws of darkness.
10135{_}1528{_}His head was scarred with the records of old wounds, a sort of series of fields of battles all over it; one eye out, one ear cropped as close as was Archbishop Leighton's father's; the remaining eye had the power of two; and above it, and in constant communication with it, was a tattered rag of an ear, which was forever unfurling itself, like an old flag; and then that bud of a tail, about one inch long, if it could in any sense be said to be long, being as broad as long--the mobility, the instantaneousness of that bud were very funny and surprising, and its expressive twinklings and winkings, the intercommunications between the eye, the ear, and it, were of the oddest and swiftest.
10135{_}1529{_}Rab had the dignity and simplicity of great size; and, having fought his way all along the road to absolute supremacy, he was as mighty in his own line as Julius Caesar or the Duke of Wellington, and had the gravity of all great fighters.
10135{_}1530{_}You must have often observed the likeness of certain men to certain animals, and of certain dogs to men.
10135{_}1531{_}Now, I never looked at Rab without thinking of the great Baptist preacher, Andrew Fuller.
10135{_}1532{_}The same large, heavy, menacing, combative, sombre, honest countenance, the same deep, inevitable eye; the same look, as of thunder asleep, but ready--neither a dog nor a man to be trifled with.
10135{_}1533{_}Next day my master, the surgeon, examined Ailie.
10135{_}1534{_}There could be no doubt it must kill her, and soon.
10135{_}1535{_}If it could be removed--it might never return--it would give her speedy relief--she should have it done.
10135{_}1536{_}She curtsied, looked at James, and said, "When?"
10135{_}1537{_}"To-morrow," said the kind surgeon--a man of few words.
10135{_}1538{_}She and James and Rab and I retired.
10135{_}1539{_}I noticed that he and she spoke little, but seemed to anticipate everything in each other.
10135{_}1540{_}The following day, at noon, the students came in, hurrying up the great stair.
10135{_}1541{_}At the first landing-place, on a small, well-known blackboard, was a bit of paper fastened by wafers, and many remains of old wafers beside it.
10135{_}1542{_}On the paper were the words:  "An operation to-day.--J.B., _Clerk_."
10135{_}1543{_}Up ran the youths, eager to secure good places; in they crowded, full of interest and talk.
10135{_}1544{_}"What's the case?"
10135{_}1545{_}"Which side is it?"
10135{_}1546{_}Don't think them heartless; they are neither better nor worse than you or I; they get over their professional horrors, and into their proper work; and in them pity, as an _emotion_, ending in itself or at best in tears and a long-drawn breath, lessens, while pity, as a _motive_, is quickened, and gains power and purpose.
10135{_}1547{_}It is well for poor human nature that it is so.
10135{_}1548{_}The operating-theatre is crowded; much talk and fun, and all the cordiality and stir of youth.
10135{_}1549{_}The surgeon with his staff of assistants is there.
10135{_}1550{_}In comes Ailie; one look at her quiets and abates the eager students.
10135{_}1551{_}That beautiful old woman is too much for them; they sit down, and are dumb, and gaze at her.
10135{_}1552{_}These rough boys feel the power of her presence.
10135{_}1553{_}She walks in quietly, but without haste; dressed in her mutch, her neckerchief, her white dimity short-gown, her black bombazeen petticoat, showing her white worsted stockings and her carpet shoes.
10135{_}1554{_}Behind her was James with Rab.
10135{_}1555{_}James sat down in the distance, and took that huge and noble head between his knees.
10135{_}1556{_}Rab looked perplexed and dangerous--forever cocking his ear and dropping it as fast.
10135{_}1557{_}Ailie stepped up on a seat, and laid herself on the table, as her friend the surgeon told her; arranged herself, gave a rapid look at James, shut her eyes, rested herself on me, and took my hand.
10135{_}1558{_}The operation was at once begun; it was necessarily slow; and chloroform--one of God's best gifts to his suffering children--was then unknown.
10135{_}1559{_}The surgeon did his work.
10135{_}1560{_}The pale face showed its pain, but was still and silent.
10135{_}1561{_}Rab's soul was working within him; he saw something strange was going on, blood flowing from his mistress, and she suffering; his ragged ear was up and importunate; he growled and gave now and then a sharp, impatient yelp; he would have liked to have done something to that man.
10135{_}1562{_}But James had him firm, and gave him a _glower_ from time to time, and an intimation of a possible kick; all the better for James--it kept his eye and his mind off Ailie.
10135{_}1563{_}It is over; she is dressed, steps gently and decently down from the table, looks for James; then turning to the surgeon and the students, she curtsies, and in a low, clear voice, begs their pardon if she has behaved ill.
10135{_}1564{_}The students--all of us--wept like children; the surgeon wrapped her up carefully, and, resting on James and me, Ailie went to her room, and Rab followed.
10135{_}1565{_}We put her to bed.
10135{_}1566{_}James took off his heavy shoes, crammed with tackets, heel-capped and toe-capped, and put them carefully under the table, saying: "Maister John, I'm for nane o' yer strynge nurse bodies for Ailie.
10135{_}1567{_}I'll be her nurse, and I'll gang aboot on my stockin' soles as canny as pussy."
10135{_}1568{_}And so he did; and handy and clever, and swift and tender as any woman was that horny-handed, snell, peremptory little man.
10135{_}1569{_}Everything she got he gave her; he seldom slept; and often I saw his small, shrewd eyes out of the darkness, fixed on her.
10135{_}1570{_}As before, they spoke little.
10135{_}1571{_}Rab behaved well, never moving, showing us how meek and gentle he could be, and occasionally, in his sleep, letting us know that he was demolishing some adversary.
10135{_}1572{_}He took a walk with me every day, generally to the Candlemaker Row; but he was sombre and mild; declined doing battle, though some fit cases offered, and indeed submitted to sundry indignities; and was always very ready to turn, and came faster back, and trotted up the stair with much lightness, and went straight to that door.
10135{_}1573{_}Jess, the mare, had been sent, with her weather-beaten cart, to Howgate, and had doubtless her own dim and placid meditations and confusions on the absence of her master and Rab and her unnatural freedom from the road and her cart.
10135{_}1574{_}For some days Ailie did well.
10135{_}1575{_}The wound healed "by the first intention"; for as James said, "Oor Ailie's skin's ower clean to beil."
10135{_}1576{_}The students came in quiet and anxious, and surrounded her bed.
10135{_}1577{_}She said she liked to see their young, honest faces.
10135{_}1578{_}The surgeon dressed her, and spoke to her in his own short, kind way, pitying her through his eyes, Rab and James outside the circle--Rab being now reconciled, and even cordial, and having made up his mind that as yet nobody required worrying, but, as you may suppose, _semper paratus_.
10135{_}1579{_}So far well; but, four days after the operation, my patient had a sudden and long shivering, a "groosin," as she called it.
10135{_}1580{_}I saw her soon after; her eyes were too bright, her cheek colored; she was restless, and ashamed of being so; the balance was lost; mischief had begun.
10135{_}1581{_}On looking at the wound, a blush of red told the secret; her pulse was rapid, her breathing anxious and quick; she wasn't herself, as she said, and was vexed at her restlessness.
10135{_}1582{_}We tried what we could.
10135{_}1583{_}James did everything, was everywhere, never in the way, never out of it; Rab subsided under the table into a dark place, and was motionless, all but his eye, which followed every one.
10135{_}1584{_}Ailie got worse; began to wander in her mind, gently; was more demonstrative in her ways to James, rapid in her questions, and sharp at times.
10135{_}1585{_}He was vexed, and said, "She was never that way afore, no, never."
10135{_}1586{_}For a time she knew her head was wrong, and was always asking our pardon--the dear, gentle old woman; then delirium set in strong, without pause.
10135{_}1587{_}Her brain gave way, and then came that terrible spectacle,    "The intellectual power, through words and things,   Went sounding on, a dim and perilous way";  she sang bits of old songs and Psalms, stopping suddenly, mingling the Psalms of David and the diviner words of his Son and Lord with homely odds and ends of ballads.
10135{_}1588{_}Nothing more touching, or in a sense more strangely beautiful, did I ever witness.
10135{_}1589{_}Her tremulous, rapid, affectionate, eager, Scotch voice--the swift, aimless, bewildered mind, the baffled utterance, the bright and perilous eye; some wild words, some household cares, something for James, the names of the dead, Rab called rapidly and in a "fremyt" voice, and he starting up, surprised, and slinking off as if he were to blame somehow, or had been dreaming he heard.
10135{_}1590{_}Many eager questions and beseechings which James and I could make nothing of, and on which she seemed to set her all, and then sink back ununderstood.
10135{_}1591{_}It was very sad, but better than many things that are not called sad.
10135{_}1592{_}James hovered about, put out and miserable, but active and exact as ever; read to her, when there was a lull, short bits from the Psalms, prose and metre, chanting the latter in his own rude and serious way, showing great knowledge of the fit words, bearing up like a man, and doating over her as his "ain Ailie."
10135{_}1593{_}"Ailie, ma woman!"
10135{_}1594{_}"Ma ain bonnie wee dawtie!"
10135{_}1595{_}The end was drawing on; the golden bowl was breaking; the silver cord was fast being loosed--that _animula, blandula, vagula, hospes, comesque_, was about to flee.
10135{_}1596{_}The body and the soul--companions for sixty years--were being sundered and taking leave.
10135{_}1597{_}She was walking, alone, through the valley of that shadow into which one day we must all enter--and yet she was not alone, for we know whose rod and staff were comforting her.
10135{_}1598{_}One night she had fallen quiet, and, as we hoped, asleep; her eyes were shut.
10135{_}1599{_}We put down the gas, and sat watching her.
10135{_}1600{_}Suddenly she sat up in bed, and, taking a bedgown which was lying on it rolled up, she held it eagerly to her breast--to the right side.
10135{_}1601{_}We could see her eyes bright with a surprising tenderness and joy, bending over this bundle of clothes.
10135{_}1602{_}She held it as a woman holds her sucking child; opening out her night-gown impatiently, and holding it close and brooding over it and murmuring foolish little words, as over one whom his mother comforteth, and who sucks and is satisfied.
10135{_}1603{_}It was pitiful and strange to see her wasted, dying look, keen and yet vague--her immense love.
10135{_}1604{_}"Preserve me!"
10135{_}1605{_}groaned James, giving way.
10135{_}1606{_}And then she rocked back and forward, as if to make it sleep, hushing it, and wasting on it her infinite fondness.
10135{_}1607{_}"Wae's me, doctor; I declare she's thinkin' it's that bairn."
10135{_}1608{_}"What bairn?"
10135{_}1609{_}"The only bairn we ever had; our wee Mysie, and she's in the Kingdom forty years and mair."
10135{_}1610{_}It was plainly true; the pain in the breast, telling its urgent story to a bewildered, ruined brain, was misread and mistaken; it suggested to her the uneasiness of a breast full of milk, and then the child; and so again once more they were together, and she had her ain wee Mysie on her bosom.
10135{_}1611{_}This was the close.
10135{_}1612{_}She sank rapidly; the delirium left her; but, as she whispered, she was "clean silly"; it was the lightening before the final darkness.
10135{_}1613{_}After having for some time lain still, her eyes shut, she said, "James!"
10135{_}1614{_}He came close to her, and, lifting up her calm, clear, beautiful eyes, she gave him a long look, turned to me kindly but shortly, looked for Rab but could not see him, then turned to her husband again, as if she would never leave off looking, shut her eyes, and composed herself.
10135{_}1615{_}She lay for some time breathing quick, and passed away so gently that, when we thought she was gone, James, in his old-fashioned way, held the mirror to her face.
10135{_}1616{_}After a long pause, one small spot of dimness was breathed out; it vanished away, and never returned, leaving the blank, clear darkness without a stain.
10135{_}1617{_}"What is our life?
10135{_}1618{_}It is even as a vapor, which appeareth for a little time, and then vanisheth away."
10135{_}1619{_}Rab all this time had been full awake and motionless; he came forward beside us; Ailie's hand, which James had held, was hanging down; it was soaked with his tears; Rab licked it all over carefully, looked at her, and returned to his place under the table.
10135{_}1620{_}James and I sat, I don't know how long, but for some time.
10135{_}1621{_}Saying nothing, he started up abruptly, and with some noise went to the table, and, putting his right fore and middle fingers each into a shoe, pulled them out and put them on, breaking one of the leather latchets, and muttering in anger, "I never did the like o' that afore!"
10135{_}1622{_}I believe he never did; nor after either.
10135{_}1623{_}"Rab!"
10135{_}1624{_}he said, roughly, and, pointing with his thumb to the bottom of the bed.
10135{_}1625{_}Rab leaped up and settled himself, his head and eye to the dead face.
10135{_}1626{_}"Maister John, ye'll wait for me," said the carrier; and disappeared in the darkness, thundering down-stairs in his heavy shoes.
10135{_}1627{_}I ran to a front window; there he was, already round the house and out at the gate, fleeing like a shadow.
10135{_}1628{_}I was afraid about him, and yet not afraid; so I sat down beside Rab, and, being wearied, fell asleep.
10135{_}1629{_}I awoke from a sudden noise outside.
10135{_}1630{_}It was November, and there had been a heavy fall of snow.
10135{_}1631{_}Rab was _in statu quo_; he heard the noise, too, and plainly knew it, but never moved.
10135{_}1632{_}I looked out; and there, at the gate, in the dim morning--for the sun was not up--was Jess and the cart, a cloud of steam rising from the old mare.
10135{_}1633{_}I did not see James; he was already at the door, and came up the stairs and met me.
10135{_}1634{_}It was less than three hours since he left, and he must have posted out--who knows how?--to Howgate, full nine miles off, yoked Jess, and driven her astonished into town.
10135{_}1635{_}He had an armful of blankets, and was streaming with perspiration.
10135{_}1636{_}He nodded to me, and spread out on the floor two pairs of clean old blankets having at their corners, "A.G., 1794," in large letters in red worsted.
10135{_}1637{_}These were the initials of Alison Graeme, and James may have looked in at her from without--himself unseen but not unthought of--when he was "wat, wat, and weary," and, after having walked many a mile over the hills, may have seen her sitting, while "a' the lave were sleeping," and by the firelight working her name on the blankets for her ain James's bed.
10135{_}1638{_}He motioned Rab down, and, taking his wife in his arms, laid her in the blankets, and happed her carefully and firmly up, leaving the face uncovered; and then, lifting her, he nodded again sharply to me, and with a resolved but utterly miserable face strode along the passage and down-stairs, followed by Rab.
10135{_}1639{_}I followed with a light; but he didn't need it.
10135{_}1640{_}I went out, holding stupidly the candle in my hand in the calm, frosty air; we were soon at the gate.
10135{_}1641{_}I could have helped him, but I saw he was not to be meddled with, and he was strong, and did not need it.
10135{_}1642{_}He laid her down as tenderly, as safely, as he had lifted her out ten days before--as tenderly as when he had her first in his arms when she was only "A.G."--sorted her, leaving that beautiful sealed face open to the heavens; and then, taking Jess by the head, he moved away.
10135{_}1643{_}He did not notice me, neither did Rab, who presided behind the cart.
10135{_}1644{_}I stood till they passed through the long shadow of the College and turned up Nicolson Street.
