10135{_}1645{_}I heard the solitary cart sound through the streets, and die away and come again; and I returned, thinking of that company going up Libberton Brae, then along Roslin Muir, the morning light touching the Pentlands, and making them like onlooking ghosts; then down the hill through Auchindinny woods, past "haunted Woodhouselee"; and as daybreak came sweeping up the bleak Lammermuirs, and fell on his own door, the company would stop, and James would take the key, and lift Ailie up again, laying her on her own bed, and, having put Jess up, would return with Rab and shut the door.
10135{_}1646{_}James buried his wife, with his neighbors mourning, Rab watching the proceedings from a distance.
10135{_}1647{_}It was snow, and that black, ragged hole would look strange in the midst of the swelling, spotless cushion of white.
10135{_}1648{_}James looked after everything; then rather suddenly fell ill, and took to bed; was insensible when the doctor came, and soon died.
10135{_}1649{_}A sort of low fever was prevailing in the village, and his want of sleep, his exhaustion, and his misery made him apt to take it.
10135{_}1650{_}The grave was not difficult to reopen.
10135{_}1651{_}A fresh fall of snow had again made all things white and smooth; Rab once more looked on, and slunk home to the stable.
10135{_}1652{_}And what of Rab?
10135{_}1653{_}I asked for him next week at the new carrier who got the good-will of James's business and was now master of Jess and her cart.
10135{_}1654{_}"How's Rab?"
10135{_}1655{_}He put me off, and said, rather rudely, "What's _your_ business wi' the dowg?"
10135{_}1656{_}I was not to be so put off.
10135{_}1657{_}"Where's Rab?"
10135{_}1658{_}He, getting confused and red, and intermeddling with his hair, said, "'Deed, sir, Rab's deid."
10135{_}1659{_}"Dead!
10135{_}1660{_}What did he die of?"
10135{_}1661{_}"Weel, sir," said he, getting redder, "he didna' exactly dee; he was killed.
10135{_}1662{_}I had to brain him wi' a rack-pin; there was nae doin' wi' him.
10135{_}1663{_}He lay in the treviss wi' the mear, and wadna come oot.
10135{_}1664{_}I tempit him wi' kail and meat, but he wad tak naething, and keepit me frae feeding the beast, and he was aye gurrin', and grup, gruppin' me by the legs.
10135{_}1665{_}I was laith to mak' awa' wi' the auld dowg, his like wasna atween this and Thornhill--but, 'deed, sir, I could do naething else."
10135{_}1666{_}I believed him.
10135{_}1667{_}Fit end for Rab, quick and complete.
10135{_}1668{_}His teeth and his friends gone, why should he keep the peace and be civil?
10135{_}1669{_}He was buried in the braeface, near the burn, the children of the village, his companions, who used to make very free with him and sit on his ample stomach as he lay half asleep at the door in the sun, watching the solemnity.
10135{_}1670{_}THE BOOTS AT THE HOLLY-TREE INN  _Charles Dickens_ (1812-1870)   Where had he been in his time?
10135{_}1671{_}he repeated, when I asked him the question, Lord, he had been everywhere!
10135{_}1672{_}And what had he been?
10135{_}1673{_}Bless you, he had been everything you could mention, a'most!
10135{_}1674{_}Seen a good deal?
10135{_}1675{_}Why, of course he had.
10135{_}1676{_}I should say so, he could assure me, if I only knew about a twentieth part of what had come in _his_ way.
10135{_}1677{_}Why, it would be easier for him, he expected, to tell what he hadn't seen than what he had.
10135{_}1678{_}Ah!
10135{_}1679{_}a deal, it would.
10135{_}1680{_}What was the curiousest thing he had seen?
10135{_}1681{_}Well!
10135{_}1682{_}He didn't know.
10135{_}1683{_}He couldn't momently name what was the curiousest thing he had seen--unless it was a Unicorn--and he see _him_ once at a fair.
10135{_}1684{_}But supposing a young gentleman not eight year old was to run away with a fine young woman of seven, might I think _that_ a queer start?
10135{_}1685{_}Certainly.
10135{_}1686{_}Then that was a start as he himself had had his blessed eyes on, and he had cleaned the shoes they run away in--and they was so little he couldn't get his hand into 'em.
10135{_}1687{_}Master Harry Walmers' father, you see, he lived at the Elmses, down away by Shooter's Hill there, six or seven miles from Lunnon.
10135{_}1688{_}He was a gentleman of spirit, and good-looking, and held his head up when he walked, and had what you call Fire about him.
10135{_}1689{_}He wrote poetry, and he rode, and he ran, and he cricketed, and he danced, and he acted, and he done it all equally beautiful.
10135{_}1690{_}He was uncommon proud of Master Harry as was his only child; but he didn't spoil him neither.
10135{_}1691{_}He was a gentleman that had a will of his own and a eye of his own, and that would be minded.
10135{_}1692{_}Consequently, though he made quite a companion of the fine bright boy, and was delighted to see him so fond of reading his fairy-books, and was never tired of hearing him say my name is Norval, or hearing him sing his songs about Young May Moons is beaming love, and When he as adores thee has left but the name, and that; still he kept the command over the child, and the child _was_ a child, and it's to be wished more of 'em was.
10135{_}1693{_}How did Boots happen to know all this?
10135{_}1694{_}Why, through being under-gardener.
10135{_}1695{_}Of course he couldn't be under-gardener, and he always about, in the summer-time, near the windows on the lawn, a-mowing, and sweeping, and weeding, and pruning, and this and that, without getting acquainted with the ways of the family.
10135{_}1696{_}Even supposing Master Harry hadn't come to him one morning early, and said, "Cobbs, how should you spell Norah, if you was asked?"
10135{_}1697{_}and then began cutting it in print all over the fence.
10135{_}1698{_}He couldn't say that he had taken particular notice of children before that; but really it was pretty to see them two mites a-going about the place together, deep in love.
10135{_}1699{_}And the courage of the boy!
10135{_}1700{_}Bless your soul, he'd have throwed off his little hat, and tucked up his little sleeves, and gone in at a lion, he would, if they had happened to meet one, and she had been frightened of him.
10135{_}1701{_}One day he stops, along with her, where Boots was hoeing weeds in the gravel, and says, speaking up, "Cobbs," he says, "I like _you."
10135{_}1702{_}"Do_ you, sir?
10135{_}1703{_}I'm proud to hear it."
10135{_}1704{_}"Yes, I do, Cobbs.
10135{_}1705{_}Why do I like you, do you think, Cobbs?"
10135{_}1706{_}"Don't know, Master Harry, I am sure."
10135{_}1707{_}"Because Norah likes you, Cobbs."
10135{_}1708{_}"Indeed, sir?
10135{_}1709{_}That's very gratifying."
10135{_}1710{_}"Gratifying, Cobbs?
10135{_}1711{_}It's better than millions of the brightest diamonds to be liked by Norah."
10135{_}1712{_}"Certainly, sir."
10135{_}1713{_}"Would you like another situation, Cobbs?"
10135{_}1714{_}"Well, sir, I shouldn't object if it was a good 'un."
10135{_}1715{_}"Then, Cobbs," says he, "you shall be our Head Gardener when we are married."
10135{_}1716{_}And he tucks her, in her little sky-blue mantle, under his arm, and walks away.
10135{_}1717{_}Boots could assure me that it was better than a picter, and equal to a play, to see them babies, with their long, bright, curling hair, their sparkling eyes, and their beautiful light tread, a-rambling about the garden, deep in love.
10135{_}1718{_}Boots was of opinion that the birds believed they was birds, and kept up with 'em, singing to please 'em.
10135{_}1719{_}Sometimes they would creep under the tulip-tree, and would sit there with their arms round one another's necks, and their soft cheeks touching, a-reading about the Prince and the Dragon, and the good and bad enchanters, and the king's fair daughter.
10135{_}1720{_}Sometimes he would hear them planning about a house in a forest, keeping bees and a cow, and living entirely on milk and honey.
10135{_}1721{_}Once he came upon them by the pond, and heard Master Harry say, "Adorable Norah, kiss me, and say you love me to distraction, or I'll jump in head foremost."
10135{_}1722{_}And Boots made no question he would have done it if she hadn't complied.
10135{_}1723{_}On the whole, Boots said it had a tendency to make him feel he was in love himself--only he didn't exactly know who with.
10135{_}1724{_}"Cobbs," said Master Harry, one evening, when Cobbs was watering the flowers, "I am going on a visit, this present midsummer, to my grandmamma's at York."
10135{_}1725{_}"Are you, indeed, sir?
10135{_}1726{_}I hope you'll have a pleasant time.
10135{_}1727{_}I am going into Yorkshire, myself, when I leave here."
10135{_}1728{_}"Are you going to your grandmamma's, Cobbs?"
10135{_}1729{_}"No, sir.
10135{_}1730{_}I haven't got such a thing."
10135{_}1731{_}"Not as a grandmamma, Cobbs?"
10135{_}1732{_}"No, sir."
10135{_}1733{_}The boy looked on at the watering of the flowers for a little while, and then said, "I shall be very glad indeed to go, Cobbs--Norah's going."
10135{_}1734{_}"You'll be all right, then, sir," says Cobbs, "with your beautiful sweetheart by your side."
10135{_}1735{_}"Cobbs," returned the boy, flushing, "I never let anybody joke about it when I can prevent them."
10135{_}1736{_}"It wasn't a joke, sir," says Cobbs, with humility--"wasn't so meant."
10135{_}1737{_}"I am glad of that, Cobbs, because I like you, you know, and you're going to live with us.
10135{_}1738{_}Cobbs!"
10135{_}1739{_}"Sir."
10135{_}1740{_}"What do you think my grandmamma gives me when I go down there?"
10135{_}1741{_}"I couldn't so much as make a guess, sir."
10135{_}1742{_}"A Bank-of-England five-pound note, Cobbs."
10135{_}1743{_}"Whew!"
10135{_}1744{_}says Cobbs, "that's a spanking sum of money, Master Harry."
10135{_}1745{_}"A person could do a great deal with such a sum of money as that--couldn't a person, Cobbs?"
10135{_}1746{_}"I believe you, sir!"
10135{_}1747{_}"Cobbs," said the boy, "I'll tell you a secret.
10135{_}1748{_}At Norah's house they have been joking her about me, and pretending to laugh at our being engaged--pretending to make game of it, Cobbs!"
10135{_}1749{_}"Such, sir," says Cobbs, "is the depravity of human natur'."
10135{_}1750{_}The boy, looking exactly like his father, stood for a few minutes with his glowing face toward the sunset, and then departed with, "Good-night, Cobbs.
10135{_}1751{_}I'm going in."
10135{_}1752{_}If I was to ask Boots how it happened that he was a-going to leave that place just at that present time, well, he couldn't rightly answer me.
10135{_}1753{_}He did suppose he might have stayed there till now if he had been anyways inclined.
10135{_}1754{_}But you see, he was younger then, and he wanted change.
10135{_}1755{_}That's what he wanted--change.
10135{_}1756{_}Mr. Walmers, he said to him when he gave him notice of his intentions to leave, "Cobbs," he says, "have you anythink to complain of?
10135{_}1757{_}I make the inquiry, because if I find that any of my people really has anythink to complain of, I wish to make it right if I can."
10135{_}1758{_}"No, sir," says Cobbs; "thanking you, sir, I find myself as well sitiwated here as I could hope to be anywheres.
10135{_}1759{_}The truth is, sir, that I'm a-going to seek my fortun'."
10135{_}1760{_}"Oh, indeed, Cobbs!"
10135{_}1761{_}he says; "I hope you may find it."
10135{_}1762{_}And Boots could assure me--which he did, touching his hair with his bootjack, as a salute in the way of his present calling--that he hadn't found it yet.
10135{_}1763{_}Well, sir!
10135{_}1764{_}Boots left the Elmses when his time was up, and Master Harry, he went down to the old lady's at York, which old lady would have given that child the teeth out of her head (if she had had any), she was so wrapped up in him.
10135{_}1765{_}What does that Infant do--for Infant you may call him, and be within the mark--but cut away from that old lady's with his Norah, on a expedition to go to Gretna Green and be married!
10135{_}1766{_}Sir, Boots was at this identical Holly-Tree Inn (having left it several times to better himself, but always come back through one thing or another), when, one summer afternoon, the coach drives up, and out of the coach gets them two children.
10135{_}1767{_}The Guard says to our Governor, "I don't quite make out these little passengers, but the young gentleman's words was, that they was to be brought here."
10135{_}1768{_}The young gentleman gets out; hands his lady out; gives the Guard something for himself; says to our Governor, "We're to stop here to-night, please.
10135{_}1769{_}Sitting-room and two bedrooms will be required.
10135{_}1770{_}Chops and cherry-pudding for two!"
10135{_}1771{_}and tucks her in her little sky-blue mantle, under his arm, and walks into the house much bolder than Brass.
10135{_}1772{_}Boots leaves me to judge what the amazement of that establishment was, when these two tiny creatures all alone by themselves was marched into the Angel--much more so when he, who had seen them without their seeing him, give the Governor his views upon the expedition they was upon.
10135{_}1773{_}"Cobbs," says the Governor, "if this is so, I must set off myself to York, and quiet their friends' minds.
10135{_}1774{_}In which case you must keep your eye upon 'em, and humor 'em till I come back.
10135{_}1775{_}But before I take these measures, Cobbs, I should wish you to find from themselves whether your opinions is correct."
10135{_}1776{_}"Sir, to you," says Cobbs, "that shall be done directly."
10135{_}1777{_}So Boots goes up-stairs to the Angel, and there he finds Master Harry, on a e'normous sofa--immense at any time, but looking like the Great Bed of Ware, compared with him--a-drying the eyes of Miss Norah with his pocket-hankecher.
10135{_}1778{_}Their little legs was entirely off the ground, of course, and it really is not possible for Boots to express to me how small them children looked.
10135{_}1779{_}"It's Cobbs!
10135{_}1780{_}It's Cobbs!"
10135{_}1781{_}cries Master Harry, and comes running to him on t'other side, and catching hold of his t'other hand, and they both jump for joy.
10135{_}1782{_}"I see you a-getting out, sir," says Cobbs.
10135{_}1783{_}"I thought it was you.
10135{_}1784{_}I thought I couldn't be mistaken in your height and figure.
10135{_}1785{_}What's the object of your journey, sir?
10135{_}1786{_}Matrimonial?"
10135{_}1787{_}"We're going to be married, Cobbs, at Gretna Green," returned the boy.
10135{_}1788{_}"We have run away on purpose.
10135{_}1789{_}Norah has been in rather low spirits, Cobbs; but she'll be happy, now we have found you to be our friend."
10135{_}1790{_}"Thank you, sir, and thank _you_, miss," says Cobbs, "for your good opinion.
10135{_}1791{_}_Did_ you bring any luggage with you, sir?"
10135{_}1792{_}If I will believe Boots when he gives me his word and honor upon it, the lady had got a parasol, a smelling-bottle, a round and a half of cold buttered toast, eight peppermint drops, and a hair-brush--seemingly a doll's.
10135{_}1793{_}The gentleman had got about half a dozen yards of string, a knife, three or four sheets of writing-paper, folded up surprising small, a orange, and a Chaney mug with his name upon it.
10135{_}1794{_}"What may be the exact nature of your plans, sir?"
10135{_}1795{_}says Cobbs.
10135{_}1796{_}"To go on," replied the boy--which the courage of that boy was something wonderful!--"in the morning, and be married to-morrow."
10135{_}1797{_}"Just so, sir," says Cobbs.
10135{_}1798{_}"Would it meet your views, sir, if I was to accompany you?"
10135{_}1799{_}When Cobbs said this, they both jumped for joy again, and cried out, "Oh yes, yes, Cobbs!
10135{_}1800{_}Yes!"
10135{_}1801{_}"Well, sir!"
10135{_}1802{_}says Cobbs.
10135{_}1803{_}"If you will excuse me having the freedom to give an opinion, what I should recommend would be this.
10135{_}1804{_}I am acquainted with a pony, sir, which, put in a pheayton that I could borrow, would take you and Mrs. Harry Walmers, Junior (myself driving, if you approved), to the end of your journey in a very short space of time.
10135{_}1805{_}I am not altogether sure, sir, that this pony will be at liberty to-morrow, but even if you had to wait over to-morrow for him, it might be worth your while.
10135{_}1806{_}As to the small account here, sir, in case you was to find yourself running at all short, that don't signify; because I am a part proprietor of this inn, and it could stand over."
10135{_}1807{_}Boots assures me that when they clapped their hands, and jumped for joy again, and called him "Good Cobbs!"
10135{_}1808{_}and "Dear Cobbs!"
10135{_}1809{_}and bent across him to kiss one another in the delight of their confiding hearts, he felt himself the meanest rascal for deceiving 'em that ever was born.
10135{_}1810{_}"Is there anything you want just at present, sir?"
10135{_}1811{_}says Cobbs, mortally ashamed of himself.
10135{_}1812{_}"We should like some cakes after dinner," answered Master Harry, folding his arms, putting out one leg, and looking straight at him, "and two apples and jam.
10135{_}1813{_}With dinner we should like to have toast and water.
10135{_}1814{_}But Norah has always been accustomed to half a glass of currant wine at dessert.
10135{_}1815{_}And so have I."
10135{_}1816{_}"It shall be ordered at the bar, sir," says Cobbs; and away he went.
10135{_}1817{_}Boots has the feeling as fresh upon him this moment of speaking as he had then, that he would far rather have had it out in half a dozen rounds with the Governor than have combined with him; and that he wished with all his heart there was any impossible place where two babies could make an impossible marriage, and live impossibly happy ever afterward.
10135{_}1818{_}However, as it couldn't be, he went into the Governor's plans, and the Governor set off for York in half an hour.
10135{_}1819{_}The way in which the women of that house--without exception--every one of 'em--married _and_ single--took to that boy when they heard the story, Boots considers surprising.
10135{_}1820{_}It was as much as he could do to keep 'em from dashing into the room and kissing him.
10135{_}1821{_}They climbed up all sorts of places, at the risk of their lives, to look at him through a pane of glass.
10135{_}1822{_}They was seven deep at the keyhole.
10135{_}1823{_}They was out of their minds about him and his bold spirit.
10135{_}1824{_}In the evening, Boots went into the room to see how the runaway couple was getting on.
10135{_}1825{_}The gentleman was on the window-seat, supporting the lady in his arms.
10135{_}1826{_}She had tears upon her face, and was lying, very tired and half asleep, with her head upon his shoulder.
10135{_}1827{_}"Mrs. Harry Walmers, Junior, fatigued, sir?"
10135{_}1828{_}says Cobbs.
10135{_}1829{_}"Yes, she is tired, Cobbs; but she is not used to be away from home, and she has been in low spirits again.
10135{_}1830{_}Cobbs, do you think you could bring a biffin, please?"
10135{_}1831{_}"I ask your pardon, sir," says Cobbs.
10135{_}1832{_}"What was it you--"  "I think a Norfolk biffin would rouse her, Cobbs.
10135{_}1833{_}She is very fond of them."
10135{_}1834{_}Boots withdrew in search of the required restorative, and, when he brought it in, the gentleman handed it to the lady, and fed her with a spoon, and took a little himself; the lady being heavy with sleep, and rather cross.
10135{_}1835{_}"What should you think, sir," says Cobbs, "of a chamber candlestick?"
10135{_}1836{_}The gentleman approved; the chambermaid went first, up the great staircase; the lady, in her sky-blue mantle, followed, gallantly escorted by the gentleman; the gentleman embraced her at her door, and retired to his own apartment, where Boots softly locked him in.
10135{_}1837{_}Boots couldn't but feel with increased acuteness what a base deceiver he was, when they consulted him at breakfast (they had ordered sweet milk-and-water, and toast and currant jelly, over-night) about the pony.
10135{_}1838{_}It really was as much as he could do, he don't mind confessing to me, to look them two young things in the face, and think what a wicked old father of lies he had grown up to be.
10135{_}1839{_}Howsomever, he went on a-lying like a Trojan about the pony.
10135{_}1840{_}He told 'em that it did so unfortunately happen that the pony was half clipped, you see, and that he couldn't be taken out in that state, for fear it should strike to his inside.
10135{_}1841{_}But that he'd be finished clipping in the course of the day, and that to-morrow morning at eight o'clock the pheayton would be ready.
10135{_}1842{_}Boots' view of the whole case, looking back on it in my room, is, that Mrs. Harry Walmers, Junior, was beginning to give in.
10135{_}1843{_}She hadn't had her hair curled when she went to bed, and she didn't seem quite up to brushing it herself, and its getting in her eyes put her out.
10135{_}1844{_}But nothing put out Master Harry.
10135{_}1845{_}He sat behind his breakfast-cup, a-tearing away at the jelly, as if he had been his own father.
10135{_}1846{_}After breakfast Boots is inclined to consider they drawed soldiers--at least he knows that many such was found in the fireplace, all on horseback.
10135{_}1847{_}In the course of the morning Master Harry rang the bell--it was surprising how that there boy did carry on--and said, in a sprightly way, "Cobbs, is there any good walks in this neighborhood?"
10135{_}1848{_}"Yes, sir," says Cobbs.
10135{_}1849{_}"There's Love Lane."
10135{_}1850{_}"Get out with you, Cobbs!"
10135{_}1851{_}--that was that there boy's expression--"you're joking."
10135{_}1852{_}"Begging your pardon, sir," says Cobbs, "there _really is_ Love Lane.
10135{_}1853{_}And a pleasant walk it is, and proud shall I be to show it to yourself and Mrs. Harry Walmers, Junior."
10135{_}1854{_}"Norah, dear," says Master Harry, "this is curious.
10135{_}1855{_}We really ought to see Love Lane.
10135{_}1856{_}Put on your bonnet, my sweetest darling, and we will go there with Cobbs."
10135{_}1857{_}Boots leaves me to judge what a Beast he felt himself to be, when that young pair told him, as they all three jogged along together, that they had made up their minds to give him two thousand guineas a year as Head Gardener, on account of his being so true a friend to 'em.
10135{_}1858{_}Boots could have wished at the moment that the earth would have opened and swallowed him up, he felt so mean, with their beaming eyes a-looking at him, and believing him.
10135{_}1859{_}Well, sir, he turned the conversation as well as he could, and he took 'em down Love Lane to the water-meadows, and there Master Harry would have drowned himself in half a moment more, a-getting out a water-lily for her--but nothing daunted that boy.
10135{_}1860{_}Well, sir, they was tired out.
10135{_}1861{_}All being so new and strange to 'em, they was tired as tired could be.
10135{_}1862{_}And they laid down on a bank of daisies, like the children in the wood, leastways meadows, and fell asleep.
10135{_}1863{_}Boots don't know--perhaps I do--but never mind, it don't signify either way--why it made a man fit to make a fool of himself to see them two pretty babies a-lying there in the clear, still day, not dreaming half so hard when they was asleep as they done when they was awake.
10135{_}1864{_}But, Lord!
10135{_}1865{_}when you come to think of yourself, you know, and what a game you have been up to ever since you was in your own cradle, and what a poor sort of chap you are, and how it's always either Yesterday with you, or To-morrow, and never To-day, that's where it is!
10135{_}1866{_}Well, sir, they woke up at last, and then one thing was getting pretty clear to Boots--namely, that Mrs. Harry Walmerses, Junior's, temper was on the move.
10135{_}1867{_}When Master Harry took her round the waist, she said he "teased her so"; and when he says, "Norah, my young May Moon, your Harry tease you?"
10135{_}1868{_}she tells him, "Yes; and I want to go home."
10135{_}1869{_}A biled fowl and baked bread-and-butter pudding brought Mrs. Walmers up a little; but Boots could have wished, he must privately own to me, to have seen her more sensible of the woice of love, and less abandoning of herself to currants.
10135{_}1870{_}However, Master Harry, he kept up, and his noble heart was as fond as ever.
10135{_}1871{_}Mrs. Walmers turned very sleepy about dusk, and began to cry.
10135{_}1872{_}Therefore, Mrs. Walmers went off to bed as per yesterday; and Master Harry ditto repeated.
10135{_}1873{_}About eleven or twelve at night comes back the Governor in a chaise, along with Mr. Walmers and a elderly lady.
10135{_}1874{_}Mr. Walmers looks amused and very serious, both at once, and says to our Missis: "We are much indebted to you, ma'am; for your kind care of our little children, which we can never sufficiently acknowledge.
10135{_}1875{_}Pray, ma'am, where is my boy?"
10135{_}1876{_}Our Missis says: "Cobbs has the dear child in charge, sir.
10135{_}1877{_}Cobbs, show Forty!"
10135{_}1878{_}Then he says to Cobbs: "Ah, Cobbs, I am glad to see _you_!
10135{_}1879{_}I understood you was here!"
10135{_}1880{_}And Cobbs says: "Yes, sir.
10135{_}1881{_}Your most obedient, sir."
10135{_}1882{_}I may be surprised to hear Boots say it, perhaps; but Boots assures me that his heart beat like a hammer, going up-stairs.
10135{_}1883{_}"I beg your pardon, sir," says he, while unlocking the door; "I do hope you are not angry with Master Harry.
10135{_}1884{_}For Master Harry is a fine boy, sir, and will do you credit and honor."
10135{_}1885{_}And Boots signifies to me that, if the fine boy's father had contradicted him in the daring state of mind in which he then was, he thinks he should have "fetched him a crack," and taken the consequences.
10135{_}1886{_}But Mr. Walmers only says: "No, Cobbs.
10135{_}1887{_}No, my good fellow.
10135{_}1888{_}Thank you!"
10135{_}1889{_}And, the door being opened, goes in.
10135{_}1890{_}Boots goes in, too, holding the light, and he sees Mr. Walmers go up to the bedside, bend gently down, and kiss the little sleeping face.
10135{_}1891{_}Then he stands looking at it for a minute, looking wonderfully like it (they do say he ran away with Mrs. Walmers); and then he gently shakes the little shoulder.
10135{_}1892{_}"Harry, my dear boy!
10135{_}1893{_}Harry!"
10135{_}1894{_}Master Harry starts up and looks at him.
10135{_}1895{_}Looks at Cobbs, too.
10135{_}1896{_}Such is the honor of that mite, that he looks at Cobbs, to see whether he has brought him into trouble.
10135{_}1897{_}"I'm not angry, my child.
10135{_}1898{_}I only want you to dress yourself and come home."
10135{_}1899{_}"Yes, pa."  Master Harry dresses himself quickly.
10135{_}1900{_}His breast begins to swell when he has nearly finished, and it swells more and more as he stands, at last, a-looking at his father; his father standing a-looking at him, the quiet image of him.
10135{_}1901{_}"Please may I"--the spirit of that little creatur', and the way he kept his rising tears down!--"please, dear pa--may I--kiss Norah before I go?"
10135{_}1902{_}"You may, my child."
10135{_}1903{_}So he takes Master Harry in his hand, and Boots leads the way with the candle, and they come to that other bedroom, where the elderly lady is seated by the bed, and poor little Mrs. Harry Walmers, Junior, is fast asleep.
10135{_}1904{_}There the father lifts the child up to the pillow, and he lays his little face down for an instant by the little warm face of poor unconscious little Mrs. Harry Walmers, Junior, and gently draws it to him--a sight so touching to the chambermaids, who are peeping through the door, that one of them called out, "It's a shame to part 'em!"
10135{_}1905{_}But this chambermaid was always, as Boots informs us, a softhearted one.
10135{_}1906{_}Not that there was any harm in that girl.
10135{_}1907{_}Far from it.
10135{_}1908{_}Finally, Boots says, that's all about it.
10135{_}1909{_}Mr. Walmers drove away in the chaise, having hold of Master Harry's hand.
10135{_}1910{_}The elderly lady and Mrs. Walmers, Junior, that was never to be (she married a Captain long afterward, and died in India), went off next day.
10135{_}1911{_}In conclusion, Boots puts it to me whether I hold with him in two opinions: firstly, that there are not many couples on their way to be married who are half as innocent of guile as those two children; secondly, that it would be a jolly good thing for a great many couples on their way to be married, if they could only be stopped in time, and brought back separately.
10135{_}1912{_}A STORY OF SEVEN DEVILS[1]  [Footnote 1: From _Amos Kilbright and Other Stories_.
10135{_}1913{_}1888.]
10135{_}1914{_}_Frank R. Stockton_ (1834-1902)   The negro church which stood in the pine woods near the little village of Oxford Cross Roads, in one of the lower counties of Virginia, was presided over by an elderly individual, known to the community in general as Uncle Pete; but on Sundays the members of his congregation addressed him as Brudder Pete.
10135{_}1915{_}He was an earnest and energetic man, and, although he could neither read nor write, he had for many years expounded the Scriptures to the satisfaction of his hearers.
10135{_}1916{_}His memory was good, and those portions of the Bible, which from time to time he had heard read, were used by him, and frequently with powerful effect, in his sermons.
10135{_}1917{_}His interpretations of the Scriptures were generally entirely original, and were made to suit the needs, or what he supposed to be the needs, of his congregation.
10135{_}1918{_}Whether as "Uncle Pete" in the garden and corn-field, or "Brudder Pete" in the church, he enjoyed the good opinion of everybody excepting one person, and that was his wife.
10135{_}1919{_}She was a high-tempered and somewhat dissatisfied person, who had conceived the idea that her husband was in the habit of giving too much time to the church, and too little to the acquisition of corn-bread and pork.
10135{_}1920{_}On a certain Saturday she gave him a most tremendous scolding, which so affected the spirits of the good man that it influenced his decision in regard to the selection of the subject for his sermon the next day.
10135{_}1921{_}His congregation was accustomed to being astonished, and rather liked it, but never before had their minds received such a shock as when the preacher announced the subject of his discourse.
10135{_}1922{_}He did not take any particular text, for this was not his custom, but he boldly stated that the Bible declared that every woman in this world was possessed by seven devils; and the evils which this state of things had brought upon the world, he showed forth with much warmth and feeling.
10135{_}1923{_}Subject-matter, principally from his own experience, crowded in upon his mind, and he served it out to his audience hot and strong.
10135{_}1924{_}If his deductions could have been proved to be correct, all women were creatures who, by reason of their sevenfold diabolic possession, were not capable of independent thought or action, and who should in tears and humility place themselves absolutely under the direction and authority of the other sex.
10135{_}1925{_}When he approached the conclusion of his sermon, Brother Peter closed with a bang the Bible, which, although he could not read a word of it, always lay open before him while he preached, and delivered the concluding exhortation of his sermon.
10135{_}1926{_}"Now, my dear brev'ren ob dis congregation," he said, "I want you to understan' dat dar's nuffin in dis yer sarmon wot you've jus' heerd ter make you think yousefs angels.
10135{_}1927{_}By no means, brev'ren; you was all brung up by women, an' you've got ter lib wid' em, an ef anythin' in dis yer worl' is ketchin', my dear brev'ren, it's habin debbils, an' from wot I've seen ob some ob de men ob dis worl' I 'spect dey is persest ob 'bout all de debbils dey got room fur.
10135{_}1928{_}But de Bible don' say nuffin p'intedly on de subjec' ob de number ob debbils in man, an' I 'spec' dose dat's got 'em--an' we ought ter feel pow'ful thankful, my dear brev'ren, dat de Bible don' say we all's got 'em--has 'em 'cordin to sarcumstances.
10135{_}1929{_}But wid de women it's dif'rent; dey's got jus' sebin, an' bless my soul, brev'ren, I think dat's 'nuff.
10135{_}1930{_}"While I was a-turnin' ober in my min' de subjec' ob dis sarmon, dere come ter me a bit ob Scripter wot I heerd at a big preachin' an' baptizin' at Kyarter's Mills, 'bout ten year' ago.
10135{_}1931{_}One ob de preachers was a-tellin' about ole mudder Ebe a-eatin' de apple, and says he: De sarpint fus' come along wid a red apple, an' says he: 'You gib dis yer to your husban', an' he think it so mighty good dat when he done eat it he gib you anything you ax him fur, ef you tell him whar de tree is.'
10135{_}1932{_}Ebe, she took one bite, an' den she frew dat apple away.
10135{_}1933{_}'Wot you mean, you triflin' sarpint,' says she, 'a fotchin' me dat apple wot ain't good fur nuffin but ter make cider wid?'
10135{_}1934{_}Den de sarpint he go fotch her a yaller apple, an' she took one bite, an' den says she: 'Go 'long wid ye, you fool sarpint, wot you fotch me dat June apple wot ain't got no taste to it?'
10135{_}1935{_}Den de sarpint he think she like sumpin' sharp, an' he fotch her a green apple.
10135{_}1936{_}She takes one bite ob it, an' den she frows it at his head, an' sings out: 'Is you 'spectin' me to gib dat apple to yer Uncle Adam an' gib him de colic?'
10135{_}1937{_}Den de debbil he fotch her a lady-apple, but she say she won't take no sich triflin' nubbins as dat to her husban', an' she took one bite ob it, an' frew it away.
10135{_}1938{_}Den he go fotch her two udder kin' ob apples, one yaller wid red stripes, an' de udder one red on one side an' green on de udder--mighty good-lookin' apples, too--de kin' you git two dollars a bar'l fur at the store.
10135{_}1939{_}But Ebe, she wouldn't hab neider ob 'em, an' when she done took one bite out ob each one, she frew it away.
10135{_}1940{_}Den de ole debbil-sarpint, he scratch he head, an' he say to hese'f: 'Dis yer Ebe, she pow'ful 'ticklar 'bout her apples.
10135{_}1941{_}Reckin I'll have ter wait till after fros', an' fotch her a real good one,' An' he done wait till after fros', and then he fotch her a' Albemarle pippin, an' when she took one bite ob dat, she jus' go 'long an' eat it all up, core, seeds, an' all.
10135{_}1942{_}'Look h'yar, sarpint,' says she, 'hab you got anudder ob dem apples in your pocket?'
10135{_}1943{_}An' den he tuk one out, an' gib it to her.
10135{_}1944{_}''Cuse me,' says she, 'I's gwine ter look up Adam, an' ef he don' want ter know war de tree is wot dese apples grow on, you can hab him fur a corn-field han'.'
10135{_}1945{_}"An' now, my dear brev'ren," said Brother Peter, "while I was a-turnin' dis subjec' ober in my min', an' wonderin' how de women come ter hab jus' seben debbils apiece, I done reckerleck dat bit ob Scripter wot I heerd at Kyarter's Mills, an' I reckon dat 'splains how de debbils got inter woman.
10135{_}1946{_}De sarpint he done fotch mudder Ebe seben apples, an' ebery one she take a bite out of gib her a debbil."
10135{_}1947{_}As might have been expected, this sermon produced a great sensation, and made a deep impression on the congregation.
10135{_}1948{_}As a rule, the men were tolerably well satisfied with it; and when the services were over many of them made it the occasion of shy but very plainly pointed remarks to their female friends and relatives.
10135{_}1949{_}But the women did not like it at all.
10135{_}1950{_}Some of them became angry, and talked very forcibly, and feelings of indignation soon spread among all the sisters of the church.
10135{_}1951{_}If their minister had seen fit to stay at home and preach a sermon like this to his own wife (who, it may be remarked, was not present on this occasion), it would have been well enough, provided he had made no allusions to outsiders; but to come there and preach such things to them was entirely too much for their endurance.
10135{_}1952{_}Each one of the women knew she had not seven devils, and only a few of them would admit of the possibility of any of the others being possessed by quite so many.
10135{_}1953{_}Their preacher's explanation of the manner in which every woman came to be possessed of just so many devils appeared to them of little importance.
10135{_}1954{_}What they objected to was the fundamental doctrine of his sermon, which was based on his assertion that the Bible declared every woman had seven devils.
10135{_}1955{_}They were not willing to believe that the Bible said any such thing.
10135{_}1956{_}Some of them went so far as to state it was their opinion that Uncle Pete had got this fool notion from some of the lawyers at the court-house when he was on a jury a month or so before.
10135{_}1957{_}It was quite noticeable that, although Sunday afternoon had scarcely begun, the majority of the women of the congregation called their minister Uncle Pete.
10135{_}1958{_}This was very strong evidence of a sudden decline in his popularity.
10135{_}1959{_}Some of the more vigorous-minded women, not seeing their minister among the other people in the clearing in front of the log church, went to look for him, but he was not to be found.
10135{_}1960{_}His wife had ordered him to be home early, and soon after the congregation had been dismissed he departed by a short cut through the woods.
10135{_}1961{_}That afternoon an irate committee, composed principally of women, but including also a few men who had expressed disbelief in the new doctrine, arrived at the cabin of their preacher, but found there only his wife, cross-grained old Aunt Rebecca.
10135{_}1962{_}She informed them that her husband was not at home.
10135{_}1963{_}"He's done 'gaged hisse'f," she said, "ter cut an' haul wood fur Kunnel Martin ober on Little Mount'n fur de whole ob nex' week.
10135{_}1964{_}It's fourteen or thirteen mile' from h'yar, an' ef he'd started ter-morrer mawnm', he'd los' a'mos' a whole day.
10135{_}1965{_}'Sides dat, I done tole him dat ef he git dar ter-night he'd have his supper frowed in.
10135{_}1966{_}Wot you all want wid him?
10135{_}1967{_}Gwine to pay him fur preachin'?"
10135{_}1968{_}Any such intention as this was instantaneously denied, and Aunt Rebecca was informed of the subject upon which her visitors had come to have a very plain talk with her husband.
10135{_}1969{_}Strange to say, the announcement of the new and startling dogma had apparently no disturbing effect upon Aunt Rebecca.
10135{_}1970{_}On the contrary, the old woman seemed rather to enjoy the news.
10135{_}1971{_}"Reckin he oughter know all 'bout dat," she said.
10135{_}1972{_}"He's done had three wives, an' he ain't got rid o' dis one yit."
10135{_}1973{_}Judging from her chuckles and waggings of the head when she made this remark, it might be imagined that Aunt Rebecca was rather proud of the fact that her husband thought her capable of exhibiting a different kind of diabolism every day in the week.
10135{_}1974{_}The leader of the indignant church-members was Susan Henry; a mulatto woman of a very independent turn of mind.
10135{_}1975{_}She prided herself that she never worked in anybody's house but her own, and this immunity from outside service gave her a certain pre-eminence among her sisters.
10135{_}1976{_}Not only did Susan share the general resentment with which the startling statement of old Peter had been received, but she felt that its promulgation had affected her position in the community.
10135{_}1977{_}If every woman was possessed by seven devils, then, in this respect, she was no better nor worse than any of the others; and at this her proud heart rebelled.
10135{_}1978{_}If the preacher had said some women had eight devils and others six, it would have been better.
10135{_}1979{_}She might then have made a mental arrangement in regard to her relative position which would have somewhat consoled her.
10135{_}1980{_}But now there was no chance for that.
10135{_}1981{_}The words of the preacher had equally debased all women.
10135{_}1982{_}A meeting of the disaffected church-members was held the next night at Susan Henry's cabin, or rather in the little yard about it, for the house was not large enough to hold the people who attended it.
10135{_}1983{_}The meeting was not regularly organized, but everybody said what he or she had to say, and the result was a great deal of clamor, and a general increase of indignation against Uncle Pete.
10135{_}1984{_}"Look h'yar!"
10135{_}1985{_}cried Susan, at the end of some energetic remarks, "is dar enny pusson h'yar who kin count up figgers?"
10135{_}1986{_}Inquiries on the subject ran through the crowd, and in a few moments a black boy, about fourteen, was pushed forward as an expert in arithmetic.
10135{_}1987{_}"Now, you Jim," said Susan, "you's been, to school, an' you kin count up figgers.
10135{_}1988{_}'Cordin' ter de chu'ch books dar's forty-seben women b'longin' to our meetin', an' ef each one ob dem dar has got seben debbils in her, I jus' wants you ter tell me how many debbils come to chu'ch ebery clear Sunday ter hear dat ole Uncle Pete preach."
10135{_}1989{_}This view of the case created a sensation, and much interest was shown in the result of Jim's calculations, which were made by the aid of a back of an old letter and a piece of pencil furnished by Susan.
10135{_}1990{_}The result was at last announced as three hundred and nineteen, which, although not precisely correct, was near enough to satisfy the company.
10135{_}1991{_}"Now, you jus' turn dat ober in you all's minds," said Susan.
10135{_}1992{_}"More'n free hundred debbils in chu'ch ebery Sunday, an' we women fotchin 'em.
10135{_}1993{_}Does anybody s'pose I's gwine ter b'lieve dat fool talk?"
10135{_}1994{_}A middle-aged man now lifted up his voice and said: "I's been thinkin' ober dis h'yar matter and I's 'cluded dat p'r'aps de words ob de preacher was used in a figgeratous form o' sense.
10135{_}1995{_}P'r'aps de seben debbils meant chillun."
10135{_}1996{_}These remarks were received with no favor by the assemblage.
10135{_}1997{_}"Oh, you git out!"
10135{_}1998{_}cried Susan.
10135{_}1999{_}"Your ole woman's got seben chillun, shore 'nuf, an' I s'pec' dey's all debbils.
10135{_}2000{_}But dem sent'ments don't apply ter all de udder women h'yar, 'tic'larly ter dem dar young uns wot ain't married yit."
10135{_}2001{_}This was good logic, but the feeling on the subject proved to be even stronger, for the mothers in the company became so angry at their children being considered devils that for a time there seemed to be danger of an Amazonian attack on the unfortunate speaker.
10135{_}2002{_}This was averted, but a great deal of uproar now ensued, and it was the general feeling that something ought to be done to show the deep-seated resentment with which the horrible charge against the mothers and sisters of the congregation had been met.
10135{_}2003{_}Many violent propositions were made, some of the younger men going so far as to offer to burn down the church.
10135{_}2004{_}It was finally agreed, quite unanimously, that old Peter should be unceremoniously ousted from his place in the pulpit which he had filled so many years.
10135{_}2005{_}As the week passed on, some of the older men of the congregation who had friendly feelings toward their old companion and preacher talked the matter over among themselves, and afterward, with many of their fellow-members, succeeded at last in gaining the general consent that Uncle Pete should be allowed a chance to explain himself, and give his grounds and reasons for his astounding statement in regard to womankind.
10135{_}2006{_}If he could show biblical authority for this, of course nothing more could be said.
10135{_}2007{_}But if he could not, then he must get down from the pulpit, and sit for the rest of his life on a back seat of the church.
10135{_}2008{_}This proposition met with the more favor, because even those who were most indignant had an earnest curiosity to know what the old man would say for himself.
10135{_}2009{_}During all this time of angry discussion, good old Peter was quietly and calmly cutting and hauling wood on the Little Mountain.
10135{_}2010{_}His mind was in a condition of great comfort and peace, for not only had he been able to rid himself, in his last sermon, of many of the hard thoughts concerning women that had been gathering themselves together for years, but his absence from home had given him a holiday from the harassments of Aunt Rebecca's tongue, so that no new notions of woman's culpability had risen within him.
10135{_}2011{_}He had dismissed the subject altogether, and had been thinking over a sermon regarding baptism, which he thought he could make convincing to certain of the younger members of his congregation.
10135{_}2012{_}He arrived at home very late on Saturday night, and retired to his simple couch without knowing anything of the terrible storm which had been gathering through the week, and which was to burst upon him on the morrow.
10135{_}2013{_}But the next morning, long before church time, he received warning enough of what was going to happen.
10135{_}2014{_}Individuals and deputations gathered in and about his cabin--some to tell him all that had been said and done; some to inform him what was expected of him; some to stand about and look at him; some to scold; some to denounce; but, alas!
10135{_}2015{_}not one to encourage; nor one to call him "Brudder Pete," that Sunday appellation dear to his ears.
10135{_}2016{_}But the old man possessed a stubborn soul, not easily to be frightened.
10135{_}2017{_}"Wot I says in de pulpit," he remarked, "I'll 'splain in de pulpit, an' you all ud better git 'long to de chu'ch, an' when de time fur de sarvice come, I'll be dar."
10135{_}2018{_}This advice was not promptly acted upon, but in the course of half an hour nearly all the villagers and loungers had gone off to the church in the woods; and when Uncle Peter had put on his high black hat, somewhat battered, but still sufficiently clerical looking for that congregation, and had given something of a polish to his cowhide shoes, he betook himself by the accustomed path to the log building where he had so often held forth to his people.
10135{_}2019{_}As soon as he entered the church he was formally instructed by a committee of the leading members that before he began to open the services, he must make it plain to the congregation that what he had said on the preceding Sunday about every woman being possessed by seven devils was Scripture truth, and not mere wicked nonsense out of his own brain.
10135{_}2020{_}If he could not do that, they wanted no more praying or preaching from him.
10135{_}2021{_}Uncle Peter made no answer, but, ascending the little pulpit, he put his hat on the bench behind him where it was used to repose, took out his red cotton handkerchief and blew his nose in his accustomed way, and looked about him.
10135{_}2022{_}The house was crowded.
10135{_}2023{_}Even Aunt Rebecca was there.
10135{_}2024{_}After a deliberate survey of his audience, the preacher spoke: "Brev'eren an' sisters, I see afore me Brudder Bill Hines, who kin read de Bible, an' has got one.
10135{_}2025{_}Ain't dat so, Brudder?"
10135{_}2026{_}Bill Hines having nodded and modestly grunted assent, the preacher continued.
10135{_}2027{_}"An' dars' Ann' Priscilla's boy, Jake, who ain't a brudder yit, though he's plenty old 'nuf, min', I tell ye; an' he kin read de Bible, fus' rate, an' has read it ter me ober an' ober ag'in.
10135{_}2028{_}Ain't dat so, Jake?"
10135{_}2029{_}Jake grinned, nodded, and hung his head, very uncomfortable at being thus publicly pointed out.
10135{_}2030{_}"An' dar's good ole Aun' Patty, who knows more Scripter dan ennybuddy h'yar, havin' been teached by de little gals from Kunnel Jasper's an' by dere mudders afore 'em.
10135{_}2031{_}I reckin she know' de hull Bible straight froo, from de Garden of Eden to de New Jerus'lum.
10135{_}2032{_}An' dar are udders h'yar who knows de Scripters, some one part an' some anudder.
10135{_}2033{_}Now I axes ebery one ob you all wot know de Scripters ef he don' 'member how de Bible tells how our Lor' when he was on dis yearth cas' seben debbils out o' Mary Magdalum?"
10135{_}2034{_}A murmur of assent came from the congregation, Most of them remembered that.
10135{_}2035{_}"But did enny ob you ebber read, or hab read to you, dat he ebber cas' 'em out o' enny udder woman?"
10135{_}2036{_}Negative grunts and shakes of the head signified that nobody had ever heard of this.
10135{_}2037{_}"Well, den," said the preacher, gazing blandly around, "all de udder women got 'em yit."
10135{_}2038{_}A deep silence fell upon the assembly, and in a few moments an elderly member arose.
10135{_}2039{_}"Brudder Pete," he said, "I reckin you mought as well gib out de hyme."
10135{_}2040{_}A DOG'S TALE[1]  [Footnote 1: 1903]  _Mark Twain_ (1835)   I  My father was a St. Bernard, my mother was a collie, but I am a Presbyterian.
10135{_}2041{_}This is what my mother told me; I do not know these nice distinctions myself.
10135{_}2042{_}To me they are only fine large words meaning nothing.
10135{_}2043{_}My mother had a fondness for such; she liked to say them, and see other dogs look surprised and envious, as wondering how she got so much education.
10135{_}2044{_}But, indeed, it was not real education; it was only show: she got the words by listening in the dining-room and drawing-room when there was company, and by going with the children to Sunday-school and listening there; and whenever she heard a large word she said it over to herself many times, and so was able to keep it until there was a dogmatic gathering in the neighborhood, then she would get it off, and surprise and distress them all, from pocket-pup to mastiff, which rewarded her for all her trouble.
10135{_}2045{_}If there was a stranger he was nearly sure to be suspicious, and when he got his breath again he would ask her what it meant.
10135{_}2046{_}And she always told him.
10135{_}2047{_}He was never expecting this, but thought he would catch her; so when she told him, he was the one that looked ashamed, whereas he had thought it was going to be she.
10135{_}2048{_}The others were always waiting for this, and glad of it and proud of her, for they knew what was going to happen, because they had had experience.
10135{_}2049{_}When she told the meaning of a big word they were all so taken up with admiration that it never occurred to any dog to doubt if it was the right one; and that was natural, because, for one thing, she answered up so promptly that it seemed like a dictionary speaking, and for another thing, where could they find out whether it was right or not?
10135{_}2050{_}for she was the only cultivated dog there was.
10135{_}2051{_}By-and-by, when I was older, she brought home the word Unintellectual, one time, and worked it pretty hard all the week at different gatherings, making much unhappiness and despondency; and it was at this time that I noticed that during that week she was asked for the meaning at eight different assemblages, and flashed out a fresh definition every time, which showed me that she had more presence of mind than culture, though I said nothing, of course.
10135{_}2052{_}She had one word which she always kept on hand, and ready, like a life-preserver, a kind of emergency word to strap on when she was likely to get washed overboard in a sudden way--that was the word Synonymous.
10135{_}2053{_}When she happened to fetch out a long word which had had its day weeks before and its prepared meanings gone to her dump-pile, if there was a stranger there of course it knocked him groggy for a couple of minutes, then he would come to, and by that time she would be away down the wind on another tack, and not expecting anything; so when he'd hail and ask her to cash in, I (the only dog on the inside of her game) could see her canvas flicker a moment,--but only just a moment,--then it would belly out taut and full, and she would say, as calm as a summer's day, "It's synonymous with supererogation," or some godless long reptile of a word like that, and go placidly about and skim away on the next tack, perfectly comfortable, you know, and leave that stranger looking profane and embarrassed, and the initiated slatting the floor with their tails in unison and their faces transfigured with a holy joy.
10135{_}2054{_}And it was the same with phrases.
10135{_}2055{_}She would drag home a whole phrase, if it had a grand sound, and play it six nights and two matinees, and explain it a new way every time,--which she had to, for all she cared for was the phrase; she wasn't interested in what it meant, and knew those dogs hadn't wit enough to catch her, anyway.
10135{_}2056{_}Yes, she was a daisy!
10135{_}2057{_}She got so she wasn't afraid of anything, she had such confidence in the ignorance of those creatures.
10135{_}2058{_}She even brought anecdotes that she had heard the family and the dinner guests laugh and shout over; and as a rule she got the nub of one chestnut hitched onto another chestnut, where, of course, it didn't fit and hadn't any point; and when she delivered the nub she fell over and rolled on the floor and laughed and barked in the most insane way, while I could see that she was wondering to herself why it didn't seem as funny as it did when she first heard it.
10135{_}2059{_}But no harm was done; the others rolled and barked too, privately ashamed of themselves for not seeing the point, and never suspecting that the fault was not with them and there wasn't any to see.
10135{_}2060{_}You can see by these things that she was of a rather vain and frivolous character; still, she had virtues, and enough to make up, I think.
10135{_}2061{_}She had a kind heart and gentle ways, and never harbored resentments for injuries done her, but put them easily out of her mind and forgot them; and she taught her children her kindly way, and from her we learned also to be brave and prompt in time of danger, and not to run away, but face the peril that threatened friend or stranger, and help him the best we could without stopping to think what the cost might be to us.
10135{_}2062{_}And she taught us, not by words only, but by example, and that is the best way and the surest and the most lasting.
10135{_}2063{_}Why, the brave things she did, the splendid things!
10135{_}2064{_}she was just a soldier; and so modest about it--well, you couldn't help admiring her, and you couldn't help imitating her; not even a King Charles spaniel could remain entirely despicable in her society.
10135{_}2065{_}So, as you see, there was more to her than her education.
10135{_}2066{_}II  When I was well grown, at last, I was sold and taken away, and I never saw her again.
10135{_}2067{_}She was broken-hearted, and so was I, and we cried; but she comforted me as well as she could, and said we were sent into this world for a wise and good purpose, and must do our duties without repining, take our life as we might find it, live it for the best good of others, and never mind about the results; they were not our affair.
10135{_}2068{_}She said men who did like this would have a noble and beautiful reward by-and-by in another world, and although we animals would not go there, to do well and right without reward would give to our brief lives a worthiness and dignity which in itself would be a reward.
10135{_}2069{_}She had gathered these things from time to time when she had gone to the Sunday-school with the children, and had laid them up in her memory more carefully than she had done with those other words and phrases; and she had studied them deeply, for her good and ours.
10135{_}2070{_}One may see by this that she had a wise and thoughtful head, for all there was so much lightness and vanity in it.
10135{_}2071{_}So we said our farewells, and looked our last upon each other through our tears; and the last thing she said--keeping it for the last to make me remember it the better, I think--was, "In memory of me, when there is a time of danger to another do not think of yourself, think of your mother, and do as she would do."
10135{_}2072{_}Do you think I could forget that?
10135{_}2073{_}No.
10135{_}2074{_}III  It was such a charming home!--my new one; a fine great house, with pictures, and delicate decorations, and rich furniture, and no gloom anywhere, but all the wilderness of dainty colors lit up with flooding sunshine; and the spacious grounds around it, and the great garden--oh, greensward, and noble trees, and flowers, no end!
10135{_}2075{_}And I was the same as a member of the family; and they loved me, and petted me, and did not give me a new name, but called me by my old one that was dear to me because my mother had given it me--Aileen Mavourneen.
10135{_}2076{_}She got it out of a song; and the Grays knew that song, and said it was a beautiful name.
10135{_}2077{_}Mrs. Gray was thirty, and so sweet and so lovely, you cannot imagine it; and Sadie was ten, and just like her mother, just a darling slender little copy of her, with auburn tails down her back, and short frocks; and the baby was a year old, and plump and dimpled, and fond of me, and never could get enough of hauling on my tail, and hugging me, and laughing out its innocent happiness; and Mr. Gray was thirty-eight, and tall and slender and handsome, a little bald in front, alert, quick in his movements, businesslike, prompt, decided, unsentimental, and with that kind of trim-chiselled face that just seems to glint and sparkle with frosty intellectuality!
10135{_}2078{_}He was a renowned scientist.
10135{_}2079{_}I do not know what the word means, but my mother would know how to use it and get effects.
10135{_}2080{_}She would know how to depress a rat-terrier with it and make a lap-dog look sorry he came.
10135{_}2081{_}But that is not the best one; the best one was Laboratory.
10135{_}2082{_}My mother could organize a Trust on that one that would skin the tax-collars off the whole herd.
10135{_}2083{_}The laboratory was not a book, or a picture, or a place to wash your hands in, as the college president's dog said--no, that is the lavatory; the laboratory is quite different, and is filled with jars, and bottles, and electrics, and wires, and strange machines; and every week other scientists came there and sat in the place, and used the machines, and discussed, and made what they called experiments and discoveries; and often I came, too, and stood around and listened, and tried to learn, for the sake of my mother, and in loving memory of her, although it was a pain to me, as realizing what she was losing out of her life and I gaining nothing at all; for try as I might, I was never able to make anything out of it at all.
10135{_}2084{_}Other times I lay on the floor in the mistress's workroom and slept, she gently using me for a footstool, knowing it pleased me, for it was a caress; other times I spent an hour in the nursery, and got well tousled and made happy; other times I watched by the crib there, when the baby was asleep and the nurse out for a few minutes on the baby's affairs; other times I romped and raced through the grounds and the garden with Sadie till we were tired out, then slumbered on the grass in the shade of a tree while she read her book; other times I went visiting among the neighbor dogs,--for there were some most pleasant ones not far away, and one very handsome and courteous and graceful one, a curly haired Irish setter by the name of Robin Adair, who was a Presbyterian like me, and belonged to the Scotch minister.
10135{_}2085{_}The servants in our house were all kind to me and were fond of me, and so, as you see, mine was a pleasant life.
10135{_}2086{_}There could not be a happier dog than I was, nor a gratefuller one.
10135{_}2087{_}I will say this for myself, for it is only the truth: I tried in all ways to do well and right, and honor my mother's memory and her teachings, and earn the happiness that had come to me, as best I could.
10135{_}2088{_}By-and-by came my little puppy, and then my cup was full, my happiness was perfect.
10135{_}2089{_}It was the dearest little waddling thing, and so smooth and soft and velvety, and had such cunning little awkward paws, and such affectionate eyes, and such a sweet and innocent face; and it made me so proud to see how the children and their mother adored it, and fondled it, and exclaimed over every little wonderful thing it did.
10135{_}2090{_}It did seem to me that life was just too lovely to--  Then came the winter.
10135{_}2091{_}One day I was standing a watch in the nursery.
10135{_}2092{_}That is to say, I was asleep on the bed.
10135{_}2093{_}The baby was asleep in the crib, which was alongside the bed, on the side next the fireplace.
10135{_}2094{_}It was the kind of crib that has a lofty tent over it made of a gauzy stuff that you can see through.
10135{_}2095{_}The nurse was out, and we two sleepers were alone.
10135{_}2096{_}A spark from the wood-fire was shot out, and it lit on the slope of the tent.
10135{_}2097{_}I suppose a quiet interval followed, then a scream from the baby woke me, and there was that tent flaming up toward the ceiling!
10135{_}2098{_}Before I could think, I sprang to the floor in my fright, and in a second was half-way to the door; but in the next half-second my mother's farewell was sounding in my ears, and I was back on the bed again.
10135{_}2099{_}I reached my head through the flames and dragged the baby out by the waistband, and tugged it along, and we fell to the floor together in a cloud of smoke; I snatched a new hold, and dragged the screaming little creature along and out at the door and around the bend of the hall, and was still tugging away, all excited and happy and proud, when the master's voice shouted:  "Begone, you cursed beast!"
10135{_}2100{_}and I jumped to save myself; but he was wonderfully quick, and chased me up, striking furiously at me with his cane, I dodging this way and that, in terror, and at last a strong blow fell upon my left fore-leg, which made me shriek and fall, for the moment, helpless; the cane went up for another blow, but never descended, for the nurse's voice rang wildly out, "The nursery's on fire!"
10135{_}2101{_}and the master rushed away in that direction, and my other bones were saved.
10135{_}2102{_}The pain was cruel, but, no matter, I must not lose any time; he might come back at any moment; so I limped on three legs to the other end of the hall, where there was a dark little stairway leading up into a garret where old boxes and such things were kept, as I had heard say, and where people seldom went.
10135{_}2103{_}I managed to climb up there, then I searched my way through the dark among the piles of things, and hid in the secretest place I could find.
10135{_}2104{_}It was foolish to be afraid there, yet still I was; so afraid that I held in and hardly even whimpered, though it would have been such a comfort to whimper, because that eases the pain, you know.
10135{_}2105{_}But I could lick my leg, and that did me some good.
10135{_}2106{_}For half an hour there was a commotion down-stairs, and shoutings, and rushing footsteps, and then there was quiet again.
10135{_}2107{_}Quiet for some minutes, and that was grateful to my spirit, for then my fears began to go down; and fears are worse than pains,--oh, much worse.
10135{_}2108{_}Then came a sound that froze me!
10135{_}2109{_}They were calling me--calling me by name--hunting for me!
10135{_}2110{_}It was muffled by distance, but that could not take the terror out of it, and it was the most dreadful sound to me that I had ever heard.
10135{_}2111{_}It went all about, everywhere, down there: along the halls, through all the rooms, in both stories, and in the basement and the cellar; then outside, and further and further away--then back, and all about the house again, and I thought it would never, never stop.
10135{_}2112{_}But at last it did, hours and hours after the vague twilight of the garret had long ago been blotted out by black darkness.
10135{_}2113{_}Then in that blessed stillness my terror fell little by little away, and I was at peace and slept.
10135{_}2114{_}It was a good rest I had, but I woke before the twilight had come again.
10135{_}2115{_}I was feeling fairly comfortable, and I could think out a plan now.
10135{_}2116{_}I made a very good one; which was, to creep down, all the way down the back stairs, and hide behind the cellar door, and slip out and escape when the iceman came at dawn, while he was inside filling the refrigerator; then I would hide all day, and start on my journey when night came; my journey to--well, anywhere where they would not know me and betray me to the master.
10135{_}2117{_}I was feeling almost cheerful now; then suddenly I thought, Why, what would life be without my puppy!
10135{_}2118{_}That was despair.
10135{_}2119{_}There was no plan for me; I saw that; I must stay where I was; stay, and wait, and take what might come--it was not my affair; that was what life is--my mother had said it.
10135{_}2120{_}Then--well, then the calling began again!
10135{_}2121{_}All my sorrows came back.
10135{_}2122{_}I said to myself, the master will never forgive.
10135{_}2123{_}I did not know what I had done to make him so bitter and so unforgiving, yet I judged it was something a dog could not understand, but which was clear to a man and dreadful.
10135{_}2124{_}They called and called--days and nights, it seemed to me.
10135{_}2125{_}So long that the hunger and thirst near drove me mad, and I recognized that I was getting very weak.
10135{_}2126{_}When you are this way you sleep a great deal, and I did.
10135{_}2127{_}Once I woke in an awful fright--it seemed to me that the calling was right there in the garret!
10135{_}2128{_}And so it was: it was Sadie's voice, and she was crying; my name was falling from her lips all broken, poor thing, and I could not believe my ears for the joy of it when I heard her say,  "Come back to us--oh, come back to us, and forgive--it is all so sad without our--"  I broke in with _such_ a grateful little yelp, and the next moment Sadie was plunging and stumbling through the darkness and the lumber and shouting for the family to hear, "She's found!
10135{_}2129{_}she's found!"
10135{_}2130{_}The days that followed--well, they were wonderful.
10135{_}2131{_}The mother and Sadie and the servants--why, they just seemed to worship me.
10135{_}2132{_}They couldn't seem to make me a bed that was fine enough; and as for food, they couldn't be satisfied with anything but game and delicacies that were out of season; and every day the friends and neighbors flocked in to hear about my heroism--that was the name they called it by, and it means agriculture.
10135{_}2133{_}I remember my mother pulling it on a kennel once, and explaining it that way, but didn't say what agriculture was, except that it was synonymous with intramural incandescence; and a dozen times a day Mrs. Gray and Sadie would tell the tale to new-comers, and say I risked my life to save the baby's, and both of us had burns to prove it, and then the company would pass me around and pet me and exclaim about me, and you could see the pride in the eyes of Sadie and her mother; and when the people wanted to know what made me limp, they looked ashamed and changed the subject, and sometimes when people hunted them this way and that way with questions about it, it looked to me as if they were going to cry.
10135{_}2134{_}And this was not all the glory; no, the master's friends came, a whole twenty of the most distinguished people, and had me in the laboratory, and discussed me as if I was a kind of discovery; and some of them said it was wonderful in a dumb beast, the finest exhibition of instinct they could call to mind; but the master said, with vehemence, "It's far above instinct; it's _reason_, and many a man, privileged to be saved and go with you and me to a better world by right of its possession, has less of it than this poor silly quadruped that's foreordained to perish"; and then he laughed, and said, "Why, look at me--I'm a sarcasm!
10135{_}2135{_}Bless you, with all my grand intelligence, the only thing I inferred was that the dog had gone mad and was destroying the child, whereas but for the beast's intelligence--it's _reason_, I tell you!--the child would have perished!"
10135{_}2136{_}They disputed and disputed, and _I_ was the very centre and subject of it all, and I wished my mother could know that this grand honor had come to me; it would have made her proud.
10135{_}2137{_}Then they discussed optics, as they called it, and whether a certain injury to the brain would produce blindness or not, but they could not agree about it, and said they must test it by experiment by-and-by; and next they discussed plants, and that interested me, because in the summer Sadie and I had planted seeds--I helped her dig the holes, you know,--and after days and days a little shrub or a flower came up there, and it was a wonder how that could happen; but it did, and I wished I could talk,--I would have told those people about it and shown them how much I knew, and been all alive with the subject; but I didn't care for the optics; it was dull, and when they came back to it again it bored me, and I went to sleep.
10135{_}2138{_}Pretty soon it was spring, and sunny and pleasant and lovely, and the sweet mother and the children patted me and the puppy good-bye, and went away on a journey and a visit to their kin, and the master wasn't any company for us, but we played together and had good times, and the servants were kind and friendly, so we got along quite happily and counted the days and waited for the family.
10135{_}2139{_}And one day those men came again, and said now for the test, and they took the puppy to the laboratory, and I limped three-leggedly along, too, feeling proud, for any attention shown the puppy was a pleasure to me, of course.
10135{_}2140{_}They discussed and experimented, and then suddenly the puppy shrieked, and they set him on the floor, and he went staggering around, with his head all bloody, and the master clapped his hands, and shouted:  "There, I've won--confess it!
10135{_}2141{_}He's as blind as a bat!"
10135{_}2142{_}And they all said,  "It's so--you've proved your theory, and suffering humanity owes you a great debt from henceforth," and they crowded around him, and wrung his hand cordially and thankfully, and praised him.
10135{_}2143{_}But I hardly saw or heard these things, for I ran at once to my little darling, and snuggled close to it where it lay, and licked the blood, and it put its head against mine, whimpering softly, and I knew in my heart it was a comfort to it in its pain and trouble to feel its mother's touch, though it could not see me.
10135{_}2144{_}Then it drooped down, presently, and its little velvet nose rested upon the floor, and it was still, and did not move any more.
10135{_}2145{_}Soon the master stopped discussing a moment, and rang in the footman, and said, "Bury it in the far corner of the garden," and then went on with the discussion, and I trotted after the footman, very happy and grateful, for I knew the puppy was out of its pain now, because it was asleep.
10135{_}2146{_}We went far down the garden to the furthest end, where the children and the nurse and the puppy and I used to play in the summer in the shade of a great elm, and there the footman dug a hole, and I saw he was going to plant the puppy, and I was glad, because it would grow and come up a fine handsome dog, like Robin Adair, and be a beautiful surprise for the family when they came home; so I tried to help him dig, but my lame leg was no good, being stiff, you know, and you have to have two, or it is no use.
10135{_}2147{_}When the footman had finished and covered little Robin up, he patted my head, and there were tears in his eyes, and he said, "Poor little doggie, you SAVED _his_ child."
10135{_}2148{_}I have watched two whole weeks, and he doesn't come up!
10135{_}2149{_}This last week a fright has been stealing upon me.
10135{_}2150{_}I think there is something terrible about this.
10135{_}2151{_}I do not know what it is, but the fear makes me sick, and I cannot eat, though the servants bring me the best of food; and they pet me so, and even come in the night, and cry, and say, "Poor doggie--do give it up and come home; _don't_ break our hearts!"
10135{_}2152{_}and all this terrifies me the more, and makes me sure something has happened.
10135{_}2153{_}And I am so weak; since yesterday I cannot stand on my feet any more.
10135{_}2154{_}And within this hour the servants, looking toward the sun where it was sinking out of sight and the night chill coming on, said things I could not understand, but they carried something cold to my heart.
10135{_}2155{_}"Those poor creatures!
10135{_}2156{_}They do not suspect.
10135{_}2157{_}They will come home in the morning, and eagerly ask for the little doggie that did the brave deed, and who of us will be strong enough to say the truth to them: 'The humble little friend is gone where go the beasts that perish.'"
10135{_}2158{_}THE OUTCASTS OF POKER FLAT[1]  [Footnote 1: From _The Luck of Roaring Camp_.
10135{_}2159{_}1871.]
10135{_}2160{_}_Bret Harte_ (1839-1902)   As Mr. John Oakhurst, gambler, stepped into the main street of Poker Flat on the morning of the 23d of November, 1850, he was conscious of a change in its moral atmosphere since the preceding night.
10135{_}2161{_}Two or three men, conversing earnestly together, ceased as he approached, and exchanged significant glances.
10135{_}2162{_}There was a Sabbath lull in the air, which, in a settlement unused to Sabbath influences, looked ominous.
10135{_}2163{_}Mr. Oakhurst's calm, handsome face betrayed small concern in these indications.
10135{_}2164{_}Whether he was conscious of any predisposing cause was another question.
10135{_}2165{_}"I reckon they're after somebody," he reflected; "likely it's me."
10135{_}2166{_}He returned to his pocket the handkerchief with which he had been whipping away the red dust of Poker Flat from his neat boots, and quietly discharged his mind of any further conjecture.
10135{_}2167{_}In point of fact, Poker Flat was "after somebody."
10135{_}2168{_}It had lately suffered the loss of several thousand dollars, two valuable horses, and a prominent citizen.
10135{_}2169{_}It was experiencing a spasm of virtuous reaction, quite as lawless and ungovernable as any of the acts that had provoked it.
10135{_}2170{_}A secret committee had determined to rid the town of all improper persons.
10135{_}2171{_}This was done permanently in regard of two men who were then hanging from the boughs of a sycamore in the gulch, and temporarily in the banishment of certain other objectionable characters.
10135{_}2172{_}I regret to say that some of these were ladies.
10135{_}2173{_}It is but due to the sex, however, to state that their impropriety was professional, and it was only in such easily established standards of evil that Poker Flat ventured to sit in judgment.
10135{_}2174{_}Mr. Oakhurst was right in supposing that he was included in this category.
10135{_}2175{_}A few of the committee had urged hanging him as a possible example, and a sure method of reimbursing themselves from his pockets of the sums he had won from them.
10135{_}2176{_}"It's agin justice," said Jim Wheeler, "to let this yer young man from Roaring Camp--an entire stranger--carry away our money."
10135{_}2177{_}But a crude sentiment of equity residing in the breasts of those who had been fortunate enough to win from Mr. Oakhurst overruled this narrower local prejudice.
10135{_}2178{_}Mr. Oakhurst received his sentence with philosophic calmness, none the less coolly that he was aware of the hesitation of his judges.
10135{_}2179{_}He was too much of a gambler not to accept fate.
10135{_}2180{_}With him life was at best an uncertain game, and he recognized the usual percentage in favor of the dealer.
10135{_}2181{_}A body of armed men accompanied the deported wickedness of Poker Flat to the outskirts of the settlement.
10135{_}2182{_}Besides Mr. Oakhurst, who was known to be a coolly desperate man, and for whose intimidation the armed escort was intended, the expatriated party consisted of a young woman familiarly known as the "Duchess"; another who had won the title of "Mother Shipton"; and "Uncle Billy," a suspected sluice-robber and confirmed drunkard.
10135{_}2183{_}The cavalcade provoked no comments from the spectators, nor was any word uttered by the escort.
10135{_}2184{_}Only when the gulch which marked the uttermost limit of Poker Flat was reached, the leader spoke briefly and to the point.
10135{_}2185{_}The exiles were forbidden to return at the peril of their lives.
10135{_}2186{_}As the escort disappeared, their pent-up feelings found vent in a few hysterical tears from the Duchess, some bad language from Mother Shipton, and a Parthian volley of expletives from Uncle Billy.
10135{_}2187{_}The philosophic Oakhurst alone remained silent.
10135{_}2188{_}He listened calmly to Mother Shipton's desire to cut somebody's heart out, to the repeated statements of the Duchess that she would die in the road, and to the alarming oaths that seemed to be bumped out of Uncle Billy as he rode forward.
10135{_}2189{_}With the easy good-humor characteristic of his class, he insisted upon exchanging his own riding-horse, "Five Spot," for the sorry mule which the Duchess rode.
10135{_}2190{_}But even this act did not draw the party into any closer sympathy.
10135{_}2191{_}The young woman readjusted her somewhat draggled plumes with a feeble, faded coquetry; Mother Shipton eyed the possessor of "Five Spot" with malevolence, and Uncle Billy included the whole party in one sweeping anathema.
10135{_}2192{_}The road to Sandy Bar--a camp that, not having as yet experienced the regenerating influences of Poker Flat, consequently seemed to offer some invitation to the emigrants--lay over a steep mountain range.
10135{_}2193{_}It was distant a day's severe travel.
10135{_}2194{_}In that advanced season, the party soon passed out of the moist, temperate regions of the foot-hills into the dry, cold, bracing air of the Sierras.
10135{_}2195{_}The trail was narrow and difficult.
10135{_}2196{_}At noon the Duchess, rolling out of her saddle upon the ground, declared her intention of going no farther, and the party halted.
10135{_}2197{_}The spot was singularly wild and impressive.
10135{_}2198{_}A wooded amphitheatre, surrounded on three sides by precipitous cliffs of naked granite, sloped gently toward the crest of another precipice that overlooked the valley.
10135{_}2199{_}It was, undoubtedly, the most suitable spot for a camp, had camping been advisable.
10135{_}2200{_}But Mr. Oakhurst knew that scarcely half the journey to Sandy Bar was accomplished; and the party were not equipped or provisioned for delay.
10135{_}2201{_}This fact he pointed out to his companions curtly, with a philosophic commentary on the folly of "throwing up their hand before the game was played out."
10135{_}2202{_}But they were furnished with liquor, which in this emergency stood them in place of food, fuel, rest, and prescience.
10135{_}2203{_}In spite of his remonstrances, it was not long before they were more or less under its influence.
10135{_}2204{_}Uncle Billy passed rapidly from a bellicose state into one of stupor, the Duchess became maudlin, and Mother Shipton snored.
10135{_}2205{_}Mr. Oakhurst alone remained erect, leaning against a rock, calmly surveying them.
