10135{_}2206{_}Mr. Oakhurst did not drink.
10135{_}2207{_}It interfered with a profession which required coolness, impassiveness, and presence of mind, and, in his own language, he "couldn't afford it."
10135{_}2208{_}As he gazed at his recumbent fellow-exiles, the loneliness begotten of his pariah-trade, his habits of life, his very vices, for the first time seriously oppressed him.
10135{_}2209{_}He bestirred himself in dusting his black clothes, washing his hands and face, and other acts characteristic of his studiously neat habits, and for a moment forgot his annoyance.
10135{_}2210{_}The thought of deserting his weaker and more pitiable companions never perhaps occurred to him.
10135{_}2211{_}Yet he could not help feeling the want of that excitement which, singularly enough, was most conducive to that calm equanimity for which he was notorious.
10135{_}2212{_}He looked at the gloomy walls that rose a thousand feet sheer above the circling pines around him; at the sky, ominously clouded; at the valley below, already deepening into shadow.
10135{_}2213{_}And, doing so, suddenly he heard his own name called.
10135{_}2214{_}A horseman slowly ascended the trail.
10135{_}2215{_}In the fresh, open face of the new-comer Mr. Oakhurst recognized Tom Simson, otherwise known as the "Innocent," of Sandy Bar.
10135{_}2216{_}He had met him some months before over a "little game," and had, with perfect equanimity, won the entire fortune--amounting to some forty dollars--of that guileless youth.
10135{_}2217{_}After the game was finished, Mr. Oakhurst drew the youthful speculator behind the door and thus addressed him: "Tommy, you're a good little man, but you can't gamble worth a cent.
10135{_}2218{_}Don't try it over again."
10135{_}2219{_}He then handed him his money back, pushed him gently from the room, and so made a devoted slave of Tom Simson.
10135{_}2220{_}There was a remembrance of this in his boyish and enthusiastic greeting of Mr. Oakhurst.
10135{_}2221{_}He had started, he said, to go to Poker Flat to seek his fortune.
10135{_}2222{_}"Alone?"
10135{_}2223{_}No, not exactly alone; in fact (a giggle), he had run away with Piney Woods.
10135{_}2224{_}Didn't Mr. Oakhurst remember Piney?
10135{_}2225{_}She that used to wait on the table at the Temperance House?
10135{_}2226{_}They had been engaged a long time, but old Jake Woods had objected, and so they had run away, and were going to Poker Flat to be married, and here they were.
10135{_}2227{_}And they were tired out, and how lucky it was they had found a place to camp, and company.
10135{_}2228{_}All this the Innocent delivered rapidly, while Piney, a stout, comely damsel of fifteen, emerged from behind the pine-tree where she had been blushing unseen, and rode to the side of her lover.
10135{_}2229{_}Mr. Oakhurst seldom troubled himself with sentiment, still less with propriety; but he had a vague idea that the situation was not fortunate.
10135{_}2230{_}He retained, however, his presence of mind sufficiently to kick Uncle Billy, who was about to say something, and Uncle Billy was sober enough to recognize in Mr. Oakhurst's kick a superior power that would not bear trifling.
10135{_}2231{_}He then endeavored to dissuade Tom Simson from delaying further, but in vain.
10135{_}2232{_}He even pointed out the fact that there was no provision, nor means of making a camp.
10135{_}2233{_}But, unluckily, the Innocent met this objection by assuring the party that he was provided with an extra mule loaded with provisions, and by the discovery of a rude attempt at a log-house near the trail.
10135{_}2234{_}"Piney can stay with Mrs. Oakhurst," said the Innocent, pointing to the Duchess, "and I can shift for myself."
10135{_}2235{_}Nothing but Mr. Oakhurst's admonishing foot saved Uncle Billy from bursting into a roar of laughter.
10135{_}2236{_}As it was, he felt compelled to retire up the canon until he could recover his gravity.
10135{_}2237{_}There he confided the joke to the tall pine-trees, with many slaps of his leg, contortions of his face, and the usual profanity.
10135{_}2238{_}But when he returned to the party, he found them seated by a fire--for the air had grown strangely chill and the sky overcast--in apparently amicable conversation.
10135{_}2239{_}Piney was actually talking in an impulsive, girlish fashion to the Duchess, who was listening with an interest and animation she had not shown for many days.
10135{_}2240{_}The Innocent was holding forth, apparently with equal effect, to Mr. Oakhurst and Mother Shipton, who was actually relaxing into amiability.
10135{_}2241{_}"Is this yer a d---d picnic?"
10135{_}2242{_}said Uncle Billy, with inward scorn, as he surveyed the sylvan group, the glancing firelight, and the tethered animals in the foreground.
10135{_}2243{_}Suddenly an idea mingled with the alcoholic fumes that disturbed his brain.
10135{_}2244{_}It was apparently of a jocular nature, for he felt impelled to slap his leg again and cram his fist into his mouth.
10135{_}2245{_}As the shadows crept slowly up the mountain, a slight breeze rocked the tops of the pine-trees, and moaned through their long and gloomy aisles.
10135{_}2246{_}The ruined cabin, patched and covered with pine-boughs, was set apart for the ladies.
10135{_}2247{_}As the lovers parted they unaffectedly exchanged a kiss, so honest and sincere that it might have been heard above the swaying pines.
10135{_}2248{_}The frail Duchess and the malevolent Mother Shipton were probably too stunned to remark upon this last evidence of simplicity, and so turned without a word to the hut.
10135{_}2249{_}The fire was replenished, the men lay down before the door, and in a few minutes were asleep.
10135{_}2250{_}Mr. Oakhurst was a light sleeper.
10135{_}2251{_}Toward morning he awoke benumbed and cold.
10135{_}2252{_}As he stirred the dying fire, the wind, which was now blowing strongly, brought to his cheek that which caused the blood to leave it--snow!
10135{_}2253{_}He started to his feet with the intention of awakening the sleepers, for there was no time to lose.
10135{_}2254{_}But turning to where Uncle Billy had been lying, he found him gone.
10135{_}2255{_}A suspicion leaped to his brain and a curse to his lips.
10135{_}2256{_}He ran to the spot where the mules had been tethered; they were no longer there.
10135{_}2257{_}The tracks were already rapidly disappearing in the snow.
10135{_}2258{_}The momentary excitement brought Mr. Oakhurst back to the fire with his usual calm.
10135{_}2259{_}He did not waken the sleepers.
10135{_}2260{_}The Innocent slumbered peacefully, with a smile on his good-humored, freckled face; the virgin Piney slept beside her frailer sisters as sweetly as though attended by celestial guardians, and Mr. Oakhurst, drawing his blanket over his shoulders, stroked his mustaches and waited for the dawn.
10135{_}2261{_}It came slowly in a whirling mist of snowflakes, that dazzled and confused the eye.
10135{_}2262{_}What could be seen of the landscape appeared magically changed.
10135{_}2263{_}He looked over the valley, and summed up the present and future in two words--"Snowed in!"
10135{_}2264{_}A careful inventory of the provisions, which, fortunately for the party, had been stored within the hut, and so escaped the felonious fingers of Uncle Billy, disclosed the fact that with care and prudence they might last ten days longer.
10135{_}2265{_}"That is," said Mr. Oakhurst, _sotto voce_ to the Innocent, "if you're willing to board us.
10135{_}2266{_}If you ain't--and perhaps you'd better not--you can wait till Uncle Billy gets back with provisions."
10135{_}2267{_}For some occult reason, Mr. Oakhurst could not bring himself to disclose Uncle Billy's rascality, and so offered the hypothesis that he had wandered from the camp and had accidentally stampeded the animals.
10135{_}2268{_}He dropped a warning to the Duchess and Mother Shipton, who of course knew the facts of their associate's defection.
10135{_}2269{_}"They'll find out the truth about us _all_ when they find out anything," he added, significantly, "and there's no good frightening them now."
10135{_}2270{_}Tom Simson not only put all his worldly store at the disposal of Mr. Oakhurst, but seemed to enjoy the prospect of their enforced seclusion.
10135{_}2271{_}"We'll have a good camp for a week, and then the snow'll melt, and we'll all go back together."
10135{_}2272{_}The cheerful gayety of the young man and Mr. Oakhurst's calm infected the others.
10135{_}2273{_}The Innocent, with the aid of pine-boughs, extemporized a thatch for the roofless cabin, and the Duchess directed Piney in the rearrangement of the interior with a taste and tact that opened the blue eyes of that provincial maiden to their fullest extent.
10135{_}2274{_}"I reckon now you're used to fine things at Poker Flat," said Piney.
10135{_}2275{_}The Duchess turned away sharply to conceal something that reddened her cheeks through their professional tint, and Mother Shipton requested Piney not to "chatter."
10135{_}2276{_}But when Mr. Oakhurst returned from a weary search for the trail, he heard the sound of happy laughter echoed from the rocks.
10135{_}2277{_}He stopped in some alarm, and his thoughts first naturally reverted to the whiskey, which he had prudently _cached_.
10135{_}2278{_}"And yet it don't somehow sound like whiskey," said the gambler.
10135{_}2279{_}It was not until he caught sight of the blazing fire through the still blinding storm and the group around it that he settled to the conviction that it was "square fun."
10135{_}2280{_}Whether Mr. Oakhurst had _cached_ his cards with the whiskey as something debarred the free access of the community, I cannot say.
10135{_}2281{_}It was certain that, in Mother Shipton's words, he "didn't say cards once," during that evening.
10135{_}2282{_}Haply the time was beguiled by an accordion, produced somewhat ostentatiously by Tom Simson from his pack.
10135{_}2283{_}Notwithstanding some difficulties attending the manipulation of this instrument, Piney Woods managed to pluck several reluctant melodies from its keys, to an accompaniment by the Innocent on a pair of bone castanets.
10135{_}2284{_}But the crowning festivity of the evening was reached in a rude camp-meeting hymn, which the lovers, joining hands, sang with great earnestness and vociferation.
10135{_}2285{_}I fear that a certain defiant tone and Covenanter's swing to its chorus, rather than any devotional quality, caused it speedily to infect the others, who at last joined in the refrain:    "I'm proud to live in the service of the Lord,   And I'm bound to die in His army."
10135{_}2286{_}The pines rocked, the storm eddied and whirled above the miserable group, and the flames of their altar leaped heavenward, as if in token of the vow.
10135{_}2287{_}At midnight the storm abated, the rolling clouds parted, and the stars glittered keenly above the sleeping camp.
10135{_}2288{_}Mr. Oakhurst, whose professional habits had enabled him to live on the smallest possible amount of sleep, in dividing the watch with Tom Simson, somehow managed to take upon himself the greater part of that duty.
10135{_}2289{_}He excused himself to the Innocent by saying that he had "often been a week without sleep."
10135{_}2290{_}"Doing what?"
10135{_}2291{_}asked Tom.
10135{_}2292{_}"Poker!"
10135{_}2293{_}replied Oakhurst, sententiously; "when a man gets a streak of luck--nigger-luck--he don't get tired.
10135{_}2294{_}The luck gives in first.
10135{_}2295{_}Luck," continued the gambler, reflectively, "is a mighty queer thing.
10135{_}2296{_}All you know about it for certain is that it's bound to change.
10135{_}2297{_}And it's finding out when it's going to change that makes you.
10135{_}2298{_}We've had a streak of bad luck since we left Poker Flat--you come along, and slap you get into it, too.
10135{_}2299{_}If you can hold your cards right along, you're all right.
10135{_}2300{_}For," added the gambler, with cheerful irrelevance--    "'I'm proud to live in the service of the Lord,   And I'm bound to die in His army,'"  The third day came, and the sun, looking through the white-curtained valley, saw the outcasts divide their slowly decreasing store of provisions for the morning meal.
10135{_}2301{_}It was one of the peculiarities of that mountain climate that its rays diffused a kindly warmth over the wintry landscape, as if in regretful commiseration of the past.
10135{_}2302{_}But it revealed drift on drift of snow piled high around the hut--a hopeless, uncharted, trackless sea of white lying below the rocky shores to which the castaways still clung.
10135{_}2303{_}Through the marvellously clear air the smoke of the pastoral village of Poker Flat rose miles away.
10135{_}2304{_}Mother Shipton saw it, and from a remote pinnacle of her rocky fastness hurled in that direction a final malediction.
10135{_}2305{_}It was her last vituperative attempt, and perhaps for that reason was invested with a certain degree of sublimity.
10135{_}2306{_}It did her good, she privately informed the Duchess.
10135{_}2307{_}"Just you go out there and cuss, and see."
10135{_}2308{_}She then set herself to the task of amusing "the child," as she and the Duchess were pleased to call Piney.
10135{_}2309{_}Piney was no chicken, but it was a soothing and original theory of the pair thus to account for the fact that she didn't swear and wasn't improper.
10135{_}2310{_}When night crept up again through the gorges, the reedy notes of the accordion rose and fell in fitful spasms and long-drawn gasps by the flickering camp-fire.
10135{_}2311{_}But music failed to fill entirely the aching void left by insufficient food, and a new diversion was proposed by Piney--story-telling.
10135{_}2312{_}Neither Mr., Oakhurst nor his female companions caring to relate their personal experiences, this plan would have failed, too, but for the Innocent.
10135{_}2313{_}Some months before he had chanced upon a stray copy of Mr. Pope's ingenious translation of the _Iliad_.
10135{_}2314{_}He now proposed to narrate the principal incidents of that poem--having thoroughly mastered the argument and fairly forgotten the words--in the current vernacular of Sandy Bar.
10135{_}2315{_}And so for the rest of that night the Homeric demigods again walked the earth.
10135{_}2316{_}Trojan bully and wily Greek wrestled in the winds, and the great pines in the canon seemed to bow to the wrath of the son of Peleus.
10135{_}2317{_}Mr. Oakhurst listened with quiet satisfaction.
10135{_}2318{_}Most especially was he interested in the fate of "Ash-heels," as the Innocent persisted in denominating the "swift-footed Achilles."
10135{_}2319{_}So with small food and much of Homer and the accordion, a week passed over the heads of the outcasts.
10135{_}2320{_}The sun again forsook them, and again from leaden skies the snowflakes were sifted over the land.
10135{_}2321{_}Day by day closer around them drew the snowy circle, until at last they looked from their prison over drifted walls of dazzling white, that towered twenty feet above their heads.
10135{_}2322{_}It became more and more difficult to replenish their fires, even from the fallen trees beside them, now half hidden in the drifts.
10135{_}2323{_}And yet no one complained.
10135{_}2324{_}The lovers turned from the dreary prospect and looked into each other's eyes, and were happy.
10135{_}2325{_}Mr. Oakhurst settled himself coolly to the losing game before him.
10135{_}2326{_}The Duchess, more cheerful than she had been, assumed the care of Piney.
10135{_}2327{_}Only Mother Shipton--once the strongest of the party--seemed to sicken and fade.
10135{_}2328{_}At midnight on the tenth day she called Oakhurst to her side.
10135{_}2329{_}"I'm going," she said, in a voice of querulous weakness, "but don't say anything about it.
10135{_}2330{_}Don't waken the kids.
10135{_}2331{_}Take the bundle from under my head and open it."
10135{_}2332{_}Mr. Oakhurst did so.
10135{_}2333{_}It contained Mother Shipton's rations for the last week, untouched.
10135{_}2334{_}"Give 'em to the child," she said, pointing to the sleeping Piney.
10135{_}2335{_}"You've starved yourself," said the gambler.
10135{_}2336{_}"That's what they call it," said the woman, querulously, as she lay down again, and, turning her face to the wall, passed quietly away.
10135{_}2337{_}The accordion and the bones were put aside that day, and Homer was forgotten.
10135{_}2338{_}When the body of Mother Shipton had been committed to the snow, Mr. Oakhurst took the Innocent aside and showed him a pair of snow-shoes, which he had fashioned from the old pack-saddle.
10135{_}2339{_}"There's one chance in a hundred to save her yet," he said, pointing to Piney; "but it's there," he added, pointing toward Poker Flat.
10135{_}2340{_}"If you can reach there in two days she's safe."
10135{_}2341{_}"And you?"
10135{_}2342{_}asked Tom Simson.
10135{_}2343{_}"I'll stay here," was the curt reply.
10135{_}2344{_}The lovers parted with a long embrace.
10135{_}2345{_}"You are not going, too?"
10135{_}2346{_}said the Duchess, as she saw Mr. Oakhurst apparently waiting to accompany him.
10135{_}2347{_}"As far as the canon," he replied.
10135{_}2348{_}He turned suddenly and kissed the Duchess, leaving her pallid face aflame and her trembling limbs rigid with amazement.
10135{_}2349{_}Night came, but not Mr. Oakhurst.
10135{_}2350{_}It brought the storm again and the whirling snow.
10135{_}2351{_}Then the Duchess, feeding the fire, found that some one had quietly piled beside the hut enough fuel to last a few days longer.
10135{_}2352{_}The tears rose to her eyes, but she hid them from Piney.
10135{_}2353{_}The women slept but little.
10135{_}2354{_}In the morning, looking into each other's faces, they read their fate.
10135{_}2355{_}Neither spoke; but Piney, accepting the position of the stronger, drew near and placed her arm around the Duchess's waist.
10135{_}2356{_}They kept this attitude for the rest of the day.
10135{_}2357{_}That night the storm reached its greatest fury, and, rending asunder the protecting pines, invaded the very hut.
10135{_}2358{_}Toward morning they found themselves unable to feed the fire, which gradually died away.
10135{_}2359{_}As the embers slowly blackened, the Duchess crept closer to Piney, and broke the silence of many hours: "Piney, can you pray?"
10135{_}2360{_}"No, dear," said Piney, simply.
10135{_}2361{_}The Duchess, without knowing exactly why, felt relieved, and, putting her head upon Piney's shoulder, spoke no more.
10135{_}2362{_}And so reclining, the younger and purer pillowing the head of her soiled sister upon her virgin breast, they fell asleep.
10135{_}2363{_}The wind lulled as if it feared to waken them.
10135{_}2364{_}Feathery drifts of snow, shaken from the long pine-boughs, flew like white-winged birds, and settled about them as they slept.
10135{_}2365{_}The moon through the rifted clouds looked down upon what had been the camp.
10135{_}2366{_}But all human stain, all trace of earthly travail, was hidden beneath the spotless mantle mercifully flung from above.
10135{_}2367{_}They slept all that day and the next, nor did they waken when voices and footsteps broke the silence of the camp.
10135{_}2368{_}And when pitying fingers brushed the snow from their wan faces, you could scarcely have told, from the equal peace that dwelt upon them, which was she that had sinned.
10135{_}2369{_}Even the law of Poker Flat recognized this, and turned away, leaving them still locked in each other's arms.
10135{_}2370{_}But at the head of the gulch, on one of the largest pine-trees, they found the deuce of clubs pinned to the bark with a bowie-knife.
10135{_}2371{_}It bore the following, written in pencil, in a firm hand:              +           BENEATH THIS TREE            LIES THE BODY                 OF           JOHN OAKHURST,    WHO STRUCK A STREAK OF BAD LUCK     ON THE 23D OF NOVEMBER, 1850,                AND        HANDED IN HIS CHECKS      ON THE 7TH DECEMBER, 1850.
10135{_}2372{_}+  And pulseless and cold, with a derringer by his side and a bullet in his heart, though still calm as in life, beneath the snow lay he who was at once the strongest and yet the weakest of the outcasts of Poker Flat.
10135{_}2373{_}THE THREE STRANGERS[1]  [Footnote 1: From _Wessex Tales_.]
10135{_}2374{_}_Thomas Hardy_ (1840)   Among the few features of agricultural England which retain an appearance but little modified by the lapse of centuries, may be reckoned the high, grassy and furzy downs, coombs, or ewe-leases, as they are indifferently called, that fill a large area of certain counties in the south and southwest.
10135{_}2375{_}If any mark of human occupation is met with hereon, it usually takes the form of the solitary cottage of some shepherd.
10135{_}2376{_}Fifty years ago such a lonely cottage stood on such a down, and may possibly be standing there now.
10135{_}2377{_}In spite of its loneliness, however, the spot, by actual measurement, was not more than five miles from a county-town.
10135{_}2378{_}Yet that affected it little.
10135{_}2379{_}Five miles of irregular upland, during the long inimical seasons, with their sleets, snows, rains, and mists, afford withdrawing space enough to isolate a Timon or a Nebuchadnezzar; much less, in fair weather, to please that less repellent tribe, the poets, philosophers, artists, and others who "conceive and meditate of pleasant things."
10135{_}2380{_}Some old earthen camp or barrow, some clump of trees, at least some starved fragment of ancient hedge is usually taken advantage of in the erection of these forlorn dwellings.
10135{_}2381{_}But, in the present case, such a kind of shelter had been disregarded.
10135{_}2382{_}Higher Crowstairs, as the house was called, stood quite detached and undefended.
10135{_}2383{_}The only reason for its precise situation seemed to be the crossing of two footpaths at right angles hard by, which may have crossed there and thus for a good five hundred years.
10135{_}2384{_}Hence the house was exposed to the elements on all sides.
10135{_}2385{_}But, though the wind up here blew unmistakably when it did blow, and the rain hit hard whenever it fell, the various weathers of the winter season were not quite so formidable on the coomb as they were imagined to be by dwellers on low ground.
10135{_}2386{_}The raw rimes were not so pernicious as in the hollows, and the frosts were scarcely so severe.
10135{_}2387{_}When the shepherd and his family who tenanted the house were pitied for their sufferings from the exposure, they said that upon the whole they were less inconvenienced by "wuzzes and flames" (hoarses and phlegms) than when they had lived by the stream of a snug neighboring valley.
10135{_}2388{_}The night of March 28, 182-, was precisely one of the nights that were wont to call forth these expressions of commiseration.
10135{_}2389{_}The level rainstorm smote walls, slopes, and hedges like the clothyard shafts of Senlac and Crecy.
10135{_}2390{_}Such sheep and outdoor animals as had no shelter stood with their buttocks to the winds; while the tails of little birds trying to roost on some scraggy thorn were blown inside-out like umbrellas.
10135{_}2391{_}The gable-end of the cottage was stained with wet, and the eavesdroppings flapped against the wall.
10135{_}2392{_}Yet never was commiseration for the shepherd more misplaced.
10135{_}2393{_}For that cheerful rustic was entertaining a large party in glorification of the christening of his second girl.
10135{_}2394{_}The guests had arrived before the rain began to fall, and they were all now assembled in the chief or living room of the dwelling.
10135{_}2395{_}A glance into the apartment at eight o'clock on this eventful evening would have resulted in the opinion that it was as cosy and comfortable a nook as could be wished for in boisterous weather.
10135{_}2396{_}The calling of its inhabitant was proclaimed by a number of highly-polished sheep crooks without stems that were hung ornamentally over the fireplace, the curl of each shining crook varying from the antiquated type engraved in the patriarchal pictures of old family Bibles to the most approved fashion of the last local sheep-fair.
10135{_}2397{_}The room was lighted by half-a-dozen candles, having wicks only a trifle smaller than the grease which enveloped them, in candlesticks that were never used but at high-days, holy-days, and family feasts.
10135{_}2398{_}The lights were scattered about the room, two of them standing on the chimney-piece.
10135{_}2399{_}This position of candles was in itself significant.
10135{_}2400{_}Candles on the chimney-piece always meant a party.
10135{_}2401{_}On the hearth, in front of a back-brand to give substance, blazed a fire of thorns, that crackled "like the laughter of the fool."
10135{_}2402{_}Nineteen persons were gathered here.
10135{_}2403{_}Of these, five women, wearing gowns of various bright hues, sat in chairs along the wall; girls shy and not shy filled the window-bench; four men, including Charley Jake the hedge-carpenter, Elijah New the parish-clerk, and John Pitcher, a neighboring dairyman, the shepherd's father-in-law, lolled in the settle; a young man and maid, who were blushing over tentative _pourparlers_ on a life-companionship, sat beneath the corner-cupboard; and an elderly engaged man of fifty or upward moved restlessly about from spots where his betrothed was not to the spot where she was.
10135{_}2404{_}Enjoyment was pretty general, and so much the more prevailed in being unhampered by conventional restrictions.
10135{_}2405{_}Absolute confidence in each other's good opinion begat perfect ease, while the finishing stroke of manner, amounting to a truly princely serenity, was lent to the majority by the absence of any expression or trait denoting that they wished to get on in the world, enlarge their minds, or do any eclipsing thing whatever--which nowadays so generally nips the bloom and _bonhomie_ of all except the two extremes of the social scale.
10135{_}2406{_}Shepherd Fennel had married well, his wife being a dairyman's daughter from a vale at a distance, who brought fifty guineas in her pocket--and kept them there, till they should be required for ministering to the needs of a coming family.
10135{_}2407{_}This frugal woman had been somewhat exercised as to the character that should be given to the gathering.
10135{_}2408{_}A sit-still party had its advantages; but an undisturbed position of ease in chairs and settles was apt to lead on the men to such an unconscionable deal of toping that they would sometimes fairly drink the house dry.
10135{_}2409{_}A dancing-party was the alternative; but this, while avoiding the foregoing objection on the score of good drink, had a counterbalancing disadvantage in the matter of good victuals, the ravenous appetites engendered by the exercise causing immense havoc in the buttery.
10135{_}2410{_}Shepherdess Fennel fell back upon the intermediate plan of mingling short dances with short periods of talk and singing, so as to hinder any ungovernable rage in either.
10135{_}2411{_}But this scheme was entirely confined to her own gentle mind: the shepherd himself was in the mood to exhibit the most reckless phases of hospitality.
10135{_}2412{_}The fiddler was a boy of those parts, about twelve years of age, who had a wonderful dexterity in jigs and reels, though his fingers were so small and short as to necessitate a constant shifting for the high notes, from which he scrambled back to the first position with sounds not of unmixed purity of tone.
10135{_}2413{_}At seven the shrill tweedle-dee of this youngster had begun, accompanied by a booming ground-bass from Elijah New, the parish-clerk, who had thoughtfully brought with him his favorite musical instrument, the serpent.
10135{_}2414{_}Dancing was instantaneous, Mrs. Fennel privately enjoining the players on no account to let the dance exceed the length of a quarter of an hour.
10135{_}2415{_}But Elijah and the boy, in the excitement of their position, quite forgot the injunction.
10135{_}2416{_}Moreover, Oliver Giles, a man of seventeen, one of the dancers, who was enamoured of his partner, a fair girl of thirty-three rolling years, had recklessly handed a new crown-piece to the musicians, as a bribe to keep going as long as they had muscle and wind.
10135{_}2417{_}Mrs. Fennel, seeing the steam begin to generate on the countenances of her guests, crossed over and touched the fiddler's elbow and put her hand on the serpent's mouth.
10135{_}2418{_}But they took no notice, and fearing she might lose her character of genial hostess if she were to interfere too markedly, she retired and sat down helpless.
10135{_}2419{_}And so the dance whizzed on with cumulative fury, the performers moving in their planet-like courses, direct and retrograde, from apogee to perigee, till the hand of the well-kicked clock at the bottom of the room had travelled over the circumference of an hour.
10135{_}2420{_}While these cheerful events were in course of enactment within Fennel's pastoral dwelling, an incident having considerable bearing on the party had occurred in the gloomy night without.
10135{_}2421{_}Mrs. Fennel's concern about the growing fierceness of the dance corresponded in point of time with the ascent of a human figure to the solitary hill of Higher Crowstairs from the direction of the distant town.
10135{_}2422{_}This personage strode on through the rain without a pause, following the little-worn path which, further on in its course, skirted the shepherd's cottage.
10135{_}2423{_}It was nearly the time of full moon, and on this account, though the sky was lined with a uniform sheet of dripping cloud, ordinary objects out of doors were readily visible.
10135{_}2424{_}The sad wan light revealed the lonely pedestrian to be a man of supple frame; his gait suggested that he had somewhat passed the period of perfect and instinctive agility, though not so far as to be otherwise than rapid of motion when occasion required.
10135{_}2425{_}At a rough guess, he might have been about forty years of age.
10135{_}2426{_}He appeared tall, but a recruiting sergeant, or other person accustomed to the judging of men's heights by the eye, would have discerned that this was chiefly owing to his gauntness, and that he was not more than five-feet-eight or nine.
10135{_}2427{_}Notwithstanding the regularity of his tread, there was caution in it, as in that of one who mentally feels his way; and despite the fact that it was not a black coat nor a dark garment of any sort that he wore, there was something about him which suggested that he naturally belonged to the black-coated tribes of men.
10135{_}2428{_}His clothes were of fustian, and his boots hobnailed, yet in his progress he showed not the mud-accustomed bearing of hobnailed and fustianed peasantry.
10135{_}2429{_}By the time that he had arrived abreast of the shepherd's premises the rain came down, or rather came along, with yet more determined violence.
10135{_}2430{_}The outskirts of the little settlement partially broke the force of wind and rain, and this induced him to stand still.
10135{_}2431{_}The most salient of the shepherd's domestic erections was an empty sty at the forward corner of his hedgeless garden, for in these latitudes the principle of masking the homelier features of your establishment by a conventional frontage was unknown.
10135{_}2432{_}The traveller's eye was attracted to this small building by the pallid shine of the wet slates that covered it.
10135{_}2433{_}He turned aside, and, finding it empty, stood under the pent-roof for shelter.
10135{_}2434{_}While he stood, the boom of the serpent within the adjacent house, and the lesser strains of the fiddler, reached the spot as an accompaniment to the surging hiss of the flying rain on the sod, its louder beating on the cabbage-leaves of the garden, on the eight or ten beehives just discernible by the path, and its dripping from the eaves into a row of buckets and pans that had been placed under the walls of the cottage.
10135{_}2435{_}For at Higher Crowstairs, as at all such elevated domiciles, the grand difficulty of housekeeping was an insufficiency of water; and a casual rainfall was utilized by turning out, as catchers, every utensil that the house contained.
10135{_}2436{_}Some queer stories might be told of the contrivances for economy in suds and dishwaters that are absolutely necessitated in upland habitations during the droughts of summer.
10135{_}2437{_}But at this season there were no such exigencies; a mere acceptance of what the skies bestowed was sufficient for an abundant store.
10135{_}2438{_}At last the notes of the serpent ceased and the house was silent.
10135{_}2439{_}This cessation of activity aroused the solitary pedestrian from the reverie into which he had elapsed, and, emerging from the shed, with an apparently new intention, he walked up the path to the house-door.
10135{_}2440{_}Arrived here, his first act was to kneel down on a large stone beside the row of vessels, and to drink a copious draught from one of them.
10135{_}2441{_}Having quenched his thirst, he rose and lifted his hand to knock, but paused with his eye upon the panel.
10135{_}2442{_}Since the dark surface of the wood revealed absolutely nothing, it was evident that he must be mentally looking through the door, as if he wished to measure thereby all the possibilities that a house of this sort might include, and how they might bear upon the question of his entry.
10135{_}2443{_}In his indecision he turned and surveyed the scene around.
10135{_}2444{_}Not a soul was anywhere visible.
10135{_}2445{_}The garden-path stretched downward from his feet, gleaming like the track of a snail; the roof of the little well (mostly dry), the well-cover, the top rail of the garden-gate, were varnished with the same dull liquid glaze; while, far away in the vale, a faint whiteness of more than usual extent showed that the rivers were high in the meads.
10135{_}2446{_}Beyond all this winked a few bleared lamplights through the beating drops--lights that denoted the situation of the county-town from which he had appeared to come.
10135{_}2447{_}The absence of all notes of life in that direction seemed to clinch his intentions, and he knocked at the door.
10135{_}2448{_}Within, a desultory chat had taken the place of movement and musical sound.
10135{_}2449{_}The hedge-carpenter was suggesting a song to the company, which nobody just then was inclined to undertake, so that the knock afforded a not unwelcome diversion.
10135{_}2450{_}"Walk in!"
10135{_}2451{_}said the shepherd, promptly.
10135{_}2452{_}The latch clicked upward, and out of the night our pedestrian appeared upon the door-mat.
10135{_}2453{_}The shepherd arose, snuffed two of the nearest candles, and turned to look at him.
10135{_}2454{_}Their light disclosed that the stranger was dark in complexion and not unprepossessing as to feature.
10135{_}2455{_}His hat, which for a moment he did not remove, hung low over his eyes, without concealing that they were large, open, and determined, moving with a flash rather than a glance round the room.
10135{_}2456{_}He seemed pleased with his survey, and, baring his shaggy head, said, in a rich, deep voice: "The rain is so heavy, friends, that I ask leave to come in and rest awhile."
10135{_}2457{_}"To be sure, stranger," said the shepherd.
10135{_}2458{_}"And faith, you've been lucky in choosing your time, for we are having a bit of a fling for a glad cause--though, to be sure, a man could hardly wish that glad cause to happen more than once a year."
10135{_}2459{_}"Nor less," spoke up a woman.
10135{_}2460{_}"For 'tis best to get your family over and done with, as soon as you can, so as to be all the earlier out of the fag o't."
10135{_}2461{_}"And what may be this glad cause?"
10135{_}2462{_}asked the stranger.
10135{_}2463{_}"A birth and christening," said the shepherd.
10135{_}2464{_}The stranger hoped his host might not be made unhappy either by too many or two few of such episodes, and being invited by a gesture to a pull at the mug, he readily acquiesced.
10135{_}2465{_}His manner, which, before entering, had been so dubious, was now altogether that of a careless and candid man.
10135{_}2466{_}"Late to be traipsing athwart this coomb--hey?"
10135{_}2467{_}said the engaged man of fifty.
10135{_}2468{_}"Late it is, master, as you say.--I'll take a seat in the chimney-corner, if you have nothing to urge against it, ma'am; for I am a little moist on the side that was next the rain."
10135{_}2469{_}Mrs. Shepherd Fennel assented, and made room for the self-invited comer, who, having got completely inside the chimney-corner, stretched out his legs and arms with the expansiveness of a person quite at home.
10135{_}2470{_}"Yes, I am rather cracked in the vamp," he said freely, seeing that the eyes of the shepherd's wife fell upon his boots, "and I am not well fitted either.
10135{_}2471{_}I have had some rough times lately, and have been forced to pick up what I can get in the way of wearing, but I must find a suit better fit for working-days when I reach home."
10135{_}2472{_}"One of hereabouts?"
10135{_}2473{_}she inquired.
10135{_}2474{_}"Not quite that--further up the country."
10135{_}2475{_}"I thought so.
10135{_}2476{_}And so be I; and by your tongue you come from my neighborhood."
10135{_}2477{_}"But you would hardly have heard of me," he said quickly.
10135{_}2478{_}"My time would be long before yours, ma'am, you see."
10135{_}2479{_}This testimony to the youthfulness of his hostess had the effect of stopping her cross-examination.
10135{_}2480{_}"There is only one thing more wanted to make me happy," continued the new-comer, "and that is a little baccy, which I am sorry to say I am out of."
10135{_}2481{_}"I'll fill your pipe," said the shepherd.
10135{_}2482{_}"I must ask you to lend me a pipe likewise."
10135{_}2483{_}"A smoker, and no pipe about 'ee?"
10135{_}2484{_}"I have dropped it somewhere on the road."
10135{_}2485{_}The shepherd filled and handed him a new clay pipe, saying, as he did so, "Hand me your baccy-box--I'll fill that too, now I am about it."
10135{_}2486{_}The man went through the movement of searching his pockets.
10135{_}2487{_}"Lost that too?"
10135{_}2488{_}said his entertainer, with some surprise.
10135{_}2489{_}"I am afraid so," said the man with some confusion.
10135{_}2490{_}"Give it to me in a screw of paper."
10135{_}2491{_}Lighting his pipe at the candle with a suction that drew the whole flame into the bowl, he resettled himself in the corner and bent his looks upon the faint steam from his damp legs, as if he wished to say no more.
10135{_}2492{_}Meanwhile the general body of guests had been taking little notice of this visitor by reason of an absorbing discussion in which they were engaged with the band about a tune for the next dance.
10135{_}2493{_}The matter being settled, they were about to stand up when an interruption came in the shape of another knock at the door.
10135{_}2494{_}At sound of the same the man in the chimney-corner took up the poker and began stirring the brands as if doing it thoroughly were the one aim of his existence; and a second time the shepherd said, "Walk in!"
10135{_}2495{_}In a moment another man stood upon the straw-woven door-mat.
10135{_}2496{_}He too was a stranger.
10135{_}2497{_}This individual was one of a type radically different from the first.
10135{_}2498{_}There was more of the commonplace in his manner, and a certain jovial cosmopolitanism sat upon his features.
10135{_}2499{_}He was several years older than the first arrival, his hair being slightly frosted, his eyebrows bristly, and his whiskers cut back from his cheeks.
10135{_}2500{_}His face was rather full and flabby, and yet it was not altogether a face without power.
10135{_}2501{_}A few grog-blossoms marked the neighborhood of his nose.
10135{_}2502{_}He flung back his long drab greatcoat, revealing that beneath it he wore a suit of cinder-gray shade throughout, large heavy seals, of some metal or other that would take a polish, dangling from his fob as his only personal ornament.
10135{_}2503{_}Shaking the water-drops from his low-crowned glazed hat, he said, "I must ask for a few minutes' shelter, comrades, or I shall be wetted to my skin before I get to Casterbridge."
10135{_}2504{_}"Make yourself at home, master," said the shepherd, perhaps a trifle less heartily than on the first occasion.
10135{_}2505{_}Not that Fennel had the least tinge of niggardliness in his composition; but the room was far from large, spare chairs were not numerous, and damp companions were not altogether desirable at close quarters for the women and girls in their bright-colored gowns.
10135{_}2506{_}However, the second comer, after taking off his greatcoat, and hanging his hat on a nail in one of the ceiling-beams as if he had been specially invited to put it there, advanced and sat down at the table.
10135{_}2507{_}This had been pushed so closely into the chimney-corner, to give all available room to the dancers, that its inner edge grazed the elbow of the man who had ensconced himself by the fire; and thus the two strangers were brought into close companionship.
10135{_}2508{_}They nodded to each other by way of breaking the ice of unacquaintance, and the first stranger handed his neighbor the family mug--a huge vessel of brown ware, having its upper edge worn away like a threshold by the rub of whole generations of thirsty lips that had gone the way of all flesh, and bearing the following inscription burnt upon its rotund side in yellow letters:    THERE IS NO FUN   UNTILL I CUM.
10135{_}2509{_}The other man, nothing loth, raised the mug to his lips, and drank on, and on, and on--till a curious blueness overspread the countenance of the shepherd's wife, who had regarded with no little surprise the first stranger's free offer to the second of what did not belong to him to dispense.
10135{_}2510{_}"I knew it!"
10135{_}2511{_}said the toper to the shepherd with much satisfaction.
10135{_}2512{_}"When I walked up your garden before coming in, and saw the hives all of a row, I said to myself, 'Where there's bees there's honey, and where there's honey there's mead,' But mead of such a truly comfortable sort as this I really didn't expect to meet in my older days."
10135{_}2513{_}He took yet another pull at the mug, till it assumed an ominous elevation.
10135{_}2514{_}"Glad you enjoy it!"
10135{_}2515{_}said the shepherd warmly.
10135{_}2516{_}"It is goodish mead," assented Mrs. Fennel, with an absence of enthusiasm which seemed to say that it was possible to buy praise for one's cellar at too heavy a price.
10135{_}2517{_}"It is trouble enough to make--and really I hardly think we shall make any more.
10135{_}2518{_}For honey sells well, and we ourselves can make shift with a drop o' small mead and metheglin for common use from the comb-washings."
10135{_}2519{_}"O, but you'll never have the heart!"
10135{_}2520{_}reproachfully cried the stranger in cinder-gray, after taking up the mug a third time and setting it down empty.
10135{_}2521{_}"I love mead, when 'tis old like this, as I love to go to church o' Sundays, or to relieve the needy any day of the week."
10135{_}2522{_}"Ha, ha, ha!"
10135{_}2523{_}said the man in the chimney-corner, who, in spite of the taciturnity induced by the pipe of tobacco, could not or would not refrain from this slight testimony to his comrade's humor.
10135{_}2524{_}Now the old mead of those days, brewed of the purest first-year or maiden honey, four pounds to the gallon--with its due complement of white of eggs, cinnamon, ginger, cloves, mace, rosemary, yeast, and processes of working, bottling, and cellaring--tasted remarkably strong; but it did not taste so strong as it actually was.
10135{_}2525{_}Hence, presently, the stranger in cinder-gray at the table, moved by its creeping influence, unbuttoned his waistcoat, threw himself back in his chair, spread his legs, and made his presence felt in various ways.
10135{_}2526{_}"Well, well, as I say," he resumed, "I am going to Casterbridge, and to Casterbridge I must go.
10135{_}2527{_}I should have been almost there by this time; but the rain drove me into your dwelling, and I'm not sorry for it."
10135{_}2528{_}"You don't live in Casterbridge?"
10135{_}2529{_}said the shepherd.
10135{_}2530{_}"Not as yet; though I shortly mean to move there."
10135{_}2531{_}"Going to set up in trade, perhaps?"
10135{_}2532{_}"No, no," said the shepherd's wife.
10135{_}2533{_}"It is easy to see that the gentleman is rich, and don't want to work at anything."
10135{_}2534{_}The cinder-gray stranger paused, as if to consider whether he would accept that definition of himself.
10135{_}2535{_}He presently rejected it by answering, "Rich is not quite the word for me, dame.
10135{_}2536{_}I do work, and I must work.
10135{_}2537{_}And even if I only get to Casterbridge by midnight I must begin work there at eight to-morrow morning.
10135{_}2538{_}Yes, het or wet, blow or snow, famine or sword, my day's work to-morrow must be done."
10135{_}2539{_}"Poor man!
10135{_}2540{_}Then, in spite o' seeming, you be off than we."
10135{_}2541{_}replied the shepherd's wife.
10135{_}2542{_}"'Tis the nature of my trade, men and maidens.
10135{_}2543{_}Tis the nature of my trade more than my poverty....
10135{_}2544{_}But really and truly I must up and off, or I shan't get a lodging in the town."
10135{_}2545{_}However, the speaker did not move, and directly added, "There's time for one more draught of friendship before I go; and I'd perform it at once if the mug were not dry."
10135{_}2546{_}"Here's a mug o' small," said Mrs. Fennel.
10135{_}2547{_}"Small, we call it, though to be sure 'tis only the first wash o' the combs."
10135{_}2548{_}"No," said the stranger, disdainfully.
10135{_}2549{_}"I won't spoil your first kindness by partaking o' your second."
10135{_}2550{_}"Certainly not," broke in Fennel.
10135{_}2551{_}"We don't increase and multiply every day, and I'll fill the mug again."
10135{_}2552{_}He went away to the dark place under the stairs where the barrel stood.
10135{_}2553{_}The shepherdess followed him.
10135{_}2554{_}"Why should you do this?"
10135{_}2555{_}she said, reproachfully, as soon as they were alone.
10135{_}2556{_}"He's emptied it once, though it held enough for ten people; and now he's not contented wi' the small, but must needs call for more o' the strong!
10135{_}2557{_}And a stranger unbeknown to any of us.
10135{_}2558{_}For my part, I don't like the look o' the man at all."
10135{_}2559{_}"But he's in the house, my honey; and 'tis a wet night, and a christening.
10135{_}2560{_}Daze it, what's a cup of mead more or less?
10135{_}2561{_}There'll be plenty more next bee-burning."
10135{_}2562{_}"Very well--this time, then," she answered, looking wistfully at the barrel.
10135{_}2563{_}"But what is the man's calling, and where is he one of, that he should come in and join us like this?"
10135{_}2564{_}"I don't know.
10135{_}2565{_}I'll ask him again."
10135{_}2566{_}The catastrophe of having the mug drained dry at one pull by the stranger in cinder-gray was effectually guarded against this time by Mrs. Fennel.
10135{_}2567{_}She poured out his allowance in a small cup, keeping the large one at a discreet distance from him.
10135{_}2568{_}When he had tossed off his portion the shepherd renewed his inquiry about the stranger's occupation.
10135{_}2569{_}The latter did not immediately reply, and the man in the chimney-corner, with sudden demonstrativeness, said, "Anybody may know my trade--I'm a wheelwright."
10135{_}2570{_}"A very good trade for these parts," said the shepherd.
10135{_}2571{_}"And anybody may know mine--if they've the sense to find it out," said the stranger in cinder-gray.
10135{_}2572{_}"You may generally tell what a man is by his claws," observed the hedge-carpenter, looking at his own hands.
10135{_}2573{_}"My fingers be as full of thorns as an old pin-cushion is of pins."
10135{_}2574{_}The hands of the man in the chimney-corner instinctively sought the shade, and he gazed into the fire as he resumed his pipe.
10135{_}2575{_}The man at the table took up the hedge-carpenter's remark, and added smartly, "True; but the oddity of my trade is that, instead of setting a mark upon me, it sets a mark upon my customers."
10135{_}2576{_}No observation being offered by anybody in elucidation of this enigma, the shepherd's wife once more called for a song.
10135{_}2577{_}The same obstacles presented themselves as at the former time--one had no voice, another had forgotten the first verse.
10135{_}2578{_}The stranger at the table, whose soul had now risen to a good working temperature, relieved the difficulty by exclaiming that, to start the company, he would sing himself.
10135{_}2579{_}Thrusting one thumb into the arm-hole of his waistcoat, he waved the other hand in the air, and, with an extemporizing gaze at the shining sheep-crooks above the mantelpiece, began:    "O my trade it is the rarest one,     Simple shepherds all--     My trade is a sight to see;   For my customers I tie, and take them up on high,     And waft 'em to a far countree!"
10135{_}2580{_}The room was silent when he had finished the verse--with one exception, that of the man in the chimney-corner, who, at the singer's word, "Chorus!"
10135{_}2581{_}joined him in a deep bass voice of musical relish:    "And waft 'em to a far countree!"
10135{_}2582{_}Oliver Giles, John Pitcher the dairyman, the parish-clerk, the engaged man of fifty, the row of young women against the wall, seemed lost in thought not of the gayest kind.
10135{_}2583{_}The shepherd looked meditatively on the ground, the shepherdess gazed keenly at the singer, and with some suspicion; she was doubting whether this stranger were merely singing an old song from recollection, or was composing one there and then for the occasion.
10135{_}2584{_}All were as perplexed at the obscure revelation as the guests at Belshazzar's Feast, except the man in the chimney-corner, who quietly said, "Second verse, stranger," and smoked on.
10135{_}2585{_}The singer thoroughly moistened himself from his lips inward, and went on with the next stanza as requested:      "My tools are but common ones,          Simple shepherds all--     My tools are no sight to see:   A little hempen string, and a post whereon to swing,     Are implements enough for me!"
10135{_}2586{_}Shepherd Fennel glanced round.
10135{_}2587{_}There was no longer any doubt that the stranger was answering his question rhythmically.
10135{_}2588{_}The guests one and all started back with suppressed exclamations.
10135{_}2589{_}The young woman engaged to the man of fifty fainted half-way, and would have proceeded, but finding him wanting in alacrity for catching her she sat down trembling.
10135{_}2590{_}"O, he's the--!"
10135{_}2591{_}whispered the people in the background, mentioning the name of an ominous public officer.
10135{_}2592{_}"He's come to do it!
10135{_}2593{_}'Tis to be at Casterbridge jail to-morrow--the man for sheep-stealing--the poor clock-maker we heard of, who used to live away at Shottsford and had no work to do--Timothy Summers, whose family were a-starving, and so he went out of Shottsford by the high-road, and took a sheep in open daylight, defying the farmer and the farmer's wife and the farmer's lad, and every man jack among 'em.
10135{_}2594{_}He" (and they nodded toward the stranger of the deadly trade) "is come from up the country to do it because there's not enough to do in his own county-town, and he's got the place here now our own county man's dead; he's going to live in the same cottage under the prison wall."
10135{_}2595{_}The stranger in cinder-gray took no notice of this whispered string of observations, but again wetted his lips.
10135{_}2596{_}Seeing that his friend in the chimney-corner was the only one who reciprocated his joviality in any way, he held out his cup toward that appreciative comrade, who also held out his own.
10135{_}2597{_}They clinked together, the eyes of the rest of the room hanging upon the singer's actions.
10135{_}2598{_}He parted his lips for the third verse; but at that moment another knock was audible upon the door.
10135{_}2599{_}This time the knock was faint and hesitating.
10135{_}2600{_}The company seemed scared; the shepherd looked with consternation toward the entrance, and it was with some effort that he resisted his alarmed wife's deprecatory glance, and uttered for the third time the welcoming words, "Walk in!"
10135{_}2601{_}The door was gently opened, and another man stood upon the mat.
10135{_}2602{_}He, like those who had preceded him, was a stranger.
10135{_}2603{_}This time it was a short, small personage, of fair complexion, and dressed in a decent suit of dark clothes.
10135{_}2604{_}"Can you tell me the way to--?"
10135{_}2605{_}he began: when, gazing round the room to observe the nature of the company among whom he had fallen, his eyes lighted on the stranger in cinder-gray.
10135{_}2606{_}It was just at the instant when the latter, who had thrown his mind into his song with such a will that he scarcely heeded the interruption, silenced all whispers and inquiries by bursting into his third verse:    "To-morrow is my working day,   Simple shepherds all--   To-morrow is a working day for me:   For the farmer's sheep is slain, and the lad who did it ta'en,   And on his soul may God ha' merc-y!"
10135{_}2607{_}The stranger in the chimney-corner, waving cups with the singer so heartily that his mead splashed over on the hearth, repeated in his bass voice as before:    "And on his soul may God ha' merc-y!"
10135{_}2608{_}All this time the third stranger had been standing in the doorway.
10135{_}2609{_}Finding now that he did not come forward or go on speaking, the guests particularly regarded him.
10135{_}2610{_}They noticed to their surprise that he stood before them the picture of abject terror--his knees trembling, his hand shaking so violently that the door-latch by which he supported himself rattled audibly: his white lips were parted, and his eyes fixed on the merry officer of justice in the middle of the room.
10135{_}2611{_}A moment more and he had turned, closed the door, and fled.
10135{_}2612{_}"What a man can it be?"
10135{_}2613{_}said the shepherd.
10135{_}2614{_}The rest, between the awfulness of their late discovery and the odd conduct of this third visitor, looked as if they knew not what to think, and said nothing.
10135{_}2615{_}Instinctively they withdrew further and further from the grim gentleman in their midst, whom some of them seemed to take for the Prince of Darkness himself, till they formed a remote circle, an empty space of floor being left between them and him--  "... circulus, cujus centrum diabolus."
10135{_}2616{_}The room was so silent--though there were more than twenty people in it--that nothing could be heard but the patter of the rain against the window-shutters, accompanied by the occasional hiss of a stray drop that fell down the chimney into the fire, and the steady puffing of the man in the corner, who had now resumed his pipe of long clay.
10135{_}2617{_}The stillness was unexpectedly broken.
10135{_}2618{_}The distant sound of a gun reverberated through the air--apparently from the direction of the county-town.
10135{_}2619{_}"Be jiggered!"
10135{_}2620{_}cried the stranger who had sung the song, jumping up.
10135{_}2621{_}"What does that mean?"
10135{_}2622{_}asked several.
10135{_}2623{_}"A prisoner escaped from the jail--that's what it means."
10135{_}2624{_}All listened.
10135{_}2625{_}The sound was repeated, and none of them spoke but the man in the chimney-corner, who said quietly, "I've often been told that in this county they fire a gun at such times; but I never heard it till now."
10135{_}2626{_}"I wonder if it is _my_ man?"
10135{_}2627{_}murmured the personage in cinder-gray.
10135{_}2628{_}"Surely it is!"
10135{_}2629{_}said the shepherd involuntarily.
10135{_}2630{_}"And surely we've zeed him!
10135{_}2631{_}That little man who looked in at the door by now, and quivered like a leaf when he zeed ye and heard your song!"
10135{_}2632{_}"His teeth chattered, and the breath went out of his body," said the dairyman.
10135{_}2633{_}"And his heart seemed to sink within him like a stone," said Oliver Giles.
10135{_}2634{_}"And he bolted as if he'd been shot at," said the hedge-carpenter.
10135{_}2635{_}"True--his teeth chattered, and his heart seemed to sink; and he bolted as if he'd been shot at," slowly summed up the man in the chimney-corner.
10135{_}2636{_}"I didn't notice it," remarked the hangman.
10135{_}2637{_}"We were all a-wondering what made him run off in such a fright," faltered one of the women against the wall, "and now 'tis explained!"
10135{_}2638{_}The firing of the alarm-gun went on at intervals, low and sullenly, and their suspicions became a certainty.
10135{_}2639{_}The sinister gentleman in cinder-gray roused himself.
10135{_}2640{_}"Is there a constable here?"
10135{_}2641{_}he asked, in thick tones.
10135{_}2642{_}"If so, let him step forward."
10135{_}2643{_}The engaged man of fifty stepped quavering out from the wall, his betrothed beginning to sob on the back of the chair.
10135{_}2644{_}"You are a sworn constable?"
10135{_}2645{_}"I be, sir."
10135{_}2646{_}"Then pursue the criminal at once, with assistance, and bring him back here.
10135{_}2647{_}He can't have gone far."
10135{_}2648{_}"I will, sir, I will--when I've got my staff.
10135{_}2649{_}I'll go home and get it, and come sharp here, and start in a body."
10135{_}2650{_}"Staff!--never mind your staff; the man'll be gone!"
10135{_}2651{_}"But I can't do nothing without my staff--can I, William, and John, and Charles Jake?
10135{_}2652{_}No; for there's the king's royal crown a-painted on en in yaller and gold, and the lion and the unicorn, so as when I raise en up and hit my prisoner, 'tis made a lawful blow thereby.
10135{_}2653{_}I wouldn't 'tempt to take up a man without my staff--no, not I.
10135{_}2654{_}If I hadn't the law to gie me courage, why, instead o' my taking up him he might take up me!"
10135{_}2655{_}"Now, I'm a king's man myself, and can give you authority enough for this," said the formidable officer in gray.
10135{_}2656{_}"Now then, all of ye, be ready.
10135{_}2657{_}Have ye any lanterns?"
10135{_}2658{_}"Yes--have ye any lanterns?--I demand it!"
10135{_}2659{_}said the constable.
10135{_}2660{_}"And the rest of you able-bodied--"  "Able-bodied men--yes--the rest of ye!"
10135{_}2661{_}said the constable.
10135{_}2662{_}"Have you some good stout staves and pitchforks--"  "Staves and pitchforks--in the name o' the law!
10135{_}2663{_}And take 'em in yer hands and go in quest, and do as we in authority tell ye!"
10135{_}2664{_}Thus aroused, the men prepared to give chase.
10135{_}2665{_}The evidence was, indeed, though circumstantial, so convincing, that but little argument was needed to show the shepherd's guests that after what they had seen it would look very much like connivance if they did not instantly pursue the unhappy third stranger, who could not as yet have gone more than a few hundred yards over such uneven country.
10135{_}2666{_}A shepherd is always well provided with lanterns; and, lighting these hastily, and with hurdle-staves in their hands, they poured out of the door, taking a direction along the crest of the hill, away from the town, the rain having fortunately a little abated.
10135{_}2667{_}Disturbed by the noise, or possibly by unpleasant dreams of her baptism, the child who had been christened began to cry heart-brokenly in the room overhead.
10135{_}2668{_}These notes of grief came down through the chinks of the floor to the ears of the women below, who jumped up one by one, and seemed glad of the excuse to ascend and comfort the baby, for the incidents of the last half-hour greatly oppressed them.
10135{_}2669{_}Thus in the space of two or three minutes the room on the ground-floor was deserted quite.
10135{_}2670{_}But it was not for long.
10135{_}2671{_}Hardly had the sound of footsteps died away when a man returned round the corner of the house from the direction the pursuers had taken.
10135{_}2672{_}Peeping in at the door, and seeing nobody there, he entered leisurely.
10135{_}2673{_}It was the stranger of the chimney-corner, who had gone out with the rest.
10135{_}2674{_}The motive of his return was shown by his helping himself to a cut piece of skimmer-cake that lay on a ledge beside where he had sat, and which he had apparently forgotten to take with him.
10135{_}2675{_}He also poured out half a cup more mead from the quantity that remained, ravenously eating and drinking these as he stood.
10135{_}2676{_}He had not finished when another figure came in just as quietly--his friend in cinder-gray.
10135{_}2677{_}"O--you here?"
10135{_}2678{_}said the latter, smiling.
10135{_}2679{_}"I thought you had gone to help in the capture."
10135{_}2680{_}And this speaker also revealed the object of his return by looking solicitously round for the fascinating mug of old mead.
10135{_}2681{_}"And I thought you had gone," said the other, continuing his skimmer-cake with some effort.
10135{_}2682{_}"Well, on second thoughts, I felt there were enough without me," said the first confidentially, "and such a night as it is, too.
10135{_}2683{_}Besides, 'tis the business o' the Government to take care of its criminals--not mine."
10135{_}2684{_}"True; so it is.
10135{_}2685{_}And I felt as you did, that there were enough without me."
10135{_}2686{_}"I don't want to break my limbs running over the humps and hollows of this wild country."
10135{_}2687{_}"Nor I neither, between you and me."
10135{_}2688{_}"These shepherd-people are used to it--simple-minded souls, you know, stirred up to anything in a moment.
10135{_}2689{_}They'll have him ready for me before the morning, and no trouble to me at all."
10135{_}2690{_}"They'll have him, and we shall have saved ourselves all labor in the matter."
10135{_}2691{_}"True, true.
10135{_}2692{_}Well, my way is to Casterbridge; and 'tis as much as my legs will do to take me that far.
10135{_}2693{_}Going the same way?"
10135{_}2694{_}"No, I am sorry to say!
10135{_}2695{_}I have to get home over there" (he nodded indefinitely to the right), "and I feel as you do, that it is quite enough for my legs to do before bedtime."
10135{_}2696{_}The other had by this time finished the mead in the mug, after which, shaking hands heartily at the door, and wishing each other well, they went their several ways.
10135{_}2697{_}In the meantime the company of pursuers had reached the end of the hog's-back elevation which dominated this part of the down.
10135{_}2698{_}They had decided on no particular plan of action; and, finding that the man of the baleful trade was no longer in their company, they seemed quite unable to form any such plan now.
10135{_}2699{_}They descended in all directions down the hill, and straightway several of the party fell into the snare set by Nature for all misguided midnight ramblers over this part of the cretaceous formation.
10135{_}2700{_}The "lanchets," or flint slopes, which belted the escarpment at intervals of a dozen yards, took the less cautious ones unawares, and losing their footing on the rubbly steep they slid sharply downward, the lanterns rolling from their hands to the bottom, and there lying on their sides till the horn was scorched through.
10135{_}2701{_}When they had again gathered themselves together, the shepherd, as the man who knew the country best, took the lead, and guided them round these treacherous inclines.
10135{_}2702{_}The lanterns, which seemed rather to dazzle their eyes and warn the fugitive than to assist them in the exploration, were extinguished, due silence was observed; and in this more rational order they plunged into the vale.
10135{_}2703{_}It was a grassy, briery, moist defile, affording some shelter to any person who had sought it; but the party perambulated it in vain, and ascended on the other side.
10135{_}2704{_}Here they wandered apart, and after an interval closed together again to report progress.
10135{_}2705{_}At the second time of closing in they found themselves near a lonely ash, the single tree on this part of the coomb, probably sown there by a passing bird some fifty years before.
10135{_}2706{_}And here, standing a little to one side of the trunk, as motionless as the trunk itself, appeared the man they were in quest of, his outline being well defined against the sky beyond.
10135{_}2707{_}The band noiselessly drew up and faced him.
10135{_}2708{_}"Your money or your life!"
10135{_}2709{_}said the constable sternly to the still figure.
10135{_}2710{_}"No, no," whispered John Pitcher.
10135{_}2711{_}"'Tisn't our side ought to say that.
10135{_}2712{_}That's the doctrine of vagabonds like him, and we be on the side of the law."
10135{_}2713{_}"Well, well," replied the constable, impatiently; "I must say something, mustn't I?
10135{_}2714{_}and if you had all the weight o' this undertaking upon your mind, perhaps you'd say the wrong thing, too!--Prisoner at the bar, surrender, in the name of the Father--the Crown, I mane!"
10135{_}2715{_}The man under the tree seemed now to notice them for the first time, and, giving them no opportunity whatever for exhibiting their courage, he strolled slowly toward them.
10135{_}2716{_}He was, indeed, the little man, the third stranger; but his trepidation had in a great measure gone.
10135{_}2717{_}"Well, travellers," he said, "did I hear you speak to me?"
10135{_}2718{_}"You did; you've got to come and be our prisoner at once!"
10135{_}2719{_}said the constable.
10135{_}2720{_}"We arrest 'ee on the charge of not biding in Casterbridge jail in a decent proper manner to be hung to-morrow morning.
10135{_}2721{_}Neighbors, do your duty, and seize the culpet!"
10135{_}2722{_}On hearing the charge, the man seemed enlightened, and, saying not another word, resigned himself with preternatural civility to the search-party, who, with their staves in their hands, surrounded him on all sides, and marched him back toward the shepherd's cottage.
10135{_}2723{_}It was eleven o'clock by the time they arrived.
10135{_}2724{_}The light shining from the open door, a sound of men's voices within, proclaimed to them as they approached the house that some new events had arisen in their absence.
10135{_}2725{_}On entering they discovered the shepherd's living-room to be invaded by two officers from Casterbridge jail, and a well-known magistrate who lived at the nearest country-seat, intelligence of the escape having become generally circulated.
10135{_}2726{_}"Gentlemen," said the constable, "I have brought back your man--not without risk and danger; but every one must do his duty!
10135{_}2727{_}He is inside this circle of able-bodied persons, who have lent me useful aid, considering their ignorance of Crown work.
10135{_}2728{_}Men, bring forward your prisoner!"
10135{_}2729{_}And the third stranger was led to the light.
10135{_}2730{_}"Who is this?"
10135{_}2731{_}said one of the officials.
10135{_}2732{_}"The man," said the constable.
10135{_}2733{_}"Certainly not," said the turnkey; and the first corroborated his statement.
10135{_}2734{_}"But how can it be otherwise?"
10135{_}2735{_}asked the constable.
10135{_}2736{_}"Or why was he so terrified at sight o' the singing instrument of the law who sat there?"
10135{_}2737{_}Here he related the strange behavior of the third stranger on entering the house during the hangman's song.
10135{_}2738{_}"Can't understand it," said the officer coolly.
10135{_}2739{_}"All I know is that it is not the condemned man.
10135{_}2740{_}He's quite a different character from this one; a gauntish fellow, with dark hair and eyes, rather good-looking, and with a musical bass voice that if you heard it once you'd never mistake as long as you lived."
10135{_}2741{_}"Why, souls--'twas the man in the chimney-corner!"
10135{_}2742{_}"Hey--what?"
10135{_}2743{_}said the magistrate, coming forward after inquiring particulars from the shepherd in the background.
10135{_}2744{_}"Haven't you got the man after all?"
10135{_}2745{_}"Well, sir," said the constable, "he's the man we were in search of, that's true; and yet he's not the man we were in search of.
10135{_}2746{_}For the man we were in search of was not the man we wanted, sir, if you understand my every-day way; for 'twas the man in the chimney-corner!"
10135{_}2747{_}"A pretty kettle of fish altogether!"
10135{_}2748{_}said the magistrate.
10135{_}2749{_}"You had better start for the other man at once."
10135{_}2750{_}The prisoner now spoke for the first time.
10135{_}2751{_}The mention of the man in the chimney-corner seemed to have moved him as nothing else could do.
10135{_}2752{_}"Sir," he said, stepping forward to the magistrate, "take no more trouble about me.
10135{_}2753{_}The time is come when I may as well speak.
10135{_}2754{_}I have done nothing; my crime is that the condemned man is my brother.
10135{_}2755{_}Early this afternoon I left home at Shottsford to tramp it all the way to Casterbridge jail to bid him farewell.
10135{_}2756{_}I was benighted, and called here to rest and ask the way.
10135{_}2757{_}When I opened the door I saw before me the very man, my brother, that I thought to see in the condemned cell at Casterbridge.
10135{_}2758{_}He was in this chimney-corner; and jammed close to him, so that he could not have got out if he had tried, was the executioner who'd come to take his life, singing a song about it and not knowing that it was his victim who was close by, joining in to save appearances.
10135{_}2759{_}My brother looked a glance of agony at me, and I know he meant, 'Don't reveal what you see; my life depends on it.'
10135{_}2760{_}I was so terror-struck that I could hardly stand, and, not knowing what I did, I turned and hurried away."
10135{_}2761{_}The narrator's manner and tone had the stamp of truth, and his story made a great impression on all around.
10135{_}2762{_}"And do you know where your brother is at the present time?"
10135{_}2763{_}asked the magistrate.
10135{_}2764{_}"I do not.
10135{_}2765{_}I have never seen him since I closed this door."
10135{_}2766{_}"I can testify to that, for we've been between ye ever since."
10135{_}2767{_}said the constable.
10135{_}2768{_}"Where does he think to fly to?--what is his occupation?"
10135{_}2769{_}"He's a watch-and-clock-maker, sir."
10135{_}2770{_}"'A said 'a was a wheelwright--a wicked rogue," said the constable.
10135{_}2771{_}"The wheels of clocks and watches he meant, no doubt," said Shepherd Fennel.
10135{_}2772{_}"I thought his hands were palish for's trade."
10135{_}2773{_}"Well, it appears to me that nothing can be gained by retaining this poor man in custody," said the magistrate; "your business lies with the other, unquestionably."
10135{_}2774{_}And so the little man was released off-hand; but he looked nothing the less sad on that account, it being beyond the power of magistrate or constable to raze out the written troubles in his brain, for they concerned another whom he regarded with more solicitude than himself.
10135{_}2775{_}When this was done, and the man had gone his way, the night was found to be so far advanced that it was deemed useless to renew the search before the next morning.
10135{_}2776{_}Next day, accordingly, the quest for the clever sheep-stealer became general and keen, to all appearance at least.
10135{_}2777{_}But the intended punishment was cruelly disproportioned to the transgression, and the sympathy of a great many country-folk in that district was strongly on the side of the fugitive.
10135{_}2778{_}Moreover, his marvellous coolness and daring in hob-and-nobbing with the hangman, under the unprecedented circumstances of the shepherd's party, won their admiration.
10135{_}2779{_}So that it may be questioned if all those who ostensibly made themselves so busy in exploring woods and fields and lanes were quite so thorough when it came to the private examination of their own lofts and outhouses.
10135{_}2780{_}Stories were afloat of a mysterious figure being occasionally seen in some old overgrown trackway or other, remote from turnpike roads; but when a search was instituted in any of these suspected quarters nobody was found.
10135{_}2781{_}Thus the days and weeks passed without tidings.
10135{_}2782{_}In brief, the bass-voiced man of the chimney-corner was never recaptured.
10135{_}2783{_}Some said that he went across the sea, others that he did not, but buried himself in the depths of a populous city.
10135{_}2784{_}At any rate, the gentleman in cinder-gray never did his morning's work at Casterbridge, nor met anywhere at all, for business purposes, the genial comrade with whom he had passed an hour of relaxation in the lonely house on the coomb.
10135{_}2785{_}The grass has long been green on the graves of Shepherd Fennel and his frugal wife; the guests who made up the christening party have mainly followed their entertainers to the tomb; the baby in whose honor they all had met is a matron in the sere and yellow leaf.
10135{_}2786{_}But the arrival of the three strangers at the shepherd's that night, and the details connected therewith, is a story as well-known as ever in the country about Higher Crowstairs.
10135{_}2787{_}_March_, 1883.
10135{_}2788{_}JULIA BRIDE[1]  [Footnote 1: 1909.]
10135{_}2789{_}_Henry James_ (1843)   I  She had walked with her friend to the top of the wide steps of the Museum, those that descended from the galleries of painting, and then, after the young man had left her, smiling, looking back, waving all gayly and expressively his hat and stick, had watched him, smiling too, but with a different intensity--had kept him in sight till he passed out of the great door.
10135{_}2790{_}She might have been waiting to see if he would turn there for a last demonstration; which was exactly what he did, renewing his cordial gesture and with his look of glad devotion, the radiance of his young face, reaching her across the great space, as she felt, in undiminished truth.
10135{_}2791{_}Yes, so she could feel, and she remained a minute even after he was gone; she gazed at the empty air as if he had filled it still, asking herself what more she wanted and what, if it didn't signify glad devotion, his whole air could have represented.
10135{_}2792{_}She was at present so anxious that she could wonder if he stepped and smiled like that for mere relief at separation; yet if he desired in that degree to break the spell and escape the danger why did he keep coming back to her, and why, for that matter, had she felt safe a moment before in letting him go?
10135{_}2793{_}She felt safe, felt almost reckless--that was the proof--so long as he was with her; but the chill came as soon as he had gone, when she took the measure, instantly, of all she yet missed.
10135{_}2794{_}She might now have been taking it afresh, by the testimony of her charming clouded eyes and of the rigor that had already replaced her beautiful play of expression.
10135{_}2795{_}Her radiance, for the minute, had "carried" as far as his, travelling on the light wings of her brilliant prettiness--he, on his side, not being facially handsome, but only sensitive, clean and eager.
10135{_}2796{_}Then, with its extinction, the sustaining wings dropped and hung.
10135{_}2797{_}She wheeled about, however, full of a purpose; she passed back through the pictured rooms, for it pleased her, this idea of a talk with Mr. Pitman--as much, that is, as anything could please a young person so troubled.
10135{_}2798{_}It happened indeed that when she saw him rise at sight of her from the settee where he had told her five minutes before that she would find him, it was just with her nervousness that his presence seemed, as through an odd suggestion of help, to connect itself.
10135{_}2799{_}Nothing truly would be quite so odd for her case as aid proceeding from Mr. Pitman; unless perhaps the oddity would be even greater for himself--the oddity of her having taken into her head an appeal to him.
10135{_}2800{_}She had had to feel alone with a vengeance--inwardly alone and miserably alarmed--to be ready to "meet," that way, at the first sign from him, the successor to her dim father in her dim father's lifetime, the second of her mother's two divorced husbands.
10135{_}2801{_}It made a queer relation for her; a relation that struck her at this moment as less edifying, less natural and graceful than it would have been even for her remarkable mother--and still in spite of this parent's third marriage, her union with Mr. Connery, from whom she was informally separated.
10135{_}2802{_}It was at the back of Julia's head as she approached Mr. Pitman, or it was at least somewhere deep within her soul, that if this last of Mrs. Connery's withdrawals from the matrimonial yoke had received the sanction of the court (Julia had always heard, from far back, so much about the "Court") she herself, as after a fashion, in that event, a party to it, would not have had the cheek to make up--which was how she inwardly phrased what she was doing--to the long, lean, loose, slightly cadaverous gentleman who was a memory, for her, of the period from her twelfth to her seventeenth year.
10135{_}2803{_}She had got on with him, perversely, much better than her mother had, and the bulging misfit of his duck waistcoat, with his trick of swinging his eye-glass, at the end of an extraordinarily long string, far over the scene, came back to her as positive features of the image of her remoter youth.
10135{_}2804{_}Her present age--for her later time had seen so many things happen--gave her a perspective.
10135{_}2805{_}Fifty things came up as she stood there before him, some of them floating in from the past, others hovering with freshness: how she used to dodge the rotary movement made by his pince-nez while he always awkwardly, and kindly, and often funnily, talked--it had once hit her rather badly in the eye; how she used to pull down and straighten his waistcoat, making it set a little better, a thing of a sort her mother never did; how friendly and familiar she must have been with him for that, or else a forward little minx; how she felt almost capable of doing it again now, just to sound the right note, and how sure she was of the way he would take it if she did; how much nicer he had clearly been, all the while, poor dear man, than his wife and the court had made it possible for him publicly to appear; how much younger, too, he now looked, in spite of his rather melancholy, his mildly jaundiced, humorously determined sallowness and his careless assumption, everywhere, from his forehead to his exposed and relaxed blue socks, almost sky-blue, as in past days, of creases and folds and furrows that would have been perhaps tragic if they hadn't seemed rather to show, like his whimsical black eyebrows, the vague, interrogative arch.
10135{_}2806{_}Of course he wasn't wretched if he wasn't more sure of his wretchedness than that!
10135{_}2807{_}Julia Bride would have been sure--had she been through what she supposed _he_ had!
10135{_}2808{_}With his thick, loose black hair, in any case, untouched by a thread of gray, and his kept gift of a certain big-boyish awkwardness--that of his taking their encounter, for instance, so amusedly, so crudely, though, as she was not unaware, so eagerly too--he could by no means have been so little his wife's junior as it had been that lady's habit, after the divorce, to represent him.
